THE RED PAGE.
The Will and the Flesh.
CHRISTIAN Science, Metaphysical Heal-
311°l ■; l aitl ' Healing, and a few other
such ideas, more or less tainted with
quackery and chicanery, have been pro-
mulgated during the last 100 years as new dis-
coveries with much industry and fervour of advo-
cacy. All depend on the one thing, essentially :
all have some truth essentially; the novelty in
each case is m the quackery part of the nostrum,
lhat in man the mind is stronger than the body ;
that mental effort deliberately put forth, or un-
consciously exercised, can make as nothing phy-
sical pain ; that agony is subjective rather than
objective is a truth which phenomena, recorded in
every age but not always perfectly understood,
have conclusively established. That, therefore,
following Mrs. Eedy’s humbug, or the Rev.
pome body or other’s revelation, pain may be
banished from human life, is the “quack” con-
elusion from a sound premise.
The subjectivity of pain may be proved in a
thousand ways. The soldier in the heat of battle,
tne athlete in the excitement of a game, incur
serious wounds, and not only feel for the time no
pain, but are actually unaware of hurt. The mind
» t?o much occupied to allow pain to be felt, and
wi 1 cons ents to it there can be no pain.
W hen that, m a measure accidental and uncon-
trolled pre-occupation can, by discipline of the
mind, be brought in any one person under the
direct command of the will, pain exists no more so
tar as he or she is concerned.
•dm"? / can be traced many stories of
Biblical and Profane history which are ofterbre-
jected by some critics as incredible miracles or
as impudent fables. The youths who walked un-
harmed in a fiery furnace, the healings of the
sick and the palsied, of the blind and the deaf and
the dumb, the story of the Roman soldier who,
ii! re a^ death, held his hand to burn in
the flame of a torch, are all possible—even the
first one (though it is to an extreme extent im-
probable)—when the true relations of mind and
matter are understood. With that understanding,
mo, comes the possibility of belief in the calmness
of martyrs like St. Lawrence, who, being killed
by grilling, calmly observed to his tormentors
that “he was sufficiently done on one side and
they might now turn him. In fact, the possi-
bilities of fanaticism or other mental exaltation in
making people insensible to physical pain are just
as commonly recognised now as the power of
mental disturbance to cause intense agony with-
out any actual physical lesion.
The seat of pain has been established by modern
science to be m the mind instead of in the body.
A sufficiently strong and well-balanced mind can,
theoretically, defy all pain, just as can the rapt
mind of the fanatic. What it is necessary to keep
ffi v^ew ’ fr° m the outset, is, however, that
that strength of mind does not necessarily
depend on any fanaticism, and can exist—
can, in fact, only exist as a stable and
reliable condition—independent of fanaticism.
Religious or other hysteria may give the mental
pre-occupation which lifts the body above pain for
a while, as alcohol may give a passing Courage
and anger a passing strength. But it is a sign o ’
mental weakness rather than of mental strength
and possibly does greater physical and menta
damage than any degree of pain. A healthy man
does not need to turn to it for relief from pain, anj
more than to take morphia to secure sleep. When
the value of mental discipline is understood—
when people learn to train their wills as well as
their bodies, and begin to know how much they
can do, or refuse to do, by mere will-force
the conquest of pain will have begun.
In “Limitless Man” Helen Wilmans puts
forward with the eagerness of a discoverer this
old truth, and manages to make her argument very
clear and convincing, in spite of a good deal of
“jargon.” It is a fault of the Anglo-Saxon
character that it is ever inclined to preach and to
put forward ideas as religions. Even agnosticism
in the hands of an earnest Anglo-Saxon becomes
something which calls for a pulpit and a collection
plate. Putting - aside Helen Wilman’s preaching,
she has some most astounding examples to adduce
of the conquest of the mind over the body. The
mcredible seems to be reached bv “the fiery
ordeal of Fiji ” (men walking unscathed over
white-hot stones) which is said to be carried through
as a rite in Fiji, and parts of Japan and India also.
But there is nothing, even in it, that can be posi-
tively set down as impossible in the light of facts
which are above all question. A hundred and
one ills which bid defiance to medicine have proved
amenable to religious hysteria or mental discipline;
the power of the mind over the body has been fur-
ther proved in the existence of pain in limbs
amputated years before. Why should not the
mind, proved strong enough to resist pain, also, to
an extent, be able to prevent lesions of the flesh ?
The Gentle Art of Criticism .
BATtTKDAY REVIEW” recently, in an
article, “Poets to the Trade,” made a
sharp attack on Stephen Phillips and his
work. It was a tirade of abuse with a
thin streak of criticism running through. A
few days later the Quarterly, appeared with a re-
view of Phillips’ work written in a similar strain.
This “ The .Slasher ’’ pointed out as a striking con-
firmation of its own particular views, and hinted
that Phillips was now in a .fair way to being
dragged off his pedestal. At this stage Sidney
Colvin showed, by means of parallel columns, that
the two articles were by the same writer, whole
passages in the one being identical with passages
in the other. A busy-bee happened to have more
stings than one. The laugh was made whole-
hearty by the lameness of the subsequent editorial
retort, which, after prefacing that an editor alone
was responsible for what appeared in his columns,
fumed and shrieked at Colvin for his “imper-
tinence ” in going behind an editor’s authority—
i.e., impeaching the prestige of the “respectable”
critical press.
The Saturday Review is the perfect expression
of that domineering, academic spirit which passes
current for criticism in England to-day, and which
is so destructive of all that is spontaneous and
original in art. Its authority arises from the fact
that it caters for those of the sheepish, parsonic
type whose aim in life is mainly to abide by what
they are told. Such an atmosphere is a deadly
enemy to art but a tender nurse to philistinism.
It is a legitimate result of the public-school cult,
the traditions of which permeate every department
of English public life. England is very jealous of
her public-school tradition ; and well she might
be, for it is the one thing that gives any kind of
individuality to her public life. In accordance
with the fashion set by Walters, of The Times , it
suits that section of England’s plutocracy which
runs her newspapers to staff their journals with
Oxford and Cambridge men of the deepest
dye, whose ability is conspicuous, but who
carry into their work the worst and most
blighting kind of class prejudice. Their
training, enlarging as it is in some respects, has a
marked tendency to narrow the outlook. There
is something occult in their unutterable supe-
riority, which inexorably bars emulation and has
the same disconcerting effect as the “hawty”
British stare has on the affable outsider. Any-
thing which does not accord precisely with the
pattern sanctioned by their eclecticism is beyond
the pale of reasonable consideration. Their
slender equipment of mother wit is so overladen
with a vast range of acquirements and clogged
with tradition that they resent spontaneity and
originality in others. Striking merit not of the
approved kind only calls forth blunderbuss
criticism, and, if particularly prominent, a kind of
donkey wit.
The truth is that there is in England no robust
public opinion in the field of art and letters. And,
if the present state of affairs were to continue, the
establishment of an English Academy would be a
calamity, for where there is no public opinion
there is no logical necessity for an institution
whose chief function should be the intelligent ex-
pression of educated opinion. The establishment
of such an institution would, as things stand, be
the death warrant of merit. Dominated by the
political or social gang of the moment it would be
for the choking-off of all merit not of the right sort
®*jd the encouragement of a glorified mediocrity.
Ihe enervated state of public opinion in England
has a striking exemplification in the fact that
Kipling has never challenged real criticism, never
even provoked any kind of discussion beyond a
hoarse roar of acclamation. The business of the
modern critic is to know where sits the wind—the
bawdy wind that kisses every cheek it meets.
H.
Shelley as a Poet of the People.
Thus Gr. C. Whitney :
Shelley (whose anniversary date was Aug. 4th)
is essentially an idealist, but so far from being a
mere secluded lover of the beautiful and a prophet
of certain vague ideals, he is first and last a demo-
cratic poet. Mr. G. Saintsbury, in his “ History
of English Literature,” makes the strange state-
ment that chance only, caused Shelley to espouse
the cause of democracy, and that he might equally
well have been a high Tory. But, so far from
being the expression of a mere mood of the
moment, Shelley’s democracy coloured almost all
he wrote, and in many places is a direct protest
against the conservative reaction which followed
the French Revolution. Where is the case for the
peopie more strongly put than in these stanzas of
the ‘ Song to the Men of England ” ;
Men of England, wherefore plough
For the lords who lay ye low ?
Wherefore weave with toil and care
The rich robes your tyrants wear ?
Wherefore feed and clothe and save,
From the cradle to the grave,
Those ungrateful drones who would
Drain your sweat—nay, drink your blood' 1
Wherefore, Bees of England, forge
Many a weapon, chain, and scourge,
That these stingless drones may spoil
The forced produce of your toil ?
The seed ye sow another reaps ;
The wealth ye find another keeps ;
The robes ye weave another weats ;
The arms 3e forge another bears.
Sow seed —but let no tyrant reap ;
Find wealth—but let no impostor heap •
Weave robes—let not the idle wear ; ’
Forge arms, in your defence to bear.
More remarkable still is the “Masque of
Anarchy ' Shelley consistently condemned the
evils of the England of his day, and persistently
spurred on the oppressed masses to revolt.
“ Queen Mab ” and “ The Revolt of Islam ” are
democratic to the core. And the whole meaning
ot Hellas is expressed in the magnificent
stanzas which form the concluding chorus I
quote one:—
Another Athens shall arise,
And to remoter time
Bequeath, like sunset to the skies,
The splendour of its prime ;
And leave, if naught so bright may live,
All earth can take or heaven can give.
Has ever the hope of the future been better sung’
Democracy may well be proud of the lonely
lshmael who worked, suffered and waited for the
dawn. Wrong still sits in high places, but
ilemocracy has m its thinkers, workers, martyrs
prophets of the past, a host who have forged the
weapons it may wield, pointed to it the path it
iu ay S? ne of them was more notable
than Shelley. The “Masque of Anarchy” gives
a fine motto to Democracy :
Rise like lions after slumber
In unvanquishable number—
Shake your chains to earth like dew
Which in sleep hath fallen on you—
¥e are many—they are few.
“Frank P.”: Re Democracy. If “A.M.” (B
19/7/’O2) is pleased to look upon our present
regime as a Democracy, then Aristophanes was
aptly quoted. But we have no Democracy— not
even an ignorant one, and “A.M.” surely must
have been trying to pull our aggregate leg when
penning that ominous warning of disaster in her
concluding 1 lines. An intelligent Democracy is not
yet born, and will not be for a long time The
Age is not even mature for its conception.
The refusal of the Licenser of Plays to pass
Maeterlinck’s “ Monna Vanna ” recalls the occa-
sion of the passing of the Licensing Act by Robert
Walpole m 1738. The manager of a London
theatre brought him a farce called “The Golden
Kump, either to ask his advice or extort money
for its non-performance. Walpole detained the
manuscript, and having made a number of pro-
fane, seditious, and blasphemous extracts, read
them to the House, and without further trouble
the Bill was passed. At various times the
“(Edipus Rex” of Sophocles, “The Cenci” of
Shelley (one of the greatest plays in the English
language since Shakespeare), and the “Ghosts”
of Ibsen (one of the greatest of modern prose-
dramas), and now Maeterlinck’s best and greatest
work, “ Monna Vanna,” have been condemned.
H. : Wemyss Reid’s Biog. of William Black is
good. Black began as a journalist on the Glasgow
Citizen. He figured in an episode almost exactly
like that of Pendennis and the Eotheringay. This
disgusted him with Glasgow, and he went to
London. The affair obviously suggested his novel
of Macleod of Dare,” but both the actress and
her admirer are very different from the models.
When Black thought he had developed a com-
plete mastery of his trade his readers fell away.
Same with novelists in general. How few are so
lucky as Cervantes, finding the gold-reef at the
end. A quartette of novels which can be named
by his admirers, destroyed Black’s nerves. Trol-
lope never had any. He could have gone on
carpentering novels till now.

Leon Brodzky on the drama :
The classical method, of which the “ CEdipus Rex ” is
the supreme model, did not consist merely of a rigid
adherence to what are called the “unities.” The
“ unities ” arise from the method, and do not constitute
the method. If it is recognised that drama does not con-
sist in telling a story by a series of scenes, but in select-
ing the essential situations, as Sophocles and other great
dramatists did, less nonsense would be talked about the
“ unities,” which obviously must hamper the writer of
plays, which are more story than drama. Plot is not
story or subject-matter, but that part of the story which
embodies the central idea. In the history, say, of
CEdipus there are many situations, one of which is the
discovery by the King that he himself is the God-
accursed criminal. And it is only that one situation
worked out with necessary amplification that Sophocles
deals with. A string of events such as one finds in nearly
every play is not drama. Happenings that one can see
do not constitute drama. The actual drama lies between
the incidents or happenings of a play. Ibsen is like
Sophocles in his methods. In “Ghosts” the scene
throughout the play is the one room, and the action is
finished within 12 hours ; but that is a very superficial
way of. explaining the resemblance. The classical form,
adopted by Ibsen, lies really in the selection of
the one culminating situation out of the story stretch-
ing back to the years before the birth of Oswald.
One of the “ story-telling dramatists ” would, no doubt,
have introduced us to Captain Alving, and worked his
way from Genesis to Revelations ; whereas dramatic art,
like all other art, is only Revelations. Max Nordau, in
“ Degeneration,” notes the peculiarity of Ibsen’s method,
but fails to appreciate its value. He says Ibsen writes a
play as if he were preparing an explosion. The explosives
are all properly arranged and the wires connected. Then,
when it is time to start, the electric current is switched
on, and you have your play. Nordau is, of course,
prejudiced against Ibsen, and cannot see the beauty of
the method, leaving aside all questions of philosophy.*
The branch of art that tells stories is the epical and the
narrative. A drama should be like a picture. It should
not tell a story. Yet, there must be a story from which
to select the situation that will reveal the dramatic idea.
As much of the story as is necessary to make the situation
clear will have to be told, or rather indicated. It is here
that the supreme artist will show his genius. In Greek
drama, the inferior artist Euripides had to resort to the
device of a long prologue to tell the story. In the “ CEdipus
Rex,” what is called the prologue is really the opening
scene. Still, it would be a great mistake to think that
everything in the way of story and incident should be
rigorously excluded. The dramatist should only remember
that, in writing a play, his business is not to tell a story.
A sculptor seizes upon that one moment when the body
is the incarnation of an idea, and reproduces it in marble.
A painter snatches that one moment from all the moments
which shows him a face or a landscape as he wants it for
the expression of his thought or emotion. And so the
dramatist must select from all the multitudinous happen-
ings of life that one “ time in the affairs of man ” which
to his imagination holds “ infinity in the palm of the
hand.”
Some Correspondents—
Wealsman : Apropos “ What is Truth ” (B. 31/5/’O2), it
would be easy to fill a volume with instances of “ honest
mistake” even among the trained memories of pressmen.
I know a Bulletin contributor of many years standing
whose “ forgettery” is in such splendid order that he
often mistakes what he reads for something he has
written. When he reads his Bulletin he marks his own
contributions for charging up. Should the charging up
be delayed for two weeks he is sure to find several pars
whose features seem so familiar that he takes them for his
own and wonders how he came to miss them in the first
prospecting. Experience (and probably Bulletin account-
ant) has taught him that it is safest to rely on the first
search, so he seldom claims for his later discoveries.
Explanation is that the matter isalwavs such as he might
easily have written ; his mind retains broad facts but
distorts or loses grip of details. One distorted detail is,
•that something he once knew he never wrote becomes
something he honestly thinks he did write.
[On other hand writer of “ What is Truth ” can recgnise
at five years interval the smallest par he has written ; but
can’t keep in mind any dates, even the date of his own
birth-year or birthday. Memories are curious things ; and
the law (which is, in this respect, an ass) treats them as
though they were fixed quantities in a mathematical
equation.]
J.P. : Apropos of par. (24/5/’O2) re literary agents and
the sale of Australian work in London. Ambrose Pratt
never said that a literary agent was a necessity. Provided
that they can rob the author and get his productions for
next to nothing, English publications.are only too anxious
to secure Australian work. It is not difficult to place
good, original work in London, but to get decently paid
for it is another matter. I wrote a few yarns for one of
Newnes’monthly magazines, some of which were accepted.
Editor wrote saying he would always be glad to
consider fiction. Submitted a 3500 word tale which was
advertised in two numbers of the magazine preceding
its appearance, and printed with great flourish and a per-
sonal par. concerning the author. Received three guineas
for it, and had to pay three shillings exchange. There
are men writing for that magazine who don’t accept less
than three guineas per 1000 words ; but if an Australian
submits work (often of greater value on account of its
originality) direct, he has to be content with 17s. 6d. per
1000. But an agent always secures the market value for
the work he sells, and, what is also of great importance,
he studies the interests of his clients, and gives valuable
advice. There are, with his aid, no wearisome delays,
and you are kept well advised as regards what is being
done with your work. An agent may be no more neces-
sary for salvation than a parson, but he can do much
toward keeping Australian writers out of the old men’s
home, and assuredly protects them from being robbed by
the boss thieves of this planet—London publishers.

BRIGHT’S DISEASE
For the full understanding of the curative action
of Warner’s Safe Cure, it is necessary to bear in
mind the processes of nature by which human life
is maintained. Reduced to their essential prin-
ciples, these processes are simply the wasting and
renewal of the tissues of the body.
It is absolutely necessary to life that the body
should waste away, and that its substance should
be renewed by food, water, and fresh air. Night
and day this proeess of waste and replaceman goes
on unceasingly. Every time a man draws breath
his body gains in weight by the oxygen, etc., taken
from the air, and every time he breathes out it
loses weight by the carbonic acid and moisture
given forth.
Insensibly, too, the body is losing weight all the
time. You can prove this by placing your hand
on a cold piece of polished glass, when you will
see that moisture given forth by the skin has con-
densed. Were the body to be entirely covered
with a coat of waterproof varnish, death would
quickly result.
Apart from the skin and lungs, the organs em-
ployed in this process of waste are the kidneys and
, liver, and the maintenance of those organs in
thorough working order is the grand secret of good
health.
The Kidneys. —The function which the kidneys
perform is to filter the blood and otherwise
separate from it the surplus water and the various
impurities which it contains. These impurities
are urea, uric acid, and mineral matter, which,
with water, constitute the urine.
When the kidneys have done their work, the
purified blood is delivered to the veins, and is
returned to the heart, having in its course
traversed the body and become laden with a fresh
supply of waste matter. The hpart again delivers
the blood to the kidneys, which once more filter
it and extract the impurities. This process is
continuous.
The Liver.— I The part the liver plays in the
process of waste is chiefly the manufacturing from
the blood the liquid called “Bile,” which is
delivered by the liver into the intestines, and acts
as a natural cathartic.
The liver and kidneys are very sensitive organs.
Sudden chills, fever, alcoholic intemperance, and
tropical heat are all liable to congest the liver and
reduce it to an abnormal condition. The kidneys
are exceedingly susoeptible to inflammation. An
ordinary feverish cold, which doses the pores and
hinders the skin in performing its share in the
removal of the waste products of the body, throws
a burden of extra work upon the kidneys which
they may be unable to perform. Influenza, or
any kind of fever, taxes the kidneys unduly, and
may cause them to break down under the strain.
Any inflammation of the kidneys is very apt to
affeot the liver, and vice versa.
Should the liver or kidneys, or both, become
unable to fulfil their purpose thoroughly, then the
blood becomes laden with urinary and biliary
poisons, which are oarried to every part of the
body.
Sometimes these poisons become deposited in
the joints or tissues, and cause Rheumatism,
Lumbago, Sciatica, or Gout. In other cases the
blood, being contaminated, is in an unfit condition
to absorb nutriment, which causes Anaemia,
Neuralgia, digestive troubles, and Debility. In
other cases the supply of bile to the intestines
being inadequate, Constipation, with its attendant
evils result; in others, Biliousness. In others
again the impurity of the blood develops boils and,
skin eruptions. Sometimes the uric acid is
deposited in the kidneys and produces Stone,
Gravel, and urinary and bladder troubles.
The reason, then, that Warner’s Safe Cure cures
so many apparently diverse disorders is that they
all proceed from one cause, namely—the inability
of the kidneys or liver to do their work properly,
and so rid the blood of these poisons in the
manner provided by nature.
Warner’s Safe Cure has complete mastery over
functional and chronic liver and kidney derange-
ments, and by restoring those vital organs to
health and activity, does away with the cause of
these several complaints. When the kidneys and
liver are efficiently carrying out the wonderful
scheme of work allotted to them by nature, none
of the disorders here enumerated can exist.
A pamphlet containing fuller particulars
relating to Warner’s Safe Cure and the diseases it
cures will be sent post free to any address on
application to H. H. Warner and Co., Limited,
Australasian Branch, 52.3 to 532 Little Lonsd.de
Street, Melbourne, Victoria.

The Case ot
Mrs. E. WILLIAMS
(by a SPECIAL REPORTER.)!
Our special recently saw Mrs. Emily
Williams, of No. 4 Yurong-sireet, East
Sydney, with reference to a matter of con-
siderable importance to the public. On
Mrs. Williams announcing that she had a
few minutes to spare, the following con-
versation took place :
Reporter: “ I desire to learn the exact
particulars about the troubles you ex-
perienced some years ago.”
Mrs Williams : “ That is about the time
that I was so ill. Well, I hardly know
what to say to you, because my sufferings
extended over three years, and I could not
explain them in a few words.”
Reporter: “I shall be all the more pleased
if you will go into detail.”
. Mrs - Williams : “ Very well, then, I will
P v ® tl , le history of my case from start
to finish. When my health began to fail I
noticed myself getting very languid and
drowsy, with a feeling as soon as I got up
m the mornings that I wanted to go back to
bed again, and not caring to do any work at
all. I thought that perhaps I was run
down through overwork, and that all I
required was a good rest, but after takina
matters very quietly for a while I found
that I was no better. The weariness was
still upon me, and my spirits were be-
coming more depressed every day. I did
not want to go out shopping or picnicing,
neither had I any desire for any amuse-
ment There was no vitality in my body
to enable me to do anything, and many a
time I felt as if I could simply lie down
and die, as every pleasure in life went away
from me. Even the privilege of enjoying
my food was denied me, for my mouth was
thick with a bitter substance, which was
especially noticeable on my tongue and
everything had a sickly taste. Besides I
had no appetite worth mentioning, a couple
of mouthfuls always proving sufficient to
turn me away from the table in disgust, yet
in spite of the small quantity I had eaten
the pains I felt were something cruel
bpasms of wind often bent me double, and
l felt as if I was smothering through
a heavy feelmg in my chest. When the
food had been in my stomach a little while
it became sour, for I could taste it in the
wind which rose up and caused me to belch
and soon afterwards I started to retch and
retch till everything was vomited back
again.
Reporter : “ Those ailments were the real
secret of your weakness.”
Mrs. Williams : “ Beyond all doubt; and
1 did get miserably weak and thin, I can
tell you. From nine stoue two pounds I
fell away to seven stone, so you may believe
that I was nothing but a living skeleton
and my skin was quiie shrunken and nearly
yellow. Such a drawn and haggard face I
had, too, clearly showing that I passed
many nights in sleeplessness and agony,
with my head aching incessantly till I
was nearly driven out of my mind. It
would not be correct to say that I never
slept, as I sometimes slumbered for three
or four hours right off; but what sort of
deep was it ? It was like an ucffy night-
mare right through, for the dreams I had
actually frightened me when I thought of
them in my waking moments. How
wretched it was to be everlastingly think-
ing of some undefinable misery that was in
store for me—that I would never be well
and happy again. Why, my feebleness had
so far advanced that if I walked a few yards
I had to stand and rest till I felt able to go on
a little further, and frequently giddiness
overtook me so that I staggered as I walked
Often and often my sight was almost gone,
as there was nothing before me but a black
mist which hung before my eyes, and when
such experiences were made all the more
terrifying by my heart palpitating and pain-
ing roe I was afraid to walk any further
\or fear that I might drop down dead. The
palpitations were so violent that I could
auily get my breath, and my gasping was
a waj's accompanied by excessive trembling
tor my nervous system was broken down
completely. Right under my shouldei
blades there was a sharp pain which hurt
me greatly, and about my loins there was a
never ceasing ache. Death would have
been welcome so that my privations could
have ended, but it was not to be that I
should die. 5 ’
Reporter: “You say you were lingering
tor three years but surely you got some
relief occasionally ? ”
Mrs Williams : ‘ 1 The only relief I got wag
when I bought my first bottle of Clements
ionic, for when I had finished it I imagined
1 felt somewhat comforted. It was a strange
sensation which came over me, and one
which left me in a state of doubt. I felt
slightly easier, but not sufficiently so to
make it clear that Clements Tonic would
save me, and I hesitated about getting any
more; but when I remembered that the
advice and medicine given to me by four
doctors were ineffective, and that I had
taken numberless other medicines and
pills without my condition being improved,
I thought the only sensible thing I could do
was to get some more Clements Tonic. Be-
sides, I had read in the papers about a lady,
who had been an invalid like myself, having
been cured by Clements Tonic, and I
thought if it would cure her it would cure-
me. And that is precisely how I was trans-
formed from a weak and dying mortal to a
strong and happy woman. It did not take
Clements Tonic long to arouse in me a feel-
ing of hunger, although I did not feel too
easy after my meals for several days, but
then the food commenced to digest, and my
tortures began to disappear. My chest felt
lighter, and the smothering sensation passed
away ; my stomach did not pain me with
spasms of wind, and my mouth was tho-
roughly cleansed. There was also some-
thing missing that had caused me a
great amount of anxiety.”
Reporter : “ What was that ? ”
Mrs. Williams: “The palpitation and
pains in my heart; and I am sure it is not
necessary for me to say how devoutly thank-
ful I was to Clements Tonic for having done
me so much good already. It gave me such
sound sleep, too; for, with the removal of
my headaches, there was very little else to
keep me awake, as the pains in my shoulders
had gone, and the aches which resulted
from weakness were driven out by the
strength which Clements Tonic infused into
my system. Such things as retching and
vomiting were soon remedied by Clements
Tonic, and my nervous system was bene-
fited by each dose of that medicine. The
puiifying qualities of Clements Tonic were
demonstrated by the clearness of my com-
plexion, and its ability to mitigate pain was
really marvellous. All I have to say is, that
I owe my life to Clements Tonic; and, as
there are many sufferers who do not know
how to cure themselves, you can publish
this account of my recovery in any way ii
pleases you.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, Emily Williams, of 4 Yurong-street, East
Sydney, in the State of New South Wales,
Commonwealth of Australia, do solemnly and
sincerely declare that I have carefully read
the annexed document, consisting of four folios,
and consecutively numbered from one to four, and
that it contains and is a true and faithful account of
my illness and cure by Clements Tonic ; and also con-
tains my full permission to publish in any way my
statements which I give voluntarily, without
receiving any payment; and I make this solemn de-
claration conscientiously believing the same to be
true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Subscribed and declared at East Sydney thi«
EDWARD W. BUTLER, J.p.

The Problem
of Health
Information that often leads to a
quick solution of it
When the health of your child becomes a
problem that seriously presents itself to you,
we can suggest the solution revealed in the
following letter: —
“ 187, Wellgate, Rotherham, England,
“ November 13th, 1900.
« Dear Sirs,—When our baby boy was six
months old he had consumption of the
bowels and appeared to be wasting away,
and became very much emaciated. He was
under treatment for some time, but did not
improve, and we were told that we should
not be able to rear him. A friend brought
us a paper containing one of your advertise-
ments, and asked us to try Scott’s Emulsion,
as the letter revealed how a similar case to
our boy’s had been benefited by your pre-
paration. ~We obtained a bottle and gavo
ARTHUR HARRISON.
(From a photograph.)
him the emulsion according to directions.
Before three days had passed he was a
different child altogether, and continuing
the treatment, he became a strong healthy
boy. In January of the present year he
contracted whooping cough, and had it very
badly. We resorted to his old medicine—•
Scott’s Emulsion —and after he had taken
three bottles he was completely cured. We
ook upon Scott’s Emulsion as a standard
remedy, and if any of the children are not
well, we immediately employ it, and it
never fails to do them good. We have not
the least doubt that Scott’s Emulsion saved
oir boy’s life.
“ (Signed) Arthur Harrison.”
Scott’s Emulsion is a standard household
remedy on account of its wide range of
usefulness. It is effectual in coughs, colds,
weak lungs, bronchitis, consumption, and
all ailments of throat and lungs, and by
enriching the blood it overcomes ansemio
and wasting tendencies. Children thrive
on Scott’s Emulsion when all other forms
of treatment have been unsuccessful. This
remedy seems just what children need to
give them strong digestion and keen appe-
tite, and to bring flesh and strength to the
entire system.
There is only one Scott’s Emulsion, which
is distinguished by our trade mark of a man
holding a big fish over his shoulder. The
merits of Scott’s Emulsion are not exagger-
ated in any degree. The letters written by
people who have used it show clearly what
may be expected from its use.
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpence to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.,
483 Kent-street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

“ON OUR SELECTION.”
BY
“Steele Rudd.’
Bards of the Backblocks.
The Peace of Yam.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I weary of these city woes,
The dust, the traffic, and the noise,
And hunger for the sweet repose
The township knew when we were hoys.
You mind the drowsy summer days,
The clean sheep drifting from the tolls,
The line of bush, the splendid blaze
Of silver on the quarry-holes ;
The straggling huts that faced the pound,
The stream of furze, its golden shade,
The slow, long, sleepy, breathing sound
The engine at “The Brothers” made.
But here eternal rush and clang
Assail us, and we chafe in touch
With men who are not worth a hang,
And things that do not matter much.
Ah, how I long from town to steal,
To leave its roar and endless strain,
And through my loose-flung body feel
The world’s pulse beating once again !
You mind how Cody’s teams were stuck
With every breaking of the drought,
How ’mid the foam and flying muck
The oaths like locusts buzzed about.
Whip-handles drummed on barrels tense,
And drivers raved at horse and mare.
That man may talk of eloquence,
Who’s heard the honest teamster swear !
’Twas fine when Harvey broke his pledge,
And razed the fixtures at The Star,
And stood to argue and allege
Upon the wreck of Paddy’s bar.
Then desolate he’d stray to brawl
With posts and trees the livelong night,
And stone the roofs of Yam, and call
The sleeping township out to fight.
Alas ! the whirr of rushing trains
Is on my nerves. The neighbors try
Their mandolins, and from the drains
Foul language rises to the sky.
You mind those warm, still evenings when
The scent of bush-fires drifted in,
And Gfleeson’s Kate and Mrs. Been
Each cursed the other, kith and kin.
They drained their cups like sisters dear,
Then swapped abuse till break of day,
And people flocked from far and near
As city-dwellers to the play.
The swaggies came from West and East;
Their morals did us little hurt,
Though Yam had always one at least,
Pursuing griffins in his shirt !
Upon the ironstone road the stock,
With slouching riders, loitered through.
Grim stockmen’s oaths the babes would mock—
The only baby-talk they knew.
Sweet there the grief o’er days that die,
The soft, grey light that chastens all,
The sweep of birds along the sky
The leader’s solitary call.
The lorries clatter here, aloof
The church bells wrangle, and the glad,
Pert sparrows battle on the roof—
Their twitter almost drives me mad !
I weary of the city’s ways,
Its vulgar strife and hateful sham,
And yearn for bushland nights and days—
The bland, idyllic peace of Yam.
EDWARD DYSON.
“Just a Little Bit.”
Oh ! love her just a little bit, but love her all the
time—
(A droughty throat, my drover mate, coats o’er
the heart with rime) —
And don’t forget her on the roads, when camping
“ tinklers” chime!
Oh ! pet her just a little bit to cheer her on her
way—
A softened word, or kindly deed, to treasure day
by day;
’Twill lighten lonely hours, old chap, when you
are miles away !
Remember, just a little bit, the joys of courting
days,
When you were keeping straight, old man, and
thoughtful in your ways ;
And now she’s yours and you are hers —whose love
the scale down weighs ?
Oh ! save your cheques, a little bit, for there are
bills to pay,
The baker oft forgets to call, the butcher growled
to-day,
And she has got to face it all while you are
miles away !
Oh ! cheer her, just a little bit, for hours are
seeming long ;
Just gurgle down the angry word when things are
going wrong—
She’s just a scrap of womanhood, and you are big
and strong!
RITA SUNYASEE.
At Geelong (Vic.), a rail way-man stumbled
and fell. A lead-pencil in his pocket pierced his
lung, and he is now in a precarious state. Pencil
accidents are common on railway platforms. At
Caulfield (Vic,), some time ago, a railway em-
ploye was instantly killed by a pencil, which was
knocked down his throat while he was holding
it between his teeth. In yet another case the pencil
went through the palate to the brain, causing
instant death.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 16, 1902.
The Five Deficits.
One by one, as the States make up their
accounts for last financial year, the deficits
keep dropping in. Every State has one,
barring Westralia, whose position is excep-
tional, as it isn’t yet affected by inter-State
freetrade, and hasn’t yet lost its border
customs revenue. And even Westralia has a
much smaller surplus than it expected. The
preliminary estimates go to show that on a
fair and honest basis ( i.e ., eliminating the
swindle by which the rents of the pro-
perties in the resumed area of Sydney were
treated as revenue, while the corresponding
liabilities were charged to loans) the
N.S.W. deficit is about £350,000, but
there are hopes that when the accounts are
more carefully inspected, some way may be
found of making it look like only £300,000.
The Victorian deficit is £437,611. The
Queensland shortage is £431,939. South Aus
tralia has a deficit of £222,316. Tasmania
closed its financial year on 31st December,
1901, with a shortage of £44,279. And on
the other side of the account there is Wes-
tralia’s solitary surplus of £198,000, which
arises solely from the fact that Westralia
still retains its border customs revenue, and
even that is a wholly fraudulent surplus
created by using loans for ordinary expendi-
ture purposes. .' Also Westralia’s border
customs are a diminishing quantity and will
disappear altogether in about another four
years, and then Westralia will be on the
same footing and in the same trouble as its
neighbors.
* * * •
If these were mere isolated events they
might be matters of only passing importance,
but they are not. They are rather a con-
tinuation of a permanent policy of deficits.
New South Wales has had 15 deficits
since 1879 —a period of 23 years. Victoria
has had 10 ; Queensland has had 11 ; South
Australia 11 also ; and Tasmania 10. Even
in good years—years when the drought was
not with us, when the inter-State customs
duties were still being collected, when the
revenue was coming in liberally from the
sale of the public estate, when a vast amount
of the expenditure which should have been
charged to revenue was being paid out of
loans, it has been the regular thing for Aus-
tralia to fail to make ends meet, even on
paper. Queensland, N.S.W., Victoria,
South Australia and Tasmania all have great
accumulated deficits to work off, and nearly
£4,000,000 of these have now been, or are
being, added to the permanent debt and
given up as a bad job. A curious supers-
tition to the effect that financial matters
are somehow different in Australia
from financial matters in other lands
through this being “ a new country ” has
aided materially in bringing about this
result. It is held that it is necessary for
Australia to borrow more than any other
place—because this is a new country. It is
pointed out that there is no need to be afraid of
consequences—because this is a new country.
When any rash individual proposes that
Australia should raise enough tax revenue
to at least pay its way, many people arise to
say that it is a disgrace to suppose that any
serious taxation can be required—in a new
country. There is a loose, floating idea that
somehow there should be 15d. in a shilling
because this is a new country, and if there
isn’t, the necessary steps should be taken at
once to cause that number of coppers to be
in a shilling now and hereafter.
* * » *
Twelve months ago it was perfectly well
known that a large source of revenue in the
shape of inter-State duties was going to be cut
'off under Federation. Whether the buyer or
the seller pays the duties is immaterial; when
the Australian people are both buyers and
sellers, as is the case with inter-State trade,
the Australian people pay the duties either
way. The Australian people were going to
cease paying this heavy taxation, therefore
the State Governments—or, at least, those
of Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania and South
Australia—knew that they must, in the ordi-
nary course of events, expect much less
revenue. As deficits were chronic even with
the border tariff receipts they hadn’t the
lease hope that they would be any less chronic
without them. Obviously the only way to
prevent things becoming any worse was to
put on new taxes to make up for those which
had been deliberately and intentionally
abolished. There was no question of putting
new burdens on the people as has been falsely
represented ; it was rather a question of pre-
venting the old burdens, which had proved
wholly insufficient to yield enough revenue to
keep the country solvent, from being dimin-
ished. It is an interesting Australian
characteristic that last year not one Govern-
ment moved in the matter. They all decided
that it was unwise to do anything till they
found out how large the deficit would be.
If they moved prematurely they might—
though it is most improbable—have put on
more taxation than was required to replace
that which had been abolished ; and there
might have been a few thousands over with
which to pay off some of the load of back
deficits, and that was a horror to be avoided
at all costs. So they all resolved that the
best way to find out how deep the precipice
might be was to fall down it. In the pro-
cess of finding out they have accumulated
liabilities, which will probably take about 40
years to pay off—or, rather, at the usual
rate of progress, they will never be paid off
at all, but will cost about £60,000 a year
interest for ever, or thereabouts.
» * * •
In Victoria, the trouble was aggravated by
the fact that old age pensions were a new
charge on the revenue, and no special taxa-
tion had been levied to meet this large extra
outgoing. If Peacock and Irvine and
Shiels couldn’t see that, with old age pen-
sions as a large new item added to the ex-
penditure, and with inter-State customs as a
large old item abolished out of the revenue,
the income, which was barely sufficient
before, was bound to fall very far short, then
their incapacity amounted almost to genius.
All the States had plunged recklessly into a
wicked and useless war without making any
provision whatever for the payment of the
bill, and the fact that the bill was still coming
in was well known. All the States knew that
the amount of interest to be provided on
account of the ever-growing public debt
was becoming a larger item each year.
The whole circumstances were obvious ;
yet there is a tendency in all quarters
to speak of the great shortage as an
unexpected calamity, an undeserved misfor-
tune, and the act of God. Philp and Irvine
and Lewis and Jenkins Iscariot all address
the Legislature on the subject in a hushed
voice, as if somebody was dead on the pre-
mises. They all state that every possibility
in the way of retrenchment must be ex-
hausted before “new burdens are laid on
the people ” —just as if it was a case of
imposing new burdens, instead of replacing,
in a different form, the ones that were taken
off last year. A disgraceful process of sweat-
ing the public service—which is, in great
measure, a very moderately-paid public
service—is to be resorted to, and all manner
of small, inefficient meannesses are proposed
in order to prevent the people being taxed
at something like the rate which the
people of England stand as a matter
of course. Some of the Premiers pro-
pose to scrape up enough, in one way
and another, or just about enough, so far as
they can calculate, to keep the accounts
square next year—that is to say, to make
them square after a few hundreds of thou-
sands of loan money have been used for
revenue purposes. Shiels, of Victoria,
proposes to find enough, by cutting down
wages and other economies, to partly square
the accounts, and is apparently quite content
if next year’s shortage is a little smaller than
this year’s. Nobody, it is quite needless to
say, proposes to do anything towards making
good the drift of the year just ended. That
goes into the general debt to wait for the
turning up of that undefined something
which is to put matters right automatically—
the something that is to turn up on the day
when the supernatural hen lays the golden
egg on Shjels’s doorstep or John See's ship
comes home from Pactolus.
* • » •
The deficits of this one year just closed—
the deficits alone, taking no account of
general borrowings—have created in the
shape of new interest burdens, a load
that, upon each inhabitant, is equal
to about one-half the entire interest
burden of Switzerland, or one-fourth that of
the United States, or one-fifth that of
Sweden. Australia doesn’t take the matter
seriously. The old, loose impression that
things don’t matter in “a new country,”
and that the natural wealth of the country
(loans being mistaken for wealth) will pull it
through, is still on top. There is even a
considerable demand for a “ reduction of the
public burdens,” and for letting things slide
even worse than they slide now. If the
sliding process continues, a good many
things are likely to happen. Old age pen-
sions will probably go overboard in some
time of violent retrenchment; they can’t be
paid out of the deficit, and the deficit added
to the public debt, for ever. State Socialism
will fall into disrepute, for though it has no-
thing to do with the great policy of letting
things slide, the fact that a country which
largely practised it has fallen into semi-
bankruptcy will be set down to its debit.
The minimum-wage policy can’t last if the
present methods continue ; it can only exist
in a reasonably prosperous country, and a
long continuance of the loan and deficit, sys-
tem will make any real prosperity out of the
question. Unless all the Democratic ele-
ments of the community are prepared to
make honest and intelligent finance the first
plank of their platform they may almost as
well get out of the political business. The
Democratic ideals can no more afford to keep
the company they do than a new sect, how-
ever well-meaning, can afford to habitually
associate with unreformed burglars. The
great disaster is not approaching any faster
now than it was fifteen yeai's ago, when there
was no Labor in politics—butit is fifteen years
nearer all the same. And if it comes while
the Labor Party is in politics that
Party having made no serious effort to
dissociate itself from the old evil methods—
Labor will find that a large share of the
obloquy will be heaped on it and on its
policy. The person who remains about when
he knows there is going to be a dead body
in the vicinity, without taking any steps to
prove that he was not responsible for its
deadness, is an ill-advised individual, and
can only expect suspicion to fall on him,
especially when the real criminal is his
bitter enemy and has influence with the
coroner.
The Dutch-German Problem.
An American authority has expressed the opinion
that the inevitable German reach-out for Holland,
commonly looked upon as one of the possibilities
of the far, dim future, is really quite an adjacent
event—one that may probably be expected within
the next five or six years. If the spirit of Jingo
had been at its best the announcement might
have led to serious excitement, even at this far
end of the British empire, but the spirit of Jingo
is weary just now, and is taking things in a torpid
fashion. The fact that Germany wants Holland,
and that some day it may raise Cain to get it, and
that in the getting of it there may be great and
general trouble, is not widely known among the
masses, but the fact itself is solid enough.
Germany, after being long weak and dis-
united, is now a strong, solid empire,
though possibly neither so strong nor so solid
as it looks; and there is every sign of a
fixed desire to gather up the fragments of Lost
Germany—the bits that fell away from the central
authority in the days when there was practically
no central authority at all. The Danish duchies
have been recovered with the long arm and the
mailed fist.' Alsace and Lorraine have been
recovered. The German provinces of Austria are
badly wanted. German Switzerland would pro-
bably not be objected to. And Holland, the
valuable province which dropped away over 600
years ago, and which drifted back into German
hands in the 16th century and drifted off again,
is wanted quite as badly as any of them—perhaps
more so.
* * * *
The German reach-out after Holland is not
in the nature of an attempt at conquest—at
least, not as conquest is commonly under-
stood. The idea is that Holland should become
a part of the empire, retaining its own
sovereigns and its own laws, as Bavaria, Saxony,
Wurtemburg, and the other sub-kingdoms do, but
supplying its share of the military force and mili-
tary contributions in time of war and sharing in
the empire’s dangers and the empire’s protection.
The German empire is solid for war and trade
purposes, but otherwise its component parts do,
m most respects, very much as they please. Ger-
many wants to recover its long-lost province for
the sake of the Dutch army and navy, and
for the sake of adding 5,000,000 of a good
fighting race to its population, and, above all,
for the sake of the Dutch ports and the Dutch
commerce. The commerce would still remain
Dutch, but it would be German as well, and would
look good in the statistics. Also, it wants to see
the great Dutch colonial possessions become an
appanage of the German empire, .and an addi-
tional incentive to the German Imperial swelled
head. But Holland broke away from Ger-
many, just as Portugal broke away from
Spain, so long ago that its language and
its traditions have diverged widely from the
old stock. Like Portugal, too, it has a great
historical Past; these two States and Sweden
probably have a bigger Past, considering their
narrow resources, than any others in Europe,
unless the very far back records of Greece are
taken into consideration. And Holland doesn’t
want its monarch to become a sub-king or sub-
queen ; it doesn’t want to share the German
glories and wear the German spiked helmet; it
doesn’t want to merge its great traditions in Ger-
many ; and it regards William the Sudden as an
aggressive and unpleasant character.
* * * *
A thousand years ago the German empire em-
braced practically all the German-speaking peo-
ples, and they were many. It was immensely
powerful in those early days, but still it
was an empire built on sand, and was be-
ginning to decay. It was divided and sub-
divided among semi-independent princes and
princelings—in fact the process of sub-division
went on and on till the country was truly described,
in comparatively recent years, as reposing under
the rule of 2000 sovereigns. Certain of these
elected the emperor, and after a while they grew
into the habit of electing a monarch who couldn’t
kick back. Germany had practically homeless
emperors, and absentee emperors (like Richard
of Cornwall, who dropped in three times
in all to see how his empire was getting
along), and penniless emperors, and emperors
(like Wenceslaus) who took to drink to forget
their dignity, and an emperor who was arrested
by his butcher, under due process of law, for his
unpaid meat-bill. When the alleged dignity came
at last to be practically hereditary in the Hafs-
burg family, that race found that its own narrow
family territories were, as a rule, all that it
could rely upon for military purposes. The
Hafsbuiigs were lords of Germany in a sense,
and Germany assisted them in time of need if it
felt inclined. Sometimes it made war on them
instead. Sometimes it engaged strangers to make
war on them. Sometimes the subordinate princes
fought among themselves. Sometimes they only
looked on. The emperors could not annex any
great extent of German territory, sufficient
to make them really powerful German sovereigns,
without fighting single-handed, their own empire
and probably France and other foreign allies as
well, so they went after strange gods and sought for
conquests in Hungary, Bohemia, Italy, and along
the Turkish border. And at last it came to pass
that the head of the German empire was a great
monarch ruling over a little bit of Germany and a
tumultuous mass of non-German States, and when
the larger of his non-German States were not in
insurrection simultaneously he got on reasonably
well. Sometimes the Turks besieged his capital.
Sometimes his alleged German subordinates allied
themselves against him with the French or the
Swedes, or the Poles. Two of the Hapsbcrgs
fought their own subjects for 30 years on religious
matters, but they were men of unusual resource.
The weaker variety of emperor, in such condi-
tions, couldn’t possibly keep the empire together,
for he didn’t own the empire. If a remote
bit of it fell away it often meant an enemy
the less. The Swiss provinces were lost.
So was Holland. So were Alsace and
Lorraine. So was the shadowy claim on the
kingdom of Arles. The Swedes took a large slice
of the empire, and kept it for a while, and part
of it fell to Denmark. Sometimes the foreign
potentate who came into possession was supposed
to become a subject prince of the emperor, who
had already more subjects of that kind than he
cared for. Sometimes the emperor couldn't pre-
vent the loss of outlying states for want of an
army. Sometimes he couldn’t go to the defence
of threatened territories because the territories of
some of his loving but too-powerful subordinates
were in the road, and he would have had his throat
cut as he went.
* „ • «
Then Brandenburg, in North Germany, grew so
powerful that it could wipe the floor at times with
its alleged master single-handed, and the kingdom
of Prussia developed. The old German empire
went to pieces in the Napoleonic wars, in which
the Emperor had to fight against France and his
own principal alleged subjects combined, and for
60 years there was no Emperor of Germany—not
even of the old homeless variety who used to be in
trouble over the unpaid meat bill. But the
Prussian kings secured the lost dignity in 1871—
or rather they secured part of it, for William the
Sudden is “German Emperor,” where Wence-
slaus the Drunkard was Emperorof Germany, and
the nominal descendant of the CLesars. Then
things assumed a new aspect. The new German
crown was made really hereditary in thef amily of the
strongest member of the league. The new Emperor
had a sufficiently powerful German following of
his own to enable him to hold his ground against
the biggest of his sub-kings. He wasn’t ham-
pered by a vast mass of Hungarian, Italian, and
Bohemian alleged subjects; nor were there 18
strange languages spoken in his dominions, and
the Turks swarming over his back fence.
The old disorderly Diet gave place to a regu-
larly-constituted Legislature, and there was
a great solid permanent German army under one
head, instead of the multitudinous contingents
which used to be granted by the Diet after four
months’ deliberation, and which used to turn up
six months late and at half their nominal strength,
if they turned up at all. Under these new and
greatly improved circumstances, the idea of
recovering the lost provinces has become a common
one in Germany. The ambition is for a new Ger-
many, which will include everybody who speaks
the German language or anything that closely
resembles it. Schleswig, Holstein, Lauenburg,
Alsace and Lorraine have already been recovered;
though, after recovery, the two latter were treated
in a loud, coarse, aggressive fashion that wasn’t
calculated to encourage the others. The German,
or partially German, provinces of Austria are
badly wanted. The German portion of Switzer-
land is of less importance, though it probably
wouldn’t be objected to. But Holland is that
portion of Lost Germany which is of the greatest
moment. The great difficulty is that it has been
lost so long that the title is very much faded and
gnawed into rags by the teeth of time. Also,
there is no party in Holland, as in Austria, which
wants to become really German again. It is 650
years since a Hollander was elected Emperor of
Germany, and he didn’t make enough profit on
the contract to be worth much in a traditional
sense.
* * * *
The,German’s view of the matter is-that he is
coming back into his lost inheritance —that his
severed race is destined to be one again—that
unity is a good thing, and that even William the
Sudden isn’t so awful as he looks. The Dutch
view of the case is different. And the two views
will doubtless collide some day. Then the map
of Europe, which has been resting for about 22
years, will be altered again in some fashion or
other.


Is Federation a Failure?
Mr. J. H. Carruthers, known somewhat to fame
as “ Cocky ” Carruthers, but described on this
occasion as “ The Honorable J. H. Carruthers,
M. Ex-Treasurer, Minister for Lands, and
Minister for Education, New South Wales,’’lends
all the imposing weight of that title to a mournful
little twitter in United Australia arguing that
Federation is practically a failure so far as
N. is concerned :
I have no hesitation in saying that the brief experience
of N.S.W. has been such that, if a poll were taken for and
against Federation to-morrow, not 20 per cent, of the
votes recorded would be in favor. And I speak as one
whose loyalty to the cause is still unshaken, and whose
vote would still be cast in favor of the Constitution.
The reasons for this grave discontent in the mind
of Mr. Carruthers are (1) that the people of
N.S.W. were led to believe that a Revenue and
not a Protective tariff would be imposed ; (2) that
the Federal Government is extravagant; (3) that
there are no advantages from Federation ; (4) that
the Federal Government shows “ manifest un-
toncern in matters affecting the States.”
* • # *
These are fair samples of the sour grumbling
which is rife in N.S.W. just now. So perhaps a
little truth on these points may be re-stated with
id vantage. First, N.S. Wales was never led to
believe that a Federal tariff would not be a Pro-
tective one. The Freetrade party in N.S.W.
fought strenuously against Federation, so long as
it could, on the ground that it would kill Free-
trade. Dry Dog Reid’s famous simile as to one
sober man going to live with five drunken neigh-
bors represented in its most candid form the Free-
trade opposition to union. When it was a question
»f becoming Federalists or going out of politics
most of the Freetrade politicians found grace.
What they tried to make out then in order to
“save their faces” is immaterial. What is im-
portant is that the clear issue was set forth from
'he very first that Federation would kill Freetrade.
* * » *
That the Federal Government is extravagant is,
to an extent, true. It has spent money on Dukes,
Marquises and Cohentingents that was abso-
lutely wasted. That its extravagance has had
any material effect on taxation or on the state of
the various Treasuries in Australia is untrue.
The waste of which the Federal Government has
been guilty has, on the whole, represented a very
paltry sum. It is the wild waste of the State
Governments, particularly of the N.S.W. State
Government (which Mr. Carruthers was so
anxious to join),' that has led to the present
desperate condition of the public accounts in
various parts of the Commonwealth.
• • • ■
That there are no advantages from federation
is partly an untruth and partly an impudent
“ turning of the cate in the pan.” There has been
a decided stimulus given to manufacturing in-
dustry in N.S.W. by the Federal tariff. That
there has not been a greater stimulus is due to the
fact that the Cobden party, with which Mr,
Carruthers associates himself, has steadily set
its face against any measures to encourage Aus-
tralian industry (has savagely endeavoied, for in-
stance, to wreck the proposed iron-works at
Lithgow), and strives, by creating the impression
that the tariff now imposed will not be per-
manent, to scare away manufactures. Mr. Car-
ruthers (with other Freetraders) is in the posi-
tion of a small boy hanging on to a man’s legs,
kicking and biting, and squealing at him because
he doesn’t get along faster.
* * ♦ m
The “neglect by the Federal Government of
matters affecting the States ” resolves itself, when
Mr. Carruthers comes down to details, into a
series of undignified grumbles because N.S.W. is
not allowed all its own way. The Federal Cabinet
sits sometimes in Melbourne : civil servants are
allowed to work in Melbourne : the Governor-
General lives in Melbourne. If only Melbourne
could be bombarded by the auxiliary squadron
how much more brotherly and Federal the
parochial politicians of the Carruthers type
would feel !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
To Rehoboam Australis.
Some items from the new “ Book of Proverbs ’
(1) It should be the ambition of every democrat to
bring about as fair a distribution of the country’s wealth
as possible, but unless there is a reasonable amount of
wealth to distribute the distribution will do little good.
(2) It is a great thing to nationalise an industry. The
first essential is to get an industry to nationalise.
(3) It is better to have an industry that isn’t national-
ised than to have no industry at all.
(4) It is just as bad to patronise the foreign sweater as
the local sweater. In one sense it is worse. We can
make the local sweater leave off sweating, but the foreign
sweater is beyond our jurisdiction.
(5) The best thing is to manufacture our own goods in
locally-owned factories —then the wages and profits both
remain in the country. The next best thing is to manu-
facture them in local factories that are owned abroad-
then the wages, at all events, remain in the country, and
we can see to it that the wages are fair and reasonable.
The worst thing of all is to import, for then both the
Wages and the profits go out of the country, while the
people who should have received these wages are sup-
ported by loans and public works.
(6) No country ever got rich by loans and public
works.
(7) A new boot factory or woollen mill or iron-work
opens up the country a great deal more than an unpaid-
for railway that leads to nowhere In particular.
(8) The outside capitalist who sends his money here
to start- a new industry which will employ people pre-
viously unemployed isn’t one-tenth as bad a public enemy
as the outside capitalist who sends his money here to buy
lands, houses and shares. The latter doesn’t afford any
new employment, or supply any new wages, or diminish
in any way the mass of imports that we have to pay for.
(9) If Australia owned all its own public works, instead
of them being really, to the extent of about £190,000,000,
the property of the English mortgagee, then every
inhabitant of the Commonwealth would be worth nearly
£5O as his share of the railways and other public
works of this country, and every family of five would be
worth nearly £250. That would be the biggest, grandest,
and most comprehensive State Socialism on record, and
every foreign loan makes State Socialism more remote.
(10) When a property is so heavily mortgaged that it
won’t produce enough to pay the interest, then it is mere
foolishness to call the nominal owner the real possessor.
Qur public works are in that state. One of the first great
industries to be really nationalised should be our railways.
(11) An enterprise owned by private citizens living in
Australia and spending their profits in Australia is quite
as national as a State enterprise that is mortgaged to the
last penny to Cohen in Europe. Then only point in favor
of the latter is that we may pay off Coiien some day—
though there are no signs of it so far.
(12) The least national thing of all is when the enter-
prise and the employment and the Cohen are in Europe,
and we only import the proceeds.
(18) The first step towards nationalising things is to
levy a weighty absentee-tax.
(14) Almost the only thing that is really nationalised
here is our deficit.

The Shearing Dispute.
At the time of the formulation of the shearers’
demand for an increase in the shearing rate from
20s. to 255. per 100 in N.S,W. this season, public
opinion was puzzled to resolve on which side the
right lay. On the one hand, the pastoralists
could plead that any addition to their working
expenses at a time of exceptional loss through
drought was unreasonable. On the other hand,
the shearers could point to the fact that the
drought seriously increased their working expenses
too; and, in addition, very greatly reduced the
number of sheep for shearing, and consequently
cut down their aggregate earnings. In brief, their
contention was that it would be a harder task
for them to shear now 20,000,000 sheep than to
shear 30,000,000 in a normal time, and that, there-
fore, by an increase in the rate, the aggregate
wage-fund should be reduced from £30,000 to
£25,000, instead of to £20,000. On which side of the
controversy the balance of j ustice was, appeared to
be a question most difficult of decision without a
very full examination of all the facts. But since
then the Pastoralists’ Union has consistently
acted in such a way as to give the public the
impression that it fears an impartial examination
into its side of the case. A reference of the matter
to the Arbitration Court was ingeniously baulked
by the promotion of the Machine Shearers’Union,
which, despite all that has been said to the con-
trary, certainly was founded in the interests of the
pastoralists, and probably is financed by the
pastoralists. Then the offer of the A.W.U.,
representing the shearers, to accept payment at
the old rate if sums representing the advance
asked for were paid into the hands of a third party
to abide an ultimate decision of the Arbitration
Court, was refused. A conference on the points in
dispute was also refused at first, and only granted,
so to speak, at the point of the bayonet. Then
the Pastoralists’ Union refused to even discuss a
compromise (the shearers suggested 225. 6d.
per 100 as a split-the-difference settlement),
and thus made the conference abortive.
All this must, in unprejudiced minds, argue
strongly against the Pastoralists’ Union. That
there have been faults on both sides is plain
enough. The A.W.U. is by no means beyond
criticism. But the responsibility for the indus-
trial war which now adds to the troublesof N.S.W.
rests mainly with the Pastoralists’ Union.

An Insult to the House of Dodderers.
Premier Lewis’s proposal for the amendment of
the Fiyspeck’s constitution:
That on or about the Ist May, 1903, the Legislative
Oouncil and House of Assembly be dissolved.
That the duration of the present House of Assembly be
extended until that date.
That no extraordinary vacancies in either of the existing
Houses be filled up between the date when the bill to give
effect to these resolutions is passed by both Houses and
the Ist May, 1903.
That after the Ist May, 1903, the Parliament of Tas-
mania shall consist of the Governor and one House only,
to be called the House of Legislature.
That the said House be composed of 30 elected members.
That for the purposes of electing members to serve in
the said House the State be divided into 20 districts, as
nearly as practicable containing an equal population, as
ascertained by the last census.
That each of such 20 districts return one member,
elected on the present House of Assembly franchise.
That 10 other districts be formed, each composed of
two of the districts referred to in paragraph 6.
That each of such 10 districts return one member,
elected on the present Legislative Council franchise, who
shall hold their seats for a term of five years, except in
the ease of members chosen at the first election, ora
general election following any dissolution of the whole
House.
That of the 10 members so elected two shall retire each
year. That the two members to retire in each of the first
five years, after the new Constitution comes into force,
or after any dissolution of the whole House, shall be
determined by lot.
That, subject to such conditions as may be prescribed,
the votes of two Orders of members, elected under para-
graphs 7 and 9 respectively, upon any question may be
taken separately, and unless a majority of members of both
Orders vote in favor of the question it shall be considered
lose.
If measure be acceptable to majority of members of one
Order, and be not acceptable to majority of members of
other Order, and if, after an interval of six months, same
measure is again proposed, and disagreement continues,
then whole House may be dissolved, and upon same
measure being again proposed within six months after
such dissolution it shall be affirmed or rejected by
majority of members voting together.
Thus the old superstition once more gets hold of
Premier Lewis. The original idea was to reduce the
Council to 10 and the Assembly to 20 members, and
to let them sit and vote as one House. The 10 were
to be elected on the present Council franchise, and
the 20 on the Assembly franchise, bub the idea of
making a speok and a scrap into two separate
branches of the Legislature was abandoned. But
Lewis has already weakened on that; the thought
of the Assembly members outvoting the Council
members, and Property losing its sacred right of
individual obstruction, was too shocking. So the
30 are to sit together, and talk together, and drink
together, and vote separately as two distinct insti-
tutions put in one room to save gas. It is
to be as if Lewis and his dog shared the
one kennel for economy of rent, but
barked differently. Whether the two “Orders”
will occupy different ends of the apart-
ment ; whether there may be a quorum at one
end and no quorum at another ; whether business
will go right on just the same if there are 12 of
one “Order” present and only one of the other,
and the one, by voting “No,” will be able to
nullify the decision of the 12 who voted “Yes ” ;
or whether half the division (that of the “ Order ”
that is sufficiently represented) will be taken to-
night and the other half be postponed till the
other “ Order ” turns up; or whether, lacking a
quorum of one “Order,” both “Orders” must
adjourn, doesn’t appear. Lewis’s original pro-
posal to lump 10 Councillors and 20 members of
Assembly into one House was at least workable.
It was simply a perpetual Joint Sitting, and there
are plenty of one-House Legislatures in the British
Empire as precedents. The idea of having two
separate Houses in one room is a wild and whirl-
ing innovation, not to say revolution. It will
save gas, flunkeys, Hansard, and one Speaker,
but it leaves the original absurdity of 10 members
pretending to be a whole House” of Parliament
just where it was. There are still to be two
Houses, but one of them is to doss with the other
for purposes of economy. If Premier Lewis
carries out his scheme he will have the satisfac-
tion of having produced the most original Par-
liament in the British Empire, and originality
isn’t a thing Tasmanians hanker after,
A Bishop on Kanakas.
“ The nationalities of Cairns are decidedly mixed,” he
says, inter alia ; “ the Federal Government, with that
rare courage which marks truly great men, have fastened
upon the most harmless and ( anparenthe.se ) the most
unprotected, for their special fury. Chinese are there
by thousands, and singularly useful they are ; Japanese
also. Hut it is the Kanaka that must go. Thesj last
seem singularly inoffensive. They work hard at work no
white man should be asked to undertake (for it is deadly
monotonous), are law-abiding, quiet people, who spend
their money where they earn it. And many of them
come under very distinct Christian influence, and live
simple, pious lives. It is curious that no plans for
‘deporting’ Chinese or Japanese have yet been put for-
ward. It is not hard to see the reason. When we wish
to play the bully, we naturally choose the smallest boy.”
—Bishop Stretch’s parish paper.
Ignorance is evident in every line of the above.
Brisbane Courier (10th June, 1901) contained a
Cairns telegram which told how 13 Kanakas
appeared before the police court charged with
rioting. Five had their heads in bandages, and
the police deposed that the prisoners had fought a
savage battle with drawn knives. On 27th keb.,
1901, a Bundaberg deputation waited on Premier
Philp concerning the Kanakas in that town, and
told him the “boys” were a “menace to the peace-
ful inhabitants.” A Royal Commission that sat
on the police in 1899 remarked that of 25 murders
(authors undiscovered) committed in recent years no
less than 18occurred in the sugar districts. “Presu-
mably,” ranthereport, “the authors are Kanakas.”
On Christmas Eve, 1899, nearly 1000 Kanakas were
rioting in the main street of Bundaberg and caused
a reign of terror, and the Bishop tells us the- poor
fellows are “singularly inoffensive.” Moreover,
they lead simple pious lives yet Q. prison
statistics show that Polynesian prisoners are,
proportionately to the Polynesian population,
three times as numerous as Europeans.
Tommy Tanna is “ harmless ” and the China-
man is “singularlyuseful”—as is also the Jap.
If the Bishop ran things he would expel no
alien of them all—he frankly goes holus-bolus for
a Mongrel Australia and denounces as cowardly
those who want a white population. He remarks,
moreover, that if the islanders “ are deported they
will undoubtedly be murdered by their heathen
countrymen.” This is a risk to which Kanakas
have always been exposed by Q. legislation but
now is the first time the clergy have ever dis-
covered it. Some 20 years ago, when kidnapping
was rife, islanders were dumped down on the
wrong islands, regardless of consequences (as
proved by Royal Commission) but never a parson
in all Bananaland dared to preach against that
state of things. Now— how zealous are the black
coats!
Done With.!
The real degree of Australian interest in the late
S’African war can be accurately measured now
that the struggle is over ; and no facts are more
valuable in assisting accurate judgment than the
recent transport incidents. The Drayton Grange
set out from S’Africa to Australia with 2000
Australian soldiers penned-up on her decks, and a
markedly-infectious and dangerous disease on
board, with no proper hospital-accommodation,
with but a slim medicine-chest (and that wrung
from reluctant authority). The Britannic was dis-
patched to Maoriland under conditions of crowd-
ing almost as bad. The Norfolk was forced to carry
an appendicitis patient on from Adelaide because
South Australia refused a landing to a soldier who
had not been one of her State citizens. All thesein-
cidents are significant. If the war against the Boers
had been one for right and liberty ; if Australian
participation therein had been a necessary duty of
patriotism; if these returning soldiers are heroes
who risked their lives to preserve the Empire and
to ward off a possible tide of invasion from these
shores —surely Australia would not be so infamous
as to allow what, in the Drayton Grange case at
least, was tantamount to the cold-blooded murder
of her soldiers. To send an over-crowded troop-
ship to sea with an epidemic of measles on board,
and with practically no hospital accommodation,
is to condemn a certain number of them to death.
The English War Office did that in the case of the
Drayton Grange, did it apparently out of unneces-
sary and reckless cruelty, for there was no reason,
except the saving of a few pounds, either to over-
crowd the transport, or to send her away pesti-
lence-laden. Yet there has been practically no
Australian protest, a grumbling word or two here
and there, but not the indictment—prompt, re-
lentless, vengeful—which a nation should frame
against the murderers of half a score or so of its
“ heroes.” But, alas for the men who went away
with the beat of drum and the great throb of
cheering throats, it is quite palpable that they
are not regarded as “heroes”; that all the talk
of politicians and papers to that effect was flam ;
and that now, having served the purpose of the
merchants who had butter and oats and meat and
horses to sell, and of the politicians who had
titles to gain, they can go hang. They’re done
with. All the War Office orders have been placed,
all the titles gathered in, all that was to be
made out of the war by the interested Jingoes
made, and now they are anxious to forget
the men who volunteered to do the killing and the
farm-burning. If the Drayton Grange had kindly
sunk with her 2000 souls on board ere she reached
the Westralian coast, there would probably not
have been a sincere tear in any representative
Jingo eye. And the more men who die of
pneumonia or appendicitis or overcrowding, the
fewer there will be to limp about after the
promised tramway billets which they won’t get,
and to look reproachfully at the men who had
promised to enshrine them for ever in the grate-
ful hearts of the nation. The Australian repre-
sentative Jingo attitude towards the returned
soldiers is that of a man towards the women whom
he has dragged on to the streets. He wants them
above all things to keep out of his sight. They’re
done with 1
