Local Government.
The N.S.W. Government proceeds to put another
patch on the State’s municipal system. There is
no attempt at establishing local government over
all the settled districts of the State, but Premier
See’s measure does a certain amount in the way
of improvement. The municipal franchise is to
be widened. Rating may be on unimproved value
if the municipality so prefers. Government
property not used for public purposes is made
rateable. The conncils are to have more extensive
powei'3 in dealing with sanitation, building,
traffic, etc. There is to be an audit of the accounts
by a State auditor ; but if the rate levied fis not
large enough and there is a deficit, he has not the
power, as he should have, to order an increased
rate to be levied till the shortage is paid off. The
general rate may be as high as Is. 6d. in
the £ on unimproved value, which is more
than the present figure but ridiculously in-
sufficient all the same. The Government may
guarantee municipal loans up to the amount
of six years’ income of the municipality, but there
is apparently no definite provision by which the
Government may recover the money if the council
doesn’t feel inclined to pay. Special rates may
be struck for the repayment of the money bor-
rowed ; it should read that they must be struck.
Any municipality may lease land for a period not
exceeding seven years when the rates have fallen
seriously in arrear, and it may sell the land 12
years later if the owner doesn’t pay up and resume
possession in the interim—which is a remarkably
long interim. The trouble is that nobody wants a
seven years’ lease for any respectable building
purpose, and if the land is only fit for building on
the seven years’ lease, is of no saleable value, or
very little. Also, it is proposed to decide at last
that mines are to be rated on surface value only—•-
winch is a decision of very doubtful wisdom,
though it is better to settle the vexed question
unwisely than not settle it at all. And the real,
all-round Local Government Bill is to be intro-
duced when Parliament has time—which it never
has. It has rows and drink and John Norton,
bub it never has time. The only way a big
measure is ever passed is by making time.

The Guile of 48 Rich Landlords.
The House of 48 Rich Landlords that sits and is
a public disgrace in Melbourne decided to postpone
for one month consideration of the Bill for the
continuation of the present Minimum Wage Law,
which law expires at the close of this session. The
Government gave notice that it would move that
the decision be rescinded —with what success
won’t be known in time to record the melancholy
facts and circumstances of the case in this issue.
In a sense the House of 48 Globular Proprietors
has an excuse that might pass muster, if it wasn’t
known that it was just as hostile when it hadn’t
the excuse. High wages make goods dearer, un-
less there are other advantages to counterbalance
this fact. Now that there are no border tariffs
Victoria can’t afford, working on even terms, to
pay more than its neighbors do—especially as
it is a State which is deficient in coal, the
great necessary of the manufacturing industry,
and so works at a disadvantage. If it pays higher
wages than its near neighbors, and, at the same
time, has to pay more for coal, its industries must
suffer under inter-State Freetrade, unless it can
make up for the handicap by extra skill. There-
fore the object of every democrat should be to get
the whole question of Labor legislation handed
over to the Federal Parliament, so that sweating
shall be abolished all round. But as it isn’t
evident yet that Victoria’s minimum-wage law is
handicapping it in the race, there is no reason or
necessity to drop it in the meantime. Such an
extreme step is only due when it is obvious that
the present system won’t pay under the new con-
ditions. The House of Landlords makes no secret
of its fixed resolve to oppose the transfer of labor
legislation to the Federal Parliament; a Minimum
Wage Law for all Australia is the last thing it is
seeking after. And it is just assolid in its objection
to a Minimum Wage Law for Victoria separately.
Its attitude is that Victoria can’t afford to have a
Minimum Wage Law if other Siates haven’t got
one; that it will do everything in its power to
prevent other States getting one; and that it'
wants to drop the present law without waiting to
find out whether Victoria can afford to maintain
it or not. And yet the Upper House goes home
quite safely on dark nights, and nothing falls on
its head.

A Lovely Fiasco!
A few weeks ago the Philp Govt, brought out a
set of regulations concerning religious education
in State schools, regulations which deliberately
violated the spirit of the Q. Education Act, which
says expressly that secular instruction only shall
be given in school hours in State schools. These
regulations were practically equivalent to clause
17 of the N.S.W. Act. The clergy themselves,
who had run a bogus referendum, admitted it
covered only “a very partial part of the popula-
tion,” and Premier Philp alluded to this result as
the opinion “of the majority.” The other day, on
a motion for adjournment of the House, it was
contended that these regulations were ultra vires ,
and out of the whole Q. Assembly only one Govt,
supporter (outside the Ministry) dared say a word
in support of the new departure. Attorney-
General Rutledge had to support Philp, but
every other lawyer who spoke was dead
against him. Then the Premier admitted the
regulations would be withdrawn for re-considera-
tion. Next day, Government journals slated
Philp for trying to please everybody, and the
Minister for Public Instruction said that he also
thought the change was ultra vires ; and the rage
of parsondom, which had worked this crooked and
illegal thing, was a terrible sight to witness.

An Unfortunate Habit.
Tasmanian Auditor-General Mitchell reports
about a score of irregularities in the accounts of
various departmental officers and local bodies.
Hobart City Council is pulled up with a round
turn for wrongly using its funds for illuminating
the way of the Duke and for paying the “ exes.”
of official trips to Melbourne and Sydney at Com-
monwealth celebration time. A very exalted
Govt, official is hauled over the coals for neglecting
to furnish correct accounts of the expenditure on
tools, &c., bought by his department at Duke
time. This official says the stock-book has been
lost, and he really “ dunno where it are.” Money
rightly payable into the Treasury has been paid
into private accounts by other officials, the only
explanation of which is that the Ducal visit so
upset their calculations that they were unable
to distinguish their own private banking
accounts from that of the Treasurer of the State !
Of course men filled to the muzzle with
loyalty and champagne might easily make such a
mistake; and it is only fair to state that they
were sorry for error, and apologised hand-
somely when they got sober. Zeehan Town Board
sent a wire of welcome to a returned contingenter
and charged it to petty cash; a £lO honorarium
was also included in the same convenient account.
Queenstown Town Board had its photo, taken,
and paid for the awful picture out of the rates.
Strahan Marine Board also had its photo, taken,
and paid tor it out of loan moneys. Some M’s.P.
visited Strahan harbor works, and ran up an
account for <£37 Bs. at the local pub. This was also
paid out of loan moneys, and like the other items,
has been disallowed by the State auditor. Numer-
ous rate-collectors have been found £llos. 4Jd. short
in their cash and a chief clerk in a large municipal
establishment was £4O odd short, thus providing a
pleasing variety for the inspectors, who were
heartily sick of discovering defalcations of
£llos. 4£d. in so many places. But as the £4O
gentleman had an unaccountable “surplus” of
£l3 odd in his cash-box, which some burglar may
have deposited there for safety, and as the “old
man ” was an alderman and promptly made up the
deficiency, there was no scandalous police court
prosecution or anything like that. The clerk was
reduced £lO a year in salary and told to
be more careful to issue a receipt to any
burglar who might wish to deposit a “sur-
plus ”in the cash-box in future. Apropos the
muddle in the one Savings Bank, owing
to the temporary absence of the postmaster,
who is serving a long sentence for a local
bank robbery, the State Auditor says, in effect:
“The bank robbery being yet, to a certain extent,
sub judice, I do not think it advisable to say as
much about the subject as I might.” This seems
to indicate that some further irregularities may be
anticipated. So far, only one prosecution has
eventuated —an uninfluential license-collector has
been committed for trial. The authorities appear
to consider that as it was Duke year, and Returned
Contingent year, and Peace year, some allowance
should be made for defaulters, and that small
irregularities should not be made the subject of
too strict inquiry. In fact, State Auditor
Mitchell is regarded as a somewhat over-zealous
officer, and will probably be given to understand
that the Crown Law authorities are of opinion
that these small deficits are not so much crimes as
casual misfortunes arising out of over excitement.

4
The English capacity for producing light
opera of a really humorous quality and of genuine
musical merit seems to have perished with the
Gilbert and Sullivan partnership. The cause is
probably as much in the lack of demand as of
power to supply. Some recent comic operas, such
as the “Rose of Persia,” which approximated to
the “Mikado’’and “ Gondoliers” level, were not
nearly so successful as “The Geisha,” “Floro-
dora,” and other pieces which were tuifeful farces
rather than comic operas. The public demands
something that will approximate very closely to
the bills of fare presented “at the Halls,” some-
thing without any definite plot to bother about,
with no humor which is not of the most simple
or obvious kind, a piece which you can drop in
to see an hour late without prejudice to the enjoy-
ment of the rest of the show. “ San Toy,” pro-
duced for the first time in Sydney, at fhe Palace
Theatre, last Saturday, caters most successfully
for the public demand. It has several bright and
tinkling tunes, some songs with fun in them, a
few good dances; gives opportunity to a bevy of
bright girls to dress well—many in gorgeous
Chinese silks, a few in modern European raiment;
and has a bright stage setting. There are jokes
as obvious as those of a Simultaneous Mission, and
not above the mental calibre even of a Legislative
Council. And there is a cuinbrously impossible
story of a Chinese girl masquerading as a boy to
escape the affection of the Chinese Emperor, then
ultimately reaching his harem, to be finally given
up by the great Peacock-feather-dispenser to the
English officer who loves her sincerely. The
officer, with a marked departure from the prin-
ciples of melodrama, doesn’t conclude that she has
been ruined by her stay under the Imperial roof.
Such a play must almost entirely depend
on the players, which is in accordance with the
present ruling tradition of the stage, that that
playwright is most successful who least hampers
with his “ book ” the talents of the actors. The
company which opened in Sydney, though it is
only a No. 2 co., a sort of understudy to the No. 1
organisation of Lauri, Carrie Moore, etc.,
created from the outset a most favorable
impression, and scored an emphatic success.
Perhaps, on the whole, its interpretation
of the piece was better than that by the No. 1
co. There was no laziness shown; no tenor was
too tired to sing or comedian too tired to dance,
and without there being any timidity there was a
marked lack of that over-confidence which verges
on assurance and presumption. There are no very
fine voices in the co. ; on the other hand, no
markedly poor ones. The whole performance was
even and pleasing.
• • * *
The four persons on whom the greatest
responsibilities rest are Mr. Fred. Graham (Li),
Miss Rose Musgrove (San Toy), Miss Lilian
Digges, as a maid, and Mr. Frank Pierce (Yen
How). Miss Musgrove in the name part is
always graceful, her acting bright, her singing, if
not distinguished, pleasing. As a pert lady’s
maid Miss Lilian Digges has the chief female
comedy part, and plays it with dainty grace,
dancing and acting well. Mr. Fred Graham’s
was the broad comedy part, and he got the utmost
possible amount of fun out of it; the times when
lie and Miss Digges are on the stage together are
the brightest of the evening. Yen How is played
by Mr. Frank Pierce in the Howard Vernon style
with much quiet humor. Other pleasing features,
in a performance which makes big calls on a very
large number of people, were Mr. Troode, as the
Emperor of China ; Mr.. Arthur Crane, as Capt.
Bobby Preston; Mr. White, as Fo Hop ; and
Miss Lulu Evans, as Poppy.

The Brough co. continued its fare welling
process at the Royal on Saturday by staging
“ The Liars ” for a two-night season. Sydney felt
better for its brief look at that picturesque play.
At the same time everybody realised that Lady
Jessica and Lady Rosamund and Dolly Coke, and
poor, obliterated Freddy Tatton, and old toothless
Archibald Coke were a most inefficient crowd of
liars, and if the thing was done as badly in real
life the great mining industry might just as well
close up for good. Brough, Leslie Victor, and
Misses Brenda Gibson and Helen Bogle did some
specially good work in “The Liars.” So did
Percy Walshe as conscientious old Coke who was
seized upon while on his way to get his teeth
attended to, and ordered to corroborate the lie,
which, being troubled by his conscience, he did by
saying “Yes-No” several times in great haste.
As the Brough programme stands at time of
writing, the co. will have done two nights of
“Lady Huntworth’s Experiment” before this
issue is before the public. To-night (Thursday)
and Friday the grand old “Village Priest” is
promised.
♦ * » «
Though “Iris” departed last Friday, the
public hasn’t yet quite finished arguing about that
surprising tragedy. In The Bulletin’s opinion
“Iris” gets ahead of “ Mrs. Tanqueray the
play which is most frequently quoted in connec-
tion withjt—in the fact that it supplies a more
solid reason for the final tragedy. Aubrey Tan-
queray married his second wife, the good woman
of bad character, and his second wife married him,
with the knowledge that her old disreputable
friends were to be dropped, and that his old
reputable friends would drop both of them.
Also they knew that where they were known
it would be practically impossible to make
new reputable friends. Yet, with wealth and
all the . world before them, and any number
of countries to choose from, where the Second
Mrs. Tanqueray’s past was a secret, they
chose to stay in England and groan themselves
miserably to death in isolation. The fall of Iris
was at least logical. She was a good woman,
whose goodness, though she didn’t know it, was
really based on the, fact that she had .-several
thousands a year. She tried to be good on £l5O
a year, and failed. She might have been good on
£l5O a year if the offer of a large income for being
bad hadn’t been constantly before her—but it was
constantly before her, and she took it. Then she
tried to negotiate for being good again with
another man on a decent income, because she pre-
ferred being good on a decent income to being bad
on one—but the other man would have none of
her. And between two stools she fell to the
ground, for through that luckless negotiation she
lost her chance of being rich and bad, and was
turned out to be bad and poor. The world is
full of people : (1) Who are conventionally good
and rich ; (2) who would be conventionally good
if they were rich ; (3) who are poor and conven-
tionally good because they never got a chance to
become rich by being bad. Iris fell out of Class I.
and into Class 11. through her solicitor going off
with her money, and she fell in a strictly logical
fashion. Her final disaster came about through
not fully realising how much better it suited her
to b 9 bad and have boots than to be virtuous with-
out them—in her failure to make the best of her
boots and her sin. Which was also logical. Any
number of people are built that way.
** * *
Bland Holt’s Lyceum drama, “ With Fly-
ing Colours,” continues to hinge its varied plot on
the incident of the man who is shot in the back of
the head and then is supposed to have committed
suicide. These fancy methods of felo de te are
rather favored on the stage, though in real life the
coroner is inclined to look suspiciously on the
corpse that shot itself in the back with a
gun at long range, or slew itself with an axe,
or cut off its own head and buried it, or practised
other fancy arts of self-destruction. The lighter
incidents of the drama are not so frequent as in
previous plays, but are just as good when they
come, notably the scene where the village (one
day every year) drives its time-worn vegetable-
cart and its ancient donkey through Bland Holt’s
cottage to maintain its ownership of the land as a
right-of-way. Considering the large amount of
common land that has been filched into big estates
this last century or two, the village ought to be
driving its ancient donkey through mostly every-
thing in England at present.

Mary Janes to the registry offices. Unfortunately

The approach of Melba had the effect,
sfemmgly, of stimulating Melbourne’s faith in
‘ Trebelli” Dolores. Audiences for the French
artist’s farewell recitals were very large, and the
enthusiasm went a shade higher than ever. At
each of the recitals Mdme. after singing about 14
times got vehemently encored for her last song.
The fact was Melbourne had been warned to
moderate its wild expectations re the coming
prima-donna, and make the most of its oppor-
tunities to hear an incomparable Dolores at
ordinary concert prices. The sweet-faced French
woman may not have all the brilliancy of Melba’s
voice, but as an artist and an entertainer she is a
more satisfying “treat” than the less versatile
“star” vvould dream of attempting to be. To
sing a varied assortment of items—ranging from
florid operatic numbers to quaint comedy ballads—
is not Melba’s business, of course. But if she
were set such a task at the conclusion of a long
tour, when all the glamour of novelty had left her,
would she be able to go on feeding and strength-
ening the admiration of her audience as Dolores
does ? People who ought to know what they are
talking about say that if the two accomplished
artists started from “scratch” trillful Melba
would make the running at first, but demure little
Dolores would forge ahead after the seventh
song.
** * *
Last Saturday was the seventh night of
“Cyrano de Bergerac ” at Melbourne Bijou, yet
the financial prospects of that foreign-looking
novelty had hardly improved during the week.
The man in the street is disinclined to take an
interest in a high-class, sombre play relating to a
French literary crank whose somewhat legendary
career terminated 250 years ago. Australia, thus
far, has not heard of Cyrano de Bergerac. Any
story of the Gascon dreamer and his exaggerated
nose needs time to soak into the prevailing ignor-
ance, and even a knowledge of that long-deceased
litterateur doesn’t make it easy for a casual visitor
to comprehend the meaning of the Bijou show
prior to the interview between De Bergerac and
his Roxarie. A leaflet explaining the passing
events of each act, especially the Ist, 2nd and sth
acts, should be issued with the programme.
4 s 4■ an unenlightened inquirer gets to
the Bijou at, say, five minutes to eight, and
finds the curtain has been up for ten minutes.
The . complex action of the scene mystifies him.
Then he fails to discover a connection between
the incidents which fogged him at the start and
the mam story which begins to unfold its beauty
in a pastrycook’s shop. As a matter of fact every
little happening throws a kindly side-light on the
central character, but Melbourne of late years has
objected to the mental strain of looking for
importance in little things. Hence the necessity
for publishing a key to the cleverness of Rostand’s
famous work. The programme with its list of 46
characters offers no suggestions re their identities.
It suffices for the few critics who have read the
book of the play, but it doesn’t even distinguish
between fugitive figures that crop up in the first
or second act only—the mummers on the side
stage, the drunken lampoonist, Madame
Ragueneau and her musketeer, &c.—and others
that follow the course of the plot.
Henry Lee’s impersonation of Cyrano is a
really good treatment of a great part. The man
must be represented as a brilliant hero in every-
thing but personal appearance, yet the nose that
casts a dark shadow over his life must not be a
preposterous appendage. The actor has to
sacrifice a certain amount of De Bergerac’s
repulsiveness in order to preserve the play from
laughter. Therefore, not being very ugly, he
must not be very young and gay, otherwise his
yearning for another fellow’s girl would not look
sufficiently hopeless. Henry Lee fills the difficult
bill as a grave and somewhat dyspeptic hero,
grown old and soured before his time through
brooding over the fact that stupider men have
more charm for the ladies. There’s a heavy side
to the noble character, and Lee makes the most
of that side, because his style favors it. Some-
times he might be a finer actor than he is, but,
taking one time with another, his performance
commands great respect for its pathos, passion,
honesty, and refinement of theatrical art.
** # *
King Charles, and the girl who relinquished
the oranges of virtue for the peaches of vice, are as
popular as ever at Melbourne Princess’s. They
ought to be matters for high moral loathing, but
somehow their sin is received with great applause.
The first appearance of Sweet Nell after her fall
from the unforbidden-fruit trade, excites a burst
of admiration on account of her expensive clothes,
and when the King comes to gaze on the beauteous
ruin he has effected, the large audience evi-
dently mistakes His Gracious Nibs for a philan-
thropist, and greets him accordingly. There’s
no room in Paul Kester’s play for a per-
son of unimpeachable respectability. One of
the ladies, Olivia Vernon, is supposed to be
innocent and “right-thinking,” nevertheless her
sense of decorum doesn’t get a shock when she
finds that friend Nell ought to figure as co-
respondent in a royal divorce case. The fair
Olivia (her voice is not more manageable than of
yore) makes no pretence of feeling sorry for the
“ creature ” who went astray with the Lord’s
Anointed. Doubtless she would shudder at a
fallen sister in a state of poverty and shame, but
inasmuch as the wages of Nell’s indiscretion are
purple and fine linen, the Lady Olivia sees nothing
to complain about. The punishment fits the
crime, she thinks. '
** * *
The revived “ Runaway Girl ” is filling in
the present week at Her Majesty’s, not because
Melbourne wanted the particular Girl more, but
because it wanted the “Circus Girl” less. All
the same a stronger company might make the
“ Circus Girl ’" look the brighter comedy of the
two. As regards the moderately pleasing spinster
from the Convent of St. Pierre, there is nothing
unkind to be said. Her young man has a new
sister this time—Cecilia Ghiloni instead of Grace
Palotta—and Miss Cecilia is a nice, bouncing
blonde with an estimable singing voice. She
smiled too copiously on Saturday. Also her accent
suffered from recollections of her brave endeavor
to represent a vulgar, old, fat woman in the
previous piece, but she was a promising Miss
Ghiloni in spite of those defects. The new Car-
menita (Miss Rose Hamilton) conveyed a notion
of how the part of a middle-aged cockney gipsy
ought to be filled, consequently brother Tam
looked all the better for the change. Harold
Thorley was the tuneful minstrel addicted to
making a bee-line for the wine-shop, and the rest
of the company repeated their former appearances
with the exception that Miss Katie Milner got
there for the first time as the bride of the elderly
insectarian who fishes for butterflies. “Robin
Hood,” the very musical opera heard in Sydney
some three years ago, will be introduced to Mel-
bourne on Saturday (16th). The manager announ-
ces important special engagements for “Robin
Hood,” including a new tenor from America.
** * *
At the Opera House the programme is
mostly as it was a week ago, the latest arrival
being one Professor Fred Harcourt, who carries
the title of “England’s Great Court Magician.”
For some misguided reason “magicians” and
“jesters” are nearly always represented as
escapees from an unknown Court. The Fred in
this case is a fairly skilful and unassuming person
whose quickness of hand would doubtless deceive
the royal eye if he were employed at a court, but
seeing that a special illusionist by appointment to
Edward VII. would be none the smarter for
having converted the monarchical mouchoir into a
tea spoon, or vice-versa , the word Court confers no
extra dignity on the performer.

its baptismal style of “Jane” for “The Wrong

name.

-* * * *
Aii illustration of the danger of trifling
with Cyrano de Bergerac was given at the Bijou
last Saturday. In the first act of the play,
Cyrano enters the theatre at the Hotel de Bur-
goyne, and peremptorily orders a mummer to
leave the stage, and the mummer duly flies from
the duellist’s wrath. When the final act was in
progress on Saturday, a man in the Bijou stalls
started to poke ridicule at Cyrano’s death-scene
by to imitate either the actor or Miss Wal-
dorf. Whereupon Henry Lee, as Cyrano, brought
down the.house by ordering the interjector to be
chucked out. The intrepid Gascon stopped the
play until a “ furore of applause ” announced the
expulsion of- the inane offender. Then Lee de
Bergerac took up his part again and died like a
masterful manager.
** * *
‘‘The Worst Woman” continues her
career of profitable sinfulness at Melbourne Royal.
Miss Duggan gets loyal assistance from Mi\ Wil-
lard and Mr. Inman in the principal parts. Bert
Bailey and his circus are pleasing breaks in the
horrors of the show. Also, J. H. Nunn gives a
good picture of the W.W.’s rapacious old wreck
of a father. The ancient commits forgery, bleeds
his daughter of her husband’s bank-notes, and
revels in inebriation on the proceeds; but he
draws the line of criminality at setting fire to
Miss Guildford. Which suggests that the last
remnant of a Nonconformist conscience still lurks
under the old man’s waistcoat.

in it.

So far as is known the deficits for last financial
year were these :
New South Wales (partly disguised by
treating rents of unpaid-for pro-
perties as revenue) about .. .. £350,000
Queensland 431939
Victoria ... 437’,611
South Australia „ „ ... .. 222,315
Tasmania .. ~ 44’279
, £1,486,144
Westralia has a surplus of £198,023 which would have
been a deficit of about £4518 if the State had stood on
the same footing as its neighbors, and had not been
allowed to collect inter-State customs duties. Four of
these deficits at least were foreseen and premeditated.
The Governments knew that, when their people were
relieved of taxation by the abolition of border duties, a
corresponding amount of taxation must be levied in other
directions to make good the loss—and not one of them
moved.
The Queensland Government proposes to add
the deficit quietly to the public debt, which will make
about £2,600,000 of deficits so treated. For next year it
is proposed to save £IOO,OOO by sweating the public
service; £57,000 by increased stamp, totalisator, and
port taxes ; and .£IOO,OOO by an income tax, which latter
is anew impost in Queensland. It is a very sweeping tax
in some respects. Males (and presumably females) under
21 and females whose incomes are less than £l5O are to be
exempt. Every male over 21 whose income is £l5O or
less is to pay £l, which gathers in even the smallest adult
male wage-earner. Oyer £l5O the rate is to be 6d. in the
£ when the income arises from personal exertion, and Is.
when it is derived from property. These imposts are
expected to yield about £257,000 in all, and by hard
economy, and a liberal use of loan moneys for revenue
purposes, a £IO,OOO surplus is expected next year. If
that can be kept up for 43 years it will just wipe out last
year’s shortage.
Victoria anticipates that next year’s deficit may
reach the appalling sum of £650,000. The hideously in-
capable Shiels proposes to cut £20,000 off the vote for
charities ; to save £75,000 by reducing the public service ;
£60,000 by cutting down railway expenditure; £50,000 off
the municipal endowment (which is, at least, a commend-
able idea, for the Local Government bodies should be
able, in most cases, to meet all their road and bridge ex-
penditure) ; and to pare £45,000 off general expenditure.
These savings make £250,000. Then he proposes to
“postpone” payment of £IOO,OOO, due on account of the
old deficit, which is simply adding that amount to the
public debt. Also to steal £28,000 from the Public Debt
Sinking Fund which Turner inaugurated. These all put
together make £376,000. And as a last resource, he says
he may later on propose £82,000 of new taxation. Even
if he does, he will only have really provided £332,000
towards an anticipated shortage of £650,000, for post-
poning obligations isn’t paying them. Asa weak, flabby,
hopeless man for a big crisis, Shiels about takes the cake.
A genuine all-round land tax like that of N.S.W. would
fill the bill, but it takes a fighting man, not a boneless
object with its tail in the dust, to argue with the House
of Landlords on that subject.
* * * •
The Tasmanian Treasurer reckons the next deficit
under present conditions at £122,000. He proposes to
cut down expenditure by £33,000 ; to raise £4OOO by
assessing the land for land-tax purposes at something
nearer to its real value ; £7OOO by a graduated land tax ;
£25,000 by increased railway rates ; £IO,OOO by probate
duties ; £SOOO by a receipt-stamp tax ; and £50,000 by
his new house-tax —total, £134,000. This looks good
enough, but the heavy expenditure of loan money on un-
productive works, and the constant increase in the inte-.
rest-bill that has to be met out of taxation, steadily eats
away every prospect of better things in Tasmania.

Very extensive alterations and improvements
have just been completed at the engineering works of
Mephen Ferguson, Melbourne. The principal item of
interest is a new Electric Welding plant, which is now in
use welding the steel-plates required in the manufacture
of the patent spiral riveted pipes which are in such large
demand for water-supply, mining and other purposes.
The new method of welding is giving very satisfactory
results, and is well worth seeing.*

The antidote to cyanide poisoning, as supplied
by Ernest de Beer, 439 Bourke-street, Melbourne, has
been approved by the Victorian Mines Department as
being the only real antidote known. All who use
cyanide should keep one or two of these on hand. The
price is 10s. each.*

* * * •
Electricity in mining is being more extensively
adopted every day. The Austral Otis Engineering Com-
pany, of South Melbourne, the Australian representatives
of Ganz and Co., have just installed at the Great
Southern Consols mine, Rutherglen, a complete,plant for
the underground haulage, doing away with the old costly
system of trucking. The work is now done by electric
locomotives at a much reduced cost; the whole of the
drives and surface works are also lighted from the same
installation. This company has also supplied electric
pumping plants to the Sons of Gwalia mine, and probably
the largest electric pumps in the world for the Charlotte
Plains and New Havilah mines at Maldon. Having
secured the services of a well-known European expert,
they are now prepared to advise, and contract for any
electric service such as lighting, pumping, hauling,
traction, rock-drilling, or the generation and transmission
of power by the latest and most approved methods, and
invite inquiries.*

The Victoria Life and General Insurance
Cos. (Melbourne) does no new business nowadays, and has
done none for many years. It is quietly winding up and
waiting for its last policy-holder to die, when the re-
maining assets will be divided among the shareholders.
It and the Australian Alliance and the Adelaide Life Cos.
went out of active life business under the impression
that the day of proprietary concerns was over in this
country, and now that their place has been taken by a
lot of new proprietary co.’s, mostly of much worse re-
pute, it seems a pity that they left the business. The
recent record of the Victoria Life and General:

contingencies. The shareholders of the three Victorias

It is satisfactory to note that the Victoria Insurance Cos.
is at last doing something to put its reserves on
a more satisfactory footing, and, though it seems to
have the hungriest crowd of dividend-eaters in Australia,
the div. hasn’t been immediately rushed up again to
45 per cent, regardless of other considerations. The
Victoria’s accumulations consist of reserve fund, £35,000 ;
contingency fund, £12,000 ; reinsurance reserve, £11,838 ;
and undivided profit, £10,250. The gross premium in-
come is £146,948 ; and the net premium income, £87,080.
According to conservative English ideas 40 per cent, of
the latter sum should be held as a reinsurance reserve, so
the Victoria’s reinsurance reserve should be about £35,000
instead of £11,838. The fact that many Australian com-
panies have no reinsurance reserve at all is no reason why
so enormously prosperous a concern as the Victoria should
have an inadequate one. This co. has had the big-divi-
dend craze in a most aggravated form; it once paid
100 per cent, for three years in succession. Asa result
its affairs now compare with those of 22 years ago as
follows:
1880. 1902. Decreas ®
Profit £71,093 £23,923 £47,170
Reserves .. .. 76,157 69,088 7069
Dividend rate .. .. 100 p.c. 27 p.c. 73 p.c.
„ (amount) .. £44,935 £13,481 £31,454
Gross Premiums .. 149,956 146,948 3,008
Interest and Rents .. 7,481 3,686 3,795
Some other insurance offices show a more remarkable
decline, but in most cases they faded through heavy
losses ; in the case of the Victoria the decline was wholly
through the big-dividend policy. In 1881 and 1882 this
co. actually took money from its reserves to keep up the
100 per cent, dividend, the year’s profits being insuffi-
cient ! It should now decide to keep its dividends down
to 20 per cent, until it has accumulated a reserve of
£200,000 in good State securities—one that will be worthy
of the leading (or what should be the leading) fire com-
pany of Australasia.

The Victoria General Insurance and Guarantee Co.’s
accumulations consist of reserve fund, £SOOO ; contin-
gency fund, £4OOO ; and undivided profit, £3280- There
is also an item, “ Estimated Outstanding Losses and Re-
insurance Fund, £2849,” and the portion of this which is
re-insurance fund ought to be included in the reserves,
but the two items being lumped together in this rather
remarkable fashion it is impossible to dissect them. Any-
how, the re-insurance fund of a co. with £10,500 net
premium income ought to amount to about £4OOO. It, is
stated that “ depreciation, if any, is allowed for in Con-
tingency Account,” so, as there seems a fair chance that
there is depreciation to provide for, the reserves are
possibly, on a true estimate, rather less than shown above,
instead of more. The turning-point in the fortunes of
the Victoria General was in 1896, when, being then only a
young office in its seventh year, it put up its dividend to
25 per cent., and took £l2 from its reserves to make up
the amount. In four out of the last seven years the big
dividend has been paid by encroaching on the reserves.
And the co., despite its splendid profits, is not a growing
one; its gross premium income in 1891 was £11,938, and
in 1902 it was £11,398 —a decline of £540.

The earnings are at the rate of £l7 12s. 7d. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders’ funds as at the commence-
ment of the year, or £25 19s. sd. per cent, on capital
alone. How these extraordinary earnings come about is
a matter that a good many other banks would be glad to
find out. Perhaps the “ruinous socialistic legislation”
of Maoriland is good for banks. Certainly in the old days
when the allegedly safe and reliable Tory party was in
charge of affairs over there, the National was a very small,
poor affair which just scraped a 5 or 7 per cent, dividend
together, and was glad when it could keep about £IO,OOO
of reserve fund intact.
The National Bank of New Zealand has
£2,732,951 of deposits, an increase of £179,696 during the
year. Its reserve of cash and State securities is £756,850,
equal to barely 23 per cent, on all liabilities to the public.
This is a better position than it showed last year ; still
the reserve isn’t a very solid one ; in the process of earn-
ing large profits the cash reserve appears to be kept quite
as low as it reasonably can be. But, taking it all round,
the National Bank of New Zealand looks quite unusually
prosperous. No bank in Australia or Tasmania shows
anything like such earning-power.

The Mercantile Mutual never makes any addition to its
reserve fund, which has stood for many years at £40,000.
When the profits accumulate they are added to capital,
consequently, though the dividend just paid is the
largest for many years, it doesn’t represent nearly as
much per cent, as the much smaller sum divided in 1893.
In 1892 the Mercantile Mutual had £15,000 paid-up
capital ; £40,000 reserve fund ; and £IOB2 undivided pro-
fit. It now has £30,000 paid-up capital; £40,000 reserve
fund ; and £9845 undivided profit. Presumably the capi-
talising process will continue till the paid-up capital
stands at £150,000, and the call liability is wholly wiped
out. The Mercantile Mutual has recently extended its
business to Melbourne, the result being that the gross
premiums have risen from £45,774 in 1900 to £55,427 in
1902. And, judging by the increased profits, it seems to
secure as good a class of business in Victoria as in N.S.W.

• * * *
N.S.W. railways and tramways will show a fine
large deficit for the year just closed. Gross revenue was
£174,990 more than at June, 1901 ; but expenses increased
by £303,682 ; so the real revenue showed a decline of
£128,692. The shortage in 1901, after paying interest,
was nearly £56,000. This year, with more interest to
pay, it will probably be £200,000.

The Malting Cos. op New South Wales is
now in its fourth year :
Divi- Carried to Preliminary
dends. Reserves. Expenses.
1899 £741
Loss. Deficit.
1900.. £735 Nil 1476
Profit.
1901.. £720 Nil £720 756
1902.. 1859 3) p.c. = £763 1096 340
Now, as last year, the co.’s largest asset reads “Land,
buildings, plant, machinery, and goodwill, £16,996.”
Concerning the italicised item three remarks may be
made :—(1) Goodwill is only a paper security and a delu-
sion ; (2) anew company which hasn’t commenced by
buying a ready-made business should have no goodwill
items, and how the Malting Cos. contrived to get one is a
mystery. In such a case it always suggests that somebody
received cash or shares without giving tangible value for
them, under which conditions this kind of entry is handy to
square the accounts with. (3) When there is a goodwill item
it should be separately stated, and the amount made
known. Also, it should be written off at the earliest
possible date. As the Malting Co.’s profits are increasing
handsomely, it should do something next year towards
putting this matter on a better footing.

* « * *
S.M. Herald, in its financial column (12/8/’O2),
expresses its wonderment that even third-rate English
cities like Nottingham can borrow money at a much lower
rate than any of the Australian States—especially, says
the Herald, as these cities are taxed much more heavily
than we are. Probably that is just the reason why they
can borrow so cheaply. Australia is getting to be known
as a place that is lightly taxed, because its politicians
would rather accumulate deficits, and steal the sinking-
funds, and borrow any amount of money and use it for
purposes that should be charged to revenue, than
raise more taxes and be honest. People now begin to
wonder if a time is coming when it will rather repudiate its
debts than pay more taxes—and when theyfind the Herald
always ready, no matter how bad the finances may be, to
urge that revenue be sacrificed in the interests of the im-
porters, they wonder still more. The difference between
a heavily-taxed city which is heavily taxed because it
pays off 'its debts systematically out of income, and a
lightly-taxed country which uses loans as revenue in
order to remain in that lightly-taxed condition, is shown
in the fact that Manchester can get money now at £2 10s.
9d. per cent., while N.S.W. pays £3 3s. 6d. per cent.
• •* *
Mr. William Cain, chairman of Cobar Chesney,
is as sanguine as ever as to the possibility of working it
at a profit as a self-dependent concern. In the trial run
of the concentration plant, made in April last, 420 tons of
ore produced 77) tons of concentrates containing by assay
1023 units of copper and 19oz. gold. Taking these figures
as a basis of calculation, Mr. Oain works out a statement
thus :—Or.: 1023 units of copper at 10s., £slllos. ; 19oz.
gold at £4, £76 ; total, £587 10s. Dr. : Mining and con-
centrating 420 tons of ore at 125., £252 ; smelting
77) tons concentrates at £2, £154 10s. ; balance,
£181; total, £587 10s. This would represent a profit
of about Bs. 7d. per ton of ore. It will be noted
that no allowance is made for loss in smelting. The
important question is : Can concentrates be smelted at
Cobar in reverb, furnaces for £2 per ton, inclusive of
realisation charges ?At Wallaroo the cost of producing
a ton of copper is over £6O, and as labor is dearer at
Cobar, transport-charges much heavier, and fuel certainly
not less costly, writer has doubts about cheaper work
being done. By a combination of blast and reverb, fur-
naces it is possible that costs might be brought below
that figure, but at present Mr. Cain seems to favor reverb,
furnaces only. On one point he is thoroughly sound :
i.e., that if the co. attempts any system of smelting it
must produce the finished metal. It would be courting
failure to produce matte for disposal to other co’s., whose
abnormal profits represent corresponding loss to the
producing co’s.
m * * *
White Cliffs opal field reckons it will have a
water-famine soon ; other places would reckon it had
been in the middle of one for a month. Every drop of
water has to be carted from 12 to 15 miles ; every bite of
horse-feed has to come from Broken Hill, 90 or 100 miles,
over a track which is itself a sandy desert, with half the
watering-places dry. Very few teams can get through at
all; those that do have to take out nearly as much feed
for the road (there and back) as they carry loading. The
price is £l2 a ton, and rising. As many of the opal-
gougers as can have cleared out; those that hang on
have been lately doing only fairly. The gem-expert,
whom the English co. which owns so much of the field
lately sent out, has resigned and left; and the future of
the co. looks more hopeless than ever.
* •* »
The half-yearly report of Great Cobar Cos. dis-
closes that three shareholders have begun a suit in Equity
to prevent the agreements recently adopted by the vast
majority of holders being carried into effect. These
agreements provide for the settlement of the previous
Equity suit, and the granting of alternative options to
purchase the mine. The balance-sheet also reveals that,
although the tribute syndicate was supposed to have
bought the mine in Dec., 1900, for £315,416, it
has paid only £88,106 to date. It is true that an Equity
suit was promptly instituted with a view to stopping the
sale, but, as an ordinary business transaction, it might
have been assumed that the purchasers would either pay
the money to be held in trust, or continue to pay the
royalties provided for in the original tribute agreement,
these, also, to be held in trust pending the settlement of
the law-suit. But neither has been done, so that,
apart from the instalment of about one-fourth of the
purchase-money, the co. has not received any revenue
for over a year and a-half. It is rumored that the second
tribute syndicate, which had an agreement to date from
theexpiry of theJßeid-Longworthterm,and which received
£40,000 compensation for the cancellation of that con-
tract, has received notice of an intended suit to recover
the amount. Law seems to have ineffable charms for
some people.

Now is boom time, and the Charters Towers
brokers are stacking cash. Recent developments in the
Brilliant Extended have filliped its neighbors. The
“ Way Leaves Bill,” passed last session, has removed to a
great extent the obstacle of freehold rights which retarded
work in several claims. Plant’s new shaft, which is ex-
pected to cut the Day Dawn reef at 2400 ft., has been
started, and the presence of good stone in the lowest
levels of the Day Dawn g.m. (the most westerly claim
working on that line), opens fair prospects for the success
of the deep shaft. Last-mentioned mine is crushing well,
and paying 6d. div. regularly. Several pups have been
thrown into the pool to sink or swim, and so far all have
floated, but whether continual calls will knock them out
or not time alone can tell. The truth of the “ Horse-
shoe” theory will be tested now, as leases have been
pegged out and floated for a mile outside its recognised
western limits, ending with the Great Britain and Day
Dawn, and the Ruby No. 1 South on the S.-E.. Writer
shrewdly suspects that Day Dawn was tacked on to Great
Britain to give it a flavor, as in the old days it was Great
Britain pure and simple. Ruby has been crushing fairly
well for some time, but has been contemptuously ignored
because it was “outside the circle.” A year ago paid-ups
could be bought for Is. ; now there are buyers at 7s.
* ** *
Smith’s Creek (N.Q. tin) has been the finest
gambling stock in Australia for some months. At first
Adelaide had the game to itself; then Melbourne joined ;
now Sydney. In Adelaide there have been well-defined
bull and bear parties ; only lately, the bulls having over-
sold, they changed places for a while with the bears
and knocked the market down to £l. Then Capt.
Warren’s report came along and picked it up again.
(Capt. Warren, by the way, is a very fine miner ; but it is
quite easy to attach too much importance to his opinion
of a tin proposition.) It is unfortunate for the reputa-
tion of the co. that three big spec.-ing brokers
should be directors ; the knock-out of the market just
before Warren’s favorable report was given to the public
smelt bad if it wasn’t. The co.’s first meeting was held
August 11. For it manager Potter made some interesting
estimates. He has two shafts down 100 ft. apart, and
drives proving 50ft. more. Trenches and shallow work-
ings on other shows have proved, he says, 250 ft. more
ground ; but to his 150 ft. he adds 50ft. each end ; adds
25ft. to his proved depth ; takes the average width as
20ft. and the average value as 8 per cent, black tin ; adds
50,000 tons of alluvial 2 percent, stuff ; and works out the
lot as containing £303,000 with tin at £IOO, or £490,000
with tin at £l3O. Costs, he says, will be covered by 1 per
cent. —so low because the stuff is very soft, requires
no stamps, and is easily dressed. Writer doesn’t pretend
to say how much this estimate is worth. One thing most
certain, though, is that the ore-body (whatever it is, lode
or pocket) is very irregular. In a few feet it changes
from 40 per cent, to 1) per cent, ore, and in width from
25ft. to 10ft. It would be only reasonably safe to calcu-
late the value of such a body when the ore had been
blocked out—when, anyhow, at least three sides had been
exposed. This has not been done in this case. The
estimate therefore looks like a guess, and the stock still
looks like a gamble, even though a dozen men report in
favor of the mine. Financially the co. is in a moderately
easy way. It has 100,000 shares, 21,985 of them unissued ;
£7416 unpaid instalments of purchase money on the last
redistribution (nearly all this has been paid since the
balance-sheet was issued); £5964 cash, and no creditors to
speak of. Apparently, however, nothing has yet been
paid towards the 100-ton plant which is under order.
Captain Warren’s report had not, at time of
writing, £een completed ;it will not be with the directors
till late in Aug. Meantime he writes that his estimate of
the value of the lode is 4 per cent.—and manager Potter’s
is 8 per cent. ! If Warren is right, what becomes of the
money-value estimate of £300,000 odd ? For, of course,
this would reduce the value by more than one half :in the
one case (assuming it takes 2 per cent, to pay) there
would be 2 per cent, for profit ;in the other case there
would be 6 per cent. Also, if we were to lop off from the
manager’s estimate the value of the ore which he only
assumes exists (the 25ft. below the 100 ft. level and the
50ft. beyond the ends of the drives) there would be less
than £IOO,OOO left. Potter may be right; Warren may be
right. It is at present a guess, a gamble.
• * * *
There is a Yes-No flavor about Captain War-
ren’s telegraphic reports on Smith’s Creek tin-mine,
N.Q. The first message read: “Report is favorable.
Average width of lode, 20ft. Average assay value of ore
in the mine, 4 per cent, tin oxide. Assay from dump
average, 5 per cent, tin oxide. Mine is payable. Will
probably turn out good mining property. Furthei
developments necessary.” Evidently he was wired to for_
something more definite, and he sent this cautious reply :
“Mine highly payable at present. Further development
is necessary to prove its permanence.” The full report
will probably show some artistic beating about the bush.
The practical man will ask why.is it that the assays are
returned in “ tin oxide” and not in “ metallic tin ?” Is
it because 2‘B and 3 - 5 per cent, respectively would look
too small ? Smith’s Creek has been boomed on reports of
from Bto 10 per cent, value in “metallic tin.” No won'
der that shares had a slump when Warren’s “ oxide ” as*
says were reported.
* * * *
Brilliant Deep Levels (C.T.) being resuscitated.
Old co. sunk a vertical shaft 2550 ft. to catch the Brilliant
reef on its underlie. The lode was cut at 2349 and 2413 ft.
level, but was of low grade. New co. proposes to sink on
the underlie from the 2413 ft. level. It is anticipated that
the rich pay chute now being worked in the Brilliant
Extended will be cut in from 400 to 500 ft. of sinking.
50,000 new shares of 10s. each being issued at 3d. on
application and 3d. on allotment, and the balance in
monthly calls not exceeding 3d. This is a laudable enter-
prise and a perfectly legitimate flotation, seeing that the
old shareholders are to get nothing more than shares, the
full capital value of which is just about what they have
spent on the property (£70,000). The only objection
which can be urged against the scheme is that it is too
liberal in the method of raising the money. In such a
brisk market as now exists at the Towers, and with every
man having a fulness of faith in the deep ground, it would
surely have been as easy to raise Is. per share at the out-
set as 6d. With £2500 to start with, and 3d. calls to
follow, there would hardly have been a risk of the co.
being stranded for funds when the inevitable reaction
takes place.
* * * *
Two years ago, when Jack Want was in England
and was badly wanted in Sydney to assist in the Anti-
Federal fight, it was cabled that he was quite indifferent
to local politics, as he had just made a big pile out of a
Queensland mine. That mine was Dottswold Freehold,
and Jack had the melancholy duty of performing the
funeral obsequies of the show on Tuesday last. As chair-
man of the co. he had to notify that all work had been
suspended owing to lack of funds, contributing share-
holders having declined to pay any more calls, and the
directors were not disposed to incur further responsi-
bility. He also stated that negotiations for the sale of the
mine in England were in abeyance.

“ M.” casts a pessimistic eye on the Briseis co.:
“ During 1901 the Briseis Tin Mines raised 109 tons of tin,
worth £12,247 (average per ton, £112). The cost of
winning this tin was £15,527, of which £2262 was London
expenditure. Four directors cut up £llsO a-year among
them, and the secretary gets £450. The co. owed its
bankers £19,487, and, as eight (8) months have elapsed
since, it is likely this loan has grown stouter. No infor-
mation is given re the cost of the race, which is 30 miles
long, though it looks as though it had cost £51,752 eight
months ago. One thing is quite certain from the balance-
sheet, the co. must raise more capital, and dividends are
hull-down on the horizon.”

Coronation in London gave about a page of cabled
matter rubbish, disconnected as a narrative,
Miculons in its pretentious efforts at fine writing.
Ims bit, for instance, following a detailed descrip-
tion of the carpets in the Abbey (which might
naturally have concluded with the name of the
enterprising firm,” etc., etc.)
The spectacle of the two transepts was the most brilliant
piece of color in the whole pageant. The flashes of
diamonds m the Peeresses’ gallery, especially on the
trains and skirts of the Ladies-in-Waiting, when they
arrived at the foot of the platform, were like the ripple of
sunshine on rough water, or the scintillation of dewdrops
in the morning sunlight.
Then follows a trifling detail about a couple of
artists being stowed away out of sight in some
corner. Why hadn’t these “great dailies” of
Australia the enterprise and the discretion to put
up £lOO or £2OO among them to get a descriptive
!^ 1Q .f* rs fc flight to send them a graphic
5000 words, instead of chucking at the public a
medley of silly high-faluting, furniture catalogues,
millinery detail, “faldstools” and fal-de-rals, in
big type, eked out with many cross-heads ? The
daily papers cater for a public whose literary
appetite they imagine to be on a par with the
idea of luxury of the lucky miner, who was going
‘to have bacon and eggs three times every
blooming day, by gosh he was.” Perhaps they
are right.

AN EASY ONE .
“ What would you do if you drew the first horse in the Melbourne Cup?" “Nothing! "
Are visions about ? The Ananias at the
other end of the cable has reported that King
Edward is going to receive Boer generals
Botha, De Wet, and De la Key, and possibly
others, as a special mark of honour, and the
utterly disgusting news hasn’t made one Jingo
arise in protest. For the be3t part of three years
we were assured, on the best Jingo oath and
assurance, that the Boers (and especially two out
of these three particular Boers) were ruffians, liars,
cowards, oppressors, hypocrites, slave-drivers,
thieves, insulters of women, treacherous mis-
creants who assassinated their enemies under the
white flag artifice; also that they were cruel,
dirty, ignorant, lazy, uncouth, unlettered, and
given to living on a mud floor. We had
these facts on the evidence of the IVEelbourne
Stock Exchange, of the church generally, of
the Tasmanian papers, of the people who
were drunk on Mafeking night, of the
Legislative Councils, of the sweaters and nigger-
labor crowd, and of almost every respectable
citizen who ever ran a fraudulent bank or floated
a “cat” mine, and diddled the poor out of their
savings, and then complained of their lack of
“thrift.” And now we are told that Edward
VII. is going to receive, as a special honor and
mark of esteem, the leaders and representatives of
this alleged horde of ruffians, and to extend to them
his sacred hand. Nothing is said about them being
purified by the Archbishop of Canterbury before
the meeting, or disinfected by the royal physician,
or searched for white flags and concealed weapons.
And everybody is quite calm. Does this mean
that the Jingo crowd has been lying all this while ?
Or does it mean that Edward VII. is such a low
character himself that it doesn’t matter whom or
what he consorts with ? Or what does it mean ?
* * * *
The discovery that the brightest jewels in
the British crown are made of paste recalls
an incident in Daudet’s realistic romance,
“ Kings in Exile,” the novel which seems to have
given the cue for Anthony Hope’s “Prisoner of
Zenda. ” When the dethroned Queen of Illyria
makes up her mind to spoil her worthless hus-
band’s crown to pay the household exes, she puts
it in the hands of Elysee, the tutor of her infant
son, and asks him if he knows his way to a likely
pawnbroker. “The crown ! For 600 years it has
been in.the royal house of Illyria. Kings have
died, rivers of noble blood have flowed in its
defence. Now it must help us to live. It is all
we have left. ... Some of the stones must be
prised out —the biggest.” So Elysee, the fanatic
royalist, groaning over the desecration of the
crown, prises the big stones from their setting,
and takes them next day to “uncle,” returning
with the news that they are all false. H.M. the
King had been the first robber of his own blessed
diadem.

“ The Australasian Coronation contin-
gents,” writes The Bulletin’s London correspon-
dent, “have bad a particularly decomposed period
up to now. Camped in an out-of-the-way hole
like the Alexandra Palace—their officers are wise
enough to flee it like an orderly-bin—not extrava-
gantly fed nor entertained, their coronating is by
no means all slieoak and quoits. Half the time
the grounds are a quagmire, and at others merely
the resort of the tired trollop. These shamelessly
haunt the ‘Black’camp, half-a-mile away, to such
an extent that a hundred of them were found at
midnight in the tents of Dyaks, Wei-hai-weians,
Nigerians, Malays, &o.”
* * * •
Mb. Kembla colliery disaster calls atten-
tion to one of the great mysteries of chemistry,
how the difference of an atom of an inherently-
harmless substance in a compound can convert it
from comparative innocuousness to greatest dead-
liness. One part of carbon with two parts of
oxygen is a mixture which life will tolerate ; one
part of carbon with one part of oxygen kills the
blood in a flash. It is as if one spoonful qf whisky
with two lumps of sugar was all right, but one
spoonful of whisky with one lump of sugar was
sudden death. The analogy, of course, is not
perfect. There is a vast difference between a
mixture and a compound. But what the differ-
ence, and why the difference, chemistry doesn’t
really know, and will never know, perhaps, until
the day that the discovery is made that there is
no such thing as dead matter on earth, and that
the chemist’s atom of gas is a first cousin to the
microbe.
Meanwhile there is an entrancing problem for
th© chemist on the edge of solution—“the fixing
of air nitrogen.” With that solved on economical
lines, there would be no such thing as barren land.
Yankee chemists think now that with the aid of
electricity they can make nitrates direct from the
air, performing in the laboratory what Nature
does in her way when the careful farmer plants a
crop of cow-pea (which draws nitrogen from the
air) and ploughs it in. A query then : If this
succeeds, and the air can be robbed in wholesale
fashion of its nitrogen, how will that loss be sup.

* * » *
This, from a locomotive fireman, is printed
for what it may be worth :
Writer adds his testimony as to the explosiveness of
Mt. Kembla coal. When using it dry and powderv, as it
often is, I have sometimes lost a lot of whiskers, eye-
brows, &e. It ignites and explodes almost before leaving
the shovel. The fire is also terrifically hot when the
coal bunker contains “ Kembla.” Was much surprised at
reading that naked lights were used in the mine.
* * * *
The unexplained Mt. Kembla colliery
explosion raises serious practical questions. Before
it occurred, Mt. Kembla mine was regarded by
all who knew it as the safest in Australia, perhaps
in the world. Now it is said that no colliery is
safe. What was it that exploded—coal-gas or
coal-dust ? No one seems to know. The mine
was never regarded as either gassy or dusty. It
looks very much as if the only chance of abso-
lutely safe coal-getting lies in making the use of
safety-lamps universal and compulsory in all
collieries, whether known to be dusty or
gassy. After what has happened the use of
naked lights in collieries seems far too
dangerous to be longer permitted, The safety-
lamp gives such a dim and inadequate light
that the miner will not use it if lie can help it:
and now there is a fortune awaiting the man who
can invent a practicable electric incandescent lamp,
light enough to be carried on the miner’s hat,
and capable of going for eight hours without
attention or possibility of failure short of actual
breakage.
* * • *
Despite its occasional man-eating catas-
trophes, coal-getting is not the most deadly form
of mining in N.S.W. For 10 years its butcher’s
bill has averaged 1*54 per 1000 employees ; the
Broken Hill bill is over 2, and was for several years
2‘4. Copper-mining kills very little under 2 •
gold-mining (in N.S.W.) under 1, as against 1*39
per 1000 in Yic. during the last 20 years.
* • * *
If a miner has to be killed, his widow is
luckiest if he dies along with 30 or 40 others in
some sensational way that pricks the printer to
get out his big black type; for then,'and only
then, will she get a dole of charity from outside.
The great public has been moved by a pro-
per sympathy (some of it practical, too) for the
Kembla sufferers. It gave hardly a thought,
and possibly not a penny, to those depending on
the 200 odd miners who, during the last four years
have died in N.S.W. through a rock falling here’
and a rock falling there, in a way that didn’t
justify more than a bald two-line par. to the effect
that John Smith, who was injured last week by a
fall of earth, died yesterday. Over this far larger
number of plain John Smiths there hasn’t been a
thrill—but the little Smiths have been just as
hungry as if their fathers had filled the papers
when they died. When the hungry cry of these
little Smiths moved the miners’ members in the
Assembly to fight for the Miners’ Accident Relief
Fund, they did for the working miner a finer thing
than was, perhaps, ever done at one stroke for any
other single body of workmen in Australia.
• • * *
Miners from other collieries, who were
amongst the rescue parties at Mt. Kembla, show
an unwillingness now to enter their own mines.
Many of them, who had risked their lives tramp-
ing many weary miles in the death-trap, hesitate
to go into mines supposed to be quite safe.

“ There are now living at North Sydney,”

“ R.A.K.” (Melbourne) on “ living a
little way out”:—
We tried it (about ten miles out, on a high road) for
two weeks and a holiday ; ’twas the holiday that smashed
us up. As dawn was breaking we got up, and dispensed
hot water to several young chaps who were camping near
by, taking care of the paraphernalia of a big picnic going
to happen that day. From 8 till 12 the air was thick with
clouds of dust raised by various picnics making for the
country. (The Chinamen’s was most cheerful and
holiday-looking, they had such a lot of nice you Ma-
ladies in their van.)
A.t noon a small boy shouted at the top of a
shrill voice : “ I say, give us some onions, quick ;
our horse has the gripes.” Just as we got back
to dinner—another knock : “ Have you got any
■ axle-grease?” said a young woman. “Any fat
’ll do ! ” roared out a man in a cart outside the gate
Then we filled two or three dozen teapots and billies
with boiling water, and loaned a corkscrew and our
“Midget” cycling outfit, and bolted the gates and
doors, and had a peaceful hour, until we caught
sight of a large array of Sunday-school women and
children, with baskets and sticks, standing out-
side the fence looking at our apples.
Bounded out at once, and, after an excited de-
bate, they went away disappointed and grum-
bling. Stayed there all the afternoon, and in-
formed hundreds of astonished unbelievers that
our fruit wasn’t a part of the free show. We also
loaned some matches, a little tea, “ and a needle
and cotton to mend Ruby’s dress.” We loaned a
. newspaper to a grandfather, because he couldn’t
join ln the sports, and numberless little packets of salt,
mustard and sugar went on their way rejoicing.
Two ladies were accommodated on our couches, be-
cause the heat had made them faint, and then we
were full-up.
The picnics began slowly to return, but they
begged for candies in vain. Wheels broke down
outside our very gates, but we were not inte-
rested.. Even u hen the boy came for just one
more onion, he was sent empty away. Oh, it’s
grand living a little way out 1
For out-and-out misstatement it would be
hard to beat Premier Irvine’s assertion when
replying to the Vic. public servants on the
retrenchment question, that private employers all
round were either reducing wages or casting their
employees adrift altogether, it would be impos-
sible for Mr. Irvine to prove anything of the kind,
because it does not exist, wages being, in the
least progressive of trades, merely stationary,
whilst in the others they have been rising,
though slowly. But his speech was exactly
what mean employers wanted, and they will be
quick to avail themselves of the excuse given them
by the first Tory Premier Victoria has had in
recent years, to cut their employees to the bone.
And any general reduction of wages will be the
very worst thing that can happen to Victoria at
the present time of drought and high prices.
When the housewife grumbles about these
matters, it is only necessary to cut down
her husband’s pay a little to start a big flare
up, but anything like a general strike in Victoria
just now would cripple the State of Sliiels the
Raven for another ten years at least.
•», * »
One of the Melb. daily-paper offices, which
was in the forefront of the Kyabram agitation,
was so eager to follow the Irvine Govt’s retrench-
ment lead that on the very next day its bosses
hinted to some of their employees that they should
be prepared for an all-round reduction of screws,
and a cutting-down of the literary staff. Yet the
Melb. morning papers are more understaffed now
than ever before in their history, and the lives of
the hard-worked newsgetters have been rendered
doubly damnable by the fact that there are now
two Parliaments to deal with instead of one. At
one time each daily was represented in the State
Parliament by a corps of half-a-dozen experts; it
happens sometimes nowadays that less than that
number are distributed between the Federal and
local Parliaments to do the whole of the arduous
work, although doubled in quantity.
* » * *
The horrors of the middle passage on board
the old-time slaver have been fairly matched by
the experiences of the returning Australian con-
tingents in the Imperial transport Drayton
Grange. Close on 2000 men crowded into a ship,
only fit to carry half the number, and with in-
sufficient stores and medicines, shows what an
indecent hurry the S’African authorities are in to
get rid of their Australian allies. The war was
over, there.were to be no more farms burned,*no
more “drives,” where the “colonials” were
wanted to fill up gaps in the line. If kept longer
they would only eat their own heads off at ss. a
day, and might give trouble. It would be better
to ship them right away and have done with
them. All the arrangements showed reckless
haste. There was no room to sling the hammocks,
and the atmosphere below is described as “foetid.”
Men slept, packed like herrings, on the decks.
The hospital only had accommodation for 40
patients (2 per cent, of the total instead of the
normal 10 per cent.). When measles broke
out, 15 cases were put into the “ officers’ smoking-
room,” and then the already-overcrowded troop-
decks were further encroached on by putting up a
canvas partition. It is worth notice that not a
single case occurred among the officers, who had
not the decency to crowd themselves a little so as
to let the hospital overflow into the saloon. After
all, the officer only, exists for the soldier, not the
soldier for the officer, and it is scarcely less a re-
proach to the officers on board the Drayton
Grange to say that they escaped the infection
that prostrated their men than it would be to say
that they all came unhurt out of an action in
which many of their men had fallen. Both facts
might be susceptible of very simple explanation,
but they will bear explaining. In any case, the
next time the Empire wants help, it is to be hoped
that Australians will not forget the Drayton
Grange and the horrors of the middle passage, as
an example of the way the Empire shows its
gratitude.

* * * »
From a Bulletin correspondent who in-
spected the Drayton Grange at Melbourne :
One of the hospitals rigged up on board —and this not
the worst—was l(ift. by 9ft. by 9ft., and in to this cupboard 18
unfortunate invalids had to be jammed. Others of these
dens were so dark and foul that the doctors could never
see the faces of the patients, and could never remain
long in them if they hoped to keep thoir own health.
Men slept and ate in the same rooms, which it was
almost impossible to clean out for the mass of packed
humanity. Many had to doss on the decks, and
even on the unguarded, open roofs of horse-boxes.
There was no provision, in a four weeks’ voyage, for baths ;
men were lucky if they got a basin of water and room to
turn round and wash themselves. The supply of drugs
and medicines was disgracefully inadequate, and even the
primary rule of any transport service—the provision of
sufficient life-boats and belts —was calmly flouted. For
passengers and crew numbering over 2000 souls, the
Drayton Grange was provided with boat accommodation
for 955 ! In case of shipwreck or fire, half of those on
board would inevitably have had to drown.
* * * *
The military panjandrums who looked
after the Drayton Grange are the grandsons of
those who sent a consignment of wooden legs to
the British soldiers in the Crimea when they were
starving.

The indications that the death of W. H. L.
Ranken, owner of TinderrV Station, near Goul-
burn, N.S.W., was not a case of suicide but
murder, make vocal the ordinarily mute horror of
the Australian wilderness. How many bodies
similarly disposed of have not been exhumed !
How many men whose names are written in the
chronicles of the “lost” went down in unequal
combat !

Notwithstanding that “ things are very
bad ” in Melbourne, the recent exhibition at the
Vic. Artists’ Gallery scored record attendances.
On the last Saturday over 300 people paid a
shilling at the door. On the two Sundays when
local appreciation of art was able to express itself
gratis the free admissions ran into four figures—a
crushing protest against the Sabbatarianism which
keeps the National Gallery closed against its
owners, the Public, on their weekly holy-day.
The fact that each time more than 1000 people
availed themselves of the not-generally-known
opportunity to inspect these V. A. S. pictures
might be quoted as further evidence of Melbourne’s
fondness for free shows. But it happened that
the Society broke the Sawbath law by offering
catalogues for sale, and a fair proportion of the
visitors invested saxpence in a catalogue. Now
the person who buys a catalogue doesn’t wait for
Sunday in order to save a shilling.
* * * *
That Victorian girl who is charged with
the hideous cruelty of leaving her illegitimate
infant for two frosty nights in a paddock, was a
Sunday-school teacher, and had quite recently
received a testimonial from her fellow-teachers in
recognition of the good work she had done in
shoving the chariot along. The Sunday-school
has been receiving a number of advts. in Victoria
of late—including the episode of the young woman
teacher who insisted on sharing a room with the
elderly man superintendent at the hotel, whereby
that good but helpless person was so shocked that
he couldn’t sleep.
THE LOST CHORD.—NEW VERSION.
Paris Nesbit, K.C., was induced to drop
his prosecution, for Conspiracy, of the opposing
witnesses in the Adelaide divorce-trial. Symon,
on the other side, acting with great consideration,
suggested that Nesbit had, no doubt, acted from a
sense of his duties as a K.C.; but now he had
thrown up his brief in the principal case he might
leave the side-issue to others. Nesbit accepted
this suggestion, but next day he was back in the
Supreme Court proceeding against Homburg,
M.P., for refusing (as a J.P.) to grant a criminal
information against Chief Justice Way, Kingston,
and one or two doctors, the charge being that
these last had committed Nesbit to an asylum for
several weeks about four years ago, during an
attack of eccentricity. Way is now Lieut.-Gov.
of the State, and it is- a nice point whether crimi-
nal proceedings against him can even be com-
menced; but Nesbit’s knowledge of the law is un-
rivalled, and the state of his health does not
seem to affect it at all.
What a multitude of chances there are for
the men who go down to the sea in ships, and there
view the tempest, and the clam, and the
whelk, and the sea-serpent, and the St.
Elmo’s fire, and other wonders of the
deep! A few days ago an Italian
barque crawled anxiously into Glenelg
(S. A.). It had been burning for a period
variously described as seven days and
one month. The hatches were battened
down to keep the fire from breaking out.
The provisions couldn’t begot at; the
men were starving, and were also roost-
ing on the rail as much as possible
because the deck was so hot that it
blistered their feet. Yet Julius Caesar
and co. brought her into port. And the
wages for that kind of thing are about
12s. a week and hard biscuits.

In one district of Victoria there
is a place known as the “House of
Seven Twins.” It originally belonged
to J R , to whom came the first
couple of innocents. He sold to D
S , whose wife shortly presented
him a pair. They were followed by
A. L. T—— and family, who also found
twins. J R—r — next took up his
abode there —and was soon blessed
with a brace. He shifted, and the place
was then occupied by H F—— and
family, with the same result—twins.
It is at present owned by the parent of
the sixth set of twins born in the
house.
* * * *
The colored brother has a gift for
developing ing strange and unpleasant
diseases. The “sleeping sickness” is
the latest to play havoc in Africa (but
there are three cases under treatment
in London hospitals). A nigger falls
into a lethargy, takes no interest in life,
and usually passes out of it in a sort of
general paralysis. So far, it has attacked
natives only; but 20,000 of them are known
to have died in the last epidemic, and the
Royal Society is standing in with the Colonial
Office to send out experts to report. It is not so
long since bubonic plague, also, was supposed to be
fatal only to the colored person.
• * * *
One of many ex-cohentingenters, unem-
ployed in Melbourne, wails to the Age about the
Jingoes’ broken promises and winds up :
It is three months since the Aurania left Capetown and
still we cannot get any word about the back pay owing
to us, which in my own case is about £5O. It is hard now
that the work is done to return home and find our former
billets gone, and no pay available, and patriotism dead.
It is hard ! Let the younger generation remember
it when the next jingo epidemic attacks this
country—say, in about 10 years time.

The wide circulation of the portraits of
the two men suspected of the Shepherd-street
tragedy gives interest to the fact that last year
the N.S.W. police photographer prepared 44,914
portraits. And that is independent of the records
of the Prison Dept., which, however, is to some
extent giving up sun-pictures in favor of hand
impressions and skull measurements.
