People are amazed at the appearance of an
anti-R.C. lecturess in Melbourne Hibernian Hall,
but that building has fallen into the hands of the
Scotch, although Shamus Turlough O’Brallaghan
went over from Carrickfergus and peopled Scot-
land with his own hands, so he did. Archbishop
Carr is going to build the new St. Patrick’s Hall
up by the Cathedral, if possible.
After many years things have begun
to hum a little about the N.S.W. native
wine trade. Wine is adulterated, the
winegrowers say. The chief complaint
that men who want a drink in town make
is that the trade is adulterated—mixed
inextricably with another which generally
keeps out of sight till'the lamps are lit.
Three-fourths of Sydney and suburban
wine-shops—many of them run under the
names of well - known vineyards—would
make better sport for the Social Gimlet-
hole people than would the much-abused
“ private bars ” of the hotels.
An enthusiastic Frenchman, Henri Col-
line, who recently visited Hans Irvine’s
big vineyard at the Great Western, near
Ararat (Vic.), thus breaks out in Mel-
bourne Leader: —
A rich claret . . . claims attention at once by
its raciness, accompanied with that soft mellow-
ness which has been described by a French con-
noisseur as “it tastes like a yard of velvet.”
Unctuous, with a full body, but exquisitely grace-
ful and round, and having a broad aromatic
bouquet, it is a wine which, my countrymen
would say, you can drink without “ fatiguing ”
either head or stomach.
And then, further on:—
Who then dares pronounce an anathema upon
that beverage which received our Master’s marked
approval; that beverage which stimulates happy
thoughts, which banishes the material and en-
thrones the ideal, which brings solace and joy
to lessen the dark lines of sorrow and trouble
which have been the common lot of humanity
since the banishment from Paradise.
The Bulletin would like to be behind
the door or under the table when the
Y.M.C.A. and the W.O.T.U. discuss this.
As a big property-owner in Hobart,
“ Tattersall ” Adams (who says he will spend
£lOO,OOO locally in building-improvements within
three years) promises to upset a number of local
traditions, among others the ancient one of City
Council opposition to modern sanitation. “Tatt.”
maintains that the proposed underground-drainage
scheme must be carried out, and smiles at the
selfish fears of property-owners who see nothing
but ruin in sanitary improvement. Says “Tatt.” :
“If the work costs us 10 per cent, it will add 25
per cent, to the value of our property. That has
been my experience in Sydney, and it will be so
here.” Apropos, when Ben Tillett told Hobart
aldermen something of. municipal progress in
London, and urged that it might pay Hobart to
raise its rate to London figures (3s. 4d. to 9s. Bd.),
the City Fathers were so flabbergasted at the
proposal that only a hasty handing round of the
snuff-box saved several apoplectic fits.
Revised apothegm : The battle of Tugela
was lost on the ping-pong tables of Sandhurst.
THE SEARCH FOR THE CAPITAL.
One of the-drollest features of the Hall
divorce case (Adelaide) was the evidence of Law-
rence, the right-thinking gardener to the Hall
establishment. When Mrs. Hall asked Lawrence
whether he, like the other servants, was prepared
to accuse her of giddiness with the gentle shepherd,
he told her that he himself had seen her meet the
good man in the evening and thought it a very
serious thing—in fact, “second only to murder.”
Then Mrs. Hall asked Lawrence if he wanted to
make a scandal in the church, and the right-
thinking gardener replied with a solemn question—
“ Did she think Wilson (the shepherd) was a man
to get up in his church and preach about
the immortality of man and then go there
and act in that way.” It is a remarkable
fact that a gardener, or anybody else, can attend
church for half a century without discovering that
the Holy Scriptures testify to the immorality
rather than the “tality ” of man, and the giddiest
parson is the most naturally sympathetic ex-
pounder of their terewths. For instance, what
was there in the alleged canoodleism of shepherd
Wilson to disqualify him for notifying the
brethren that Solomon had 500 wives and 1000
concubines? Strict “propriety” of life, as the
term is understood nowadays, seems altogether
ridiculous in a man who professes reverence for
the prophets of old.
Bailes, M.L.A., has a Bill in hand to
amend the Vic. Wills Act in such a manner as
to guarantee a share of a dead man’s estate
to his widow. Bailes proposes, however,
that the widow is to have no share if,
during 12 months previous to the demise,
she has been guilty of conduct that would
have entitled her husband to a divorce.
This clause points out the method of
attack for those who may desire to rob
the widow of her mite.

A man was assaulted at a Dubbo church
by an irate R.C. worshipper for keeping his
head covered during service. Had it been a
synagogue he might have been punched by some
Hebrew Mendoza if he had kept his head un-
covered. In a Mahomedan mosque trouble would
ensue if he kept his boots on. Divine fashions
seem to vary like human ones, and all depends on
the peculiar taste of the presiding deity.
* * * *
“ A.C.H.” : Re a recent B. par advocating
the substitution of male for female domestic labor:
After having tried a succession of haughty, in-
different, lazy and dirty “generals,” I obtained
the services of a lad of 18 as a substitute for Mary
Jane, and up to date am perfectly satisfied with
the change. After a few lessons the boy learned
to scrub and wash with surprising cleanliness,
putting more “elbow grease’’into the performance
than any of my other helps. Of course it seems a
bit queer at first to dispense with the familiar
draggle-tail, but one very soon gets used to it,
and the boy does as he is told—a very refreshing
change after the usual kitchen lady’s “sass.” In
his spare moments he has no objection to digging
or wood chopping—occupations that Mary Jane
would scorn.
* • * *
There was a rather droll correspondence
in the Argus last week. One venerable gentleman
writes about the impudence of girls. Another
venerable rushes to the defence. Here’s his gist:
“ You old stupid ! Seeing your white hairs, the
girls only treat you as an old woman. They ain’t
afraid of you. Ask the young chaps ? They find
the girls more modest and retiring than ever.
That’s woman’s proper game.”

Chief Secretary Jenkins (S.A.) states that
the reason why suspected lepers among the
Alligator River blacks are not placed in the
lazaret near Port Darwin is that the last two
jnmates committed suicide. A cheerful place !
William M'Culloch, the hideously “ loyal ”
Victorian who managed to get away to the
Coronation on a Govt, mission of some sort or
other, and was duly enrolled in the ignoble army
of C.M.G’s , is now apologising for having
accepted the poor little bauble. He explains to
the effect that it was thrust upon him when he
wasn't looking. Also, the aged Jingo denies that
he wanted a handle in front of his name rather
than three letters after it, and here M'Culloch
claims the benefit of the doubt on the ground that
he is a widower. Inasmuch as perhaps 50 per
cent, of the Australian “.knights” have taken up
the rather expensive bauble to oblige their Lady,
it may be that M'Culloch didn’t seek the “honor”
which wasn’t offered to him. Nevertheless, it is
clear that the man who became a C.M.G.
“against his wish” would have suffered the in-
fliction of the extra K. with equal unwillingness,
and have even borne the anguish of being made a
dook for the Empire’s sake. What The Bulletin
really admires in Mac. is his artless candour con-
cerning his food-carrying capacity. He wrote
from London the other week: “It is nothing
uncommon to have two banquets and two ‘at-
homes ’in the one evening. We can manage the
two at-homes, but, alas ! only one dinner in one
day.” Alas, indeed !
At a Vic. up-country town, the other day,
the hearing of a charge was interrupted by one of
the honorary magistrates, who remarked :
We believe the man committed the offence and is guilty,
and no matter what evidence you produce, we have de-
cided to commit him for trial.
Which just about shows the standard of intelli-
gence and of justice among the unpaid magistracy.

The decision of N.S.W. Full Court, in the
Count de Rossi application the other day,
establishes that the Statute of Limitations not
only applies between subject and subject, but also
between the Government and the subject. The
Rossi bill of £lO,OOO ran back to 1858.
THE DEFAMATORY TRUTH.
No. 1 : “ You ought to he ducked in a ’orse-
trough, you hateful thi ng! ”
No. 2: “ Oh , you I you're ”
No. 1: “ Just don't you dare to say what I am
or I’ll taring yer neck.”
Pity the sorrow
Of William M'Culloch :
Fain would he borrow
A No. 2 stomach,
Then he’d gaily make light"
Of two banquets a night—
O, the joys of existence he’d double !
But he now has to choose
Which big dinner he’ll lose,
And he loses it — that is the trouble.
Dame Nature is cruel
To William M'Culloch,
She limits his fuel
To one little stomach.
Though the cards to invite
Offer two feeds per night
And to both he is solemnly beckoned,
Like a sinner accurst
He must stick to the first
And forego the delights of the second.

Admiral and Lady Beaumont said their Coron-
ation prayers amongst the cannon on board the
flagship, and at 12.30 the warships started scaring
the seagulls with their firing. Warship illumina
tions were the only elegant thing in the way of
knocking luminous holes in the dusk ; the primal
lines of the vessels traced in unwinking lights
gave them a mysterious, remote effect.
Someone writes that a dress worn by Lady
Carmichael Anstruther (Miss Sylvia Darley,
whose father, we are all glad to hear, has just
arrived in London in good health) at the last
Oourt was a Worth creation of great beauty,
.raqum is the more famous dress-builder now
so returned earth-girdlers tell me. Any woman
who can afford Paquin clothes doesn’t say, “ I’ll
go and dress,” she says, “ I’ll put on my Paquin.”
A M. L. acquaintance of mine who went to the
Coronation that Failed got a Paquin visiting
gown, put it on, and drove to a beauty-parlor to
get advice how to make herself more worthy of
hei marvellous setting. She sat down in the first
chair she saw, and that happened to be freshly
enamelled in turquoise-blue. The beautv-parlor
people had to pay her a trifle over two-thirds of the
cost of that gown, which was £75.
Genius Jean Gerardy dropped £l5OO by his
Austialasian tour. Yet he carried out his pro-
grammes and filled his dates unfalteringly. Franz
vyilczek, the violinist now in Sydney, asserts that
Gerardy never gave his musical wealth so lavishly
to New-Yorkers, and that he never played better.
.Perhaps a case of “the anguish of the singer
making the sweetness of his strain. ” A section of
Sydney-siders say that Gerardy’s failure was due
to his intensely classical programmes and their
strictly German character. To fetch a Sydney
audience give them Italian music—especially
Verdi s. There s a rhythm about it that is gener-
ally described by classic musicians as being
things 1 Sydney public likes sweet
In the face of the unanimity with which the
public stayed away from his recitals, Jean could
not have been flattered by the praise of the Aus-
tralian musical critics. He never was really
exhilarated until a Melbourne critic pio-
nounced Concerto in A Minor
, n .® “ dl tch-water” and unworthy of the
cellist s programme. He returned from Bris-
bane last week On the 25th Franz Wilczek
goes, with all his music mute as far as Aus-
tralia is concerned, though he is delighted with
the country and the people (who have talked two-
tooth ewes at him until he doesn’t know if he
wears clothes or a fleece). When Prince Henry
brother to the Mailed Fist, visited America, it was
Wilczek who was selected to play for him at some
famous Vanderbilt party or other. He also
appeared at the first function at White House
during the M Kmley Presidency.

The ball to be held in Sidney University.
Jubilee week (end of Sept.), is to be a purely
invitation affair, and is already giving rise to much
heartburning. The Sports Union (which controls
the annual ball) intended to sell tickets for the
Sept, festivity as was done in connection with the
July ball, but is so flush just now with the
proceeds of football matches —held on the
University Oval, boat races, and the last ball,
that it has been decided to make the next ball a
very dignified and select affair. Garden parties,
dinners and picnics are other gaieties promised to
the festive grad, and a ponderous publication con-
taining articles by professors is another joy to
look forward to.
Pekin, according to the gospel of “San Toy”
and scenic painter Gordon, must have been an
inviting place to loot. Makes me wish I had been
amongst the allied troops. Such a color-scheme !
It enables you to endure the drivel of the dia-
logue. Silver dragons against rose and gold
pillars and hangings. Grave, long-legged birds,
scrolls, stars, gods, Chinese trees and flowers, and
clouds and waves. Gordon must be Chinese
Gordon. He must have soaked himself with
Pastern color-poetry and then set to work.
Ihe piece affects the audience as it might an
imaginative child and is aided by the grotesque
orchestral music—a section of the audience, I
ought to say, for one part of it (a big one, too) was
composed qjf youths who were just beginning to
feel the ends of their moustaches sprouting, and
kept its attention exclusively for the understand-
Jj'Rs “f the maidens. Rose Musgrove as the little
San Toy with dwarfed feet moves in a mysterious
half-shuffle, half-run ; the contracted, dainty steps
reminding you of arpeggio notes. A sparrow-like
lady s maid (Miss L. Digges), four handsome
English girls m rose-pink, cream, helio and green,
a pair of faultless-looking Foreign-Office-like
youths and an impossible Consul in a tight
Reckitts-blue suit, make up an English colony in
1 ekin that would cause an interruption of diplo-
matic relations between any two Powers.
No fashionable Englishwoman, says the re-
turned (but frustrated) Coronater, would use a
sponge or towel on her face. These articles are
destructive to the complexion. She washes her
face with medicated cotton-wool and dries it with
chiffon yes, real chiffon. The first wash is in
water permeated with cleansing powder; the
second water is made astringent; and the third is
a distilled and scented liquor. Then, if you make
up at all, you use Someone’s “perfectly pure”
powder, “absolutely harmless” rouge, and “inno-
cuous” lip-salve. If you have very valuable
jewellery, such as famous sapphires or rubies, you
should dye your hair to harmonise with them.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ The Tennysons’Coronation Party, on Friday
night last, turned out to be a small and select
Commonwealth evening. Only the drawing-room
of Gov’ment House was used for the occasion, and
the Federal official world with its bediamonded
wife came along in uncrowded fashion, and was
leisurely presented by Better-Not Wally to the
Gov.-Gen. and his pleasant spouse. In the dis-
tance Lord Dicky made himself naice to people,
and pointed out the late Poet Laureate’s heir-
looms the manuscript of ‘Break, Break, Break,’
and the Galatian Cup which, after being used by
Irving and Ellen Terry for goodness knows how
many years, was subsequently presented to Alfred
the Laureate; also two paintings of the Gov.-
General s bairns, by Kate Greenaway.
The Windsor-uniformed politicians meandered
about uncomfortably. William Lyne presented a
most extraordinary pair of pink legs, showing
strange bulgings. Never were poetically-colored
stockings wedded to such strangely-fatted calves.
The short, stout, substantial members exposed by
Gov. Clarke cut the ground from under even the
muscle-proud Waxellency Madden. The most
stupendous, prodigiously tine, swashbuckling mili-
taire was the new State Commandant Gordon,
who swaggered about extensively, and aggressively
used a supercilious eye-glass. His idea of himself
is evidently colossal. He monopolised all the
fresh air in the reception room. Boss Hutton
wore his furrows and row of medals (to say
nothing of other things) with a comparatively
modest air.
‘ wasn’t it ? One of the Federal Ministers
at the Tennyson’s party had a hole in the back of
his white stocking. Fortunately white underwear
covered up the otherwise nude patch. And he
has a wife to look after him too.
Minister of Customs Kingston had drawn his
silk stockings up over his Jaegers—no, not Jaegers,
home - knitted pink - woollens, I fancy, from the
rosy color that blushed through.
“ The Tennysons made themselves very agree-
able. His Ex. s great bald pate shone with a
benignant light on the company—the bumps of
amiability, discretion and circumspection being
quite conspicuous to the discerning eye. He
seems anxious to do his duty, looks keenly at his
guests and talks seriously—that is, practically.
His consort is sweet. She shimmered in cloud-
grey satin, encrusted with gold and silver em-
broidery. Coronation-red warmed the northern
latitudes of the bodice. There were diamonds, of
course, and their most effective mounting ap-
peared m the Coronation loops forming
a background to the tiara. Lady Tenny-
son plays unconsciously with her fan.
Her face is full of expression, and her eyes kindle
with intelligence. She talks earnestly, sincerely
¥ou were disposed to like her by those stories of
her good work in South Australia ; but you must
like her when you meet her.
“ Most luminous among the dames that night
was Lady Hutton. She walked in beauty and
black satin, veiled and spangled. Great life in
that sparkling, splendidly-cut dress. Surmount-
mg her sunny head (quite a shower of summer
sunshine) was a great tiara against a cluster of
blue chiffon. The Madden family were immense.
Lady bil verlocks, despite her grand black velvet
with its Maltese draping, and blush-pink panels
and towering coiffure, and Miss Clarke, in her
pretty blue, were quickly lost to sight when the
Maddens appeared on parade—Guy in his
kilts (a living replica of the fat Joe Sedley
of Thackeray’s immortal gallery). Lady Madden,
juvenile—though in black—as her daughters, and
those two robust belles in wide, long-trained,
rustling, scintillating, gorgeous gowns. Miss
Ruby was in a sparkling white satin with panel
reefs of black cobwebs, and in heir highly-polished
hair were sprinkled dewdrops of chenille. Sister
Lesly, who becomes increasingly plump, was even
brighter in a.shimmering white frock, with hand-
painted exotics sprawling up the sides and about
the hips. Clumps of white lilac garnished the low-
cut bodice. The two sisters posed by the large fire-
place, the generous glare of it throwing their faces
into rosy relief. Gorgeous frocking characterised
the wife of Deputy-Postmaster Outtrim. She had a
Paris-branded creation, like a moonlight sonata—
luminous black and silver. Mrs. I. Isaacs tricked
forth her poetic arum-lily figure in a white silk,
over which forget-me-nots were spread in sweet
profusion.
“There wasn’t much sitting-space to spare on
the stands at Caulfield on Saturday, and the fight
in the members’ rooms for tea at 4 o’clock was
reminiscent of prosperous Cup days. The large
Coronation crowd was in exuberant spirits, many
actresses keeping things lively with their boister-
ous gaiety. A prominent horsey man—at present
a grass-widower—took three of the best known
Thespians in Melbourne under his wealthy wing.
The trio were discovered at one time practising
skirt-hitchings before the mirrors in the dressing-
room. Sassiety dames looked on in amazement,
but took the wrinkle back to the stand for dis-
cussion. And as the sportive trio flaunted on the
terrace afterwards, the world of fashion reckoned
the rehearsal of train manipulation was worth the
trouble, since no under-skirts and ankles
were shown to such cunning advantage by
any other flutterers in the swagger gathering.
The Tennysons were present in their royal blue
and gold enclosure. Lady Tennyson is no frocker.
Never did a more homely or kindlier-faced dame
face an aggressive world of watchers from vice-
regal quarters. His Ex. fixed the crowd with his
monocle, and studied their faces with a careful
air. Smart people rather squirm under the focus
of that highly-polished eye-glass. It drops from
its holding very abruptly, the scrutiny ended, and
dangles, as a reserve force, over the chest.
“The Gov. Clarkes gushed and beamed. The
two Y.R. box parties did not mingle much. The
Maddens, in subdued mourning garb, took a lease
of the most prominent Terrace tiles for the day.
They have wonderful standing power. Miss Mil-
licent Fairbarn was the fairest figure of the day.
She wore all cream, the daintiest of Russian cut
costumes, with sky blue tints in an expensive lace
collar. There was blue, too, in her rustic hat.
The most advanced millinery treasure of the day
was trotted out by Mrs. C. Wallace (one of the
ever contesting clan). This was a novelty in grass
lawn toques with forget-me-nots and small rose-
bud borders. It was more talked about than
even many of the swagger under-skirts. A lusty
comedienne carried off the honors for loud effects
by an eye-splitting, blue-bag costume, which was
hitched yards above a sunflower petticoat.
“By the way, dress criticism now, when men
are about, doesn’t draw the tine at the silk petti-
coat. It will be interesting to note if further of
the mysteries are to be topics of man-and-woman
conversation.
“Treasurer Shiels, thin, cheerful, flicking a lean
leg with a riding-crop, caused the one Tennyson
laugh of the day. The Gov.-Gen mistook the
man of figures for somebody else ; then Wally
dropped a word in the Excellency’s ear, explana-
tions followed and there were roars of laughter.
The Gov.-Gen. is another man when he gives
himself up to mirth. He looked—for the moment
—jolly. But the impression was quickly effaced,
and the impassive mask veiled the big, pale face
again. A curious feature of the Tennyson turn-
out is the big-crowned silk hat, the rim of which
nearly smothers the ears and covers the back hair ;
and His Ex. has only that back-patch on his noble
pate.
“"Two Western dames illuminated the d.c. of
the Princess on Wednesday night with their
rivalry in diamonds. Mrs. Everard Browne beat
Mrs. “Pursey” Chirnside by a neck. In both
cases gems flashed on every reasonably available
inch of space. But a colossal collar gave the
race to Mrs. Everard. Both the dames wore
white, with sobering jet ornamentations to tone
down the blazing riot of their gems. Mrs. Browne
sported the new half-sleeves of chiffon that fall
out under the elbow, balloonwise, and are caught
in with a jewelled band at the wrist. All the
front part of the arm is thus displayed, but not
the back. Women with knobby elbows will be
glad of the innovation.
No bugle-blast, and no thunder of carriage-
wheels, burst on the visitor’s ear, this year, at
Fmdon Harriers’ race meeting. Wednesday was
a dampish day, and an aesthetic dread of blue
noses may have prevented the attendance of the
mo3t swagger. The best turn-out was one of all
black—it would have been deadly but for a little
gold, quietly, almost apologetically, introduced
into the waist-belt. This was worn by Mrs.
Stewart Maearthur. A big black ruffle,’
an over - weighted black canoe-hat, black
brolly, and black gloves were of the most
approved order, but rather too mournful.
Mrs. Lawson wore a short black skirt, a very long
and large black coat, with tremendous revers and
collar of the Jack Tar order, blue and black stripe.
A big, pouched front supplied more blueness. An
immense saucer hat in vivid sky-blue tones, curled
up from the wearer’s dark head in the most
sudden and disconcerting manner. This scribe
cannot remember any bolder bid for dress honors
at any outing for a number of years.
“ Capt. Vallange—the Waxellency’s son-in-law
—has dropped his genial tone since secretarying

agreeable.
The mistress of ‘ Rippon Lea ’ is forward with
a series of musical parties, one for each month till
December. The tap of hospitality is turned on at
the bargoods’ all the year round. The practical
hostess enjoins silence during musical numbers.
The penalty for infringement is ultimate banish-
ment from her hearth.
“As in Harborville, the outcry against ‘mixed
marriages’ by the Melb. R.C. clergy is strong.
Parish priests implore the swells of their flocks to
keep their daughters for the Catholic young men of
the district. But ambitious mothers don’t enthuse,
it is a well-known fact that Society circles boast a
plenitude of R.C. girls and suffer a sad lack of
corresponding R.C. males. Michael has a lot less
mil about him that Kathleen. It is only in the
humblest ranks that the good R.C. eligible man
abounds; in other walks it is generally a choice
between a toney Protestant and a not-so-toney
R.C. And, as up to 70 years of age, this world is
of a lot more importance to most of us than the
possible next, the Protestant boy carries off the
nice R.C. girl nearly every time. This is the
sharpest thorn in the side of Archbishop Carr.

established,” and so can’t look at the matter in
a mother’s light.
The chiffon world revelled in first gloatings
Tt/T 6 ™ shows last week. See the eagle-eyed
Melbourne female of fashion as she swoops down
on a Spring Show ! How she fastens on a cravat,
a toque, a ruffle —prices it, examines it, com-
pares it, and finally drops it with a snap-shot
of its features tucked away in the mental
dark-room to be developed and produced for
the home sewing-circle later on ! Sometimes
she actually makes a purchase snapping
an imported novelty, and hastily negotiating for a
few alterations (thrown in for the original price).
And how they hustle ! Elbows are worked like
oar-blades to make a passage through the crowd.
Row and then there are recriminations. Again,
friends find themselves at hostile oar-practice, face
each other suddenly, apologise, laugh, and adjourn
for tea. There is nothing on earth, save a baby-
competition, that brings out the fighting instincts
of the smart woman like a battle at a Spring
Show.
“A Florence Nightingale on the staff of a
popular private hospital received instant dismissal
a week or two ago on being discovered, with skirts
hoisted, and a feather-brush in her nob, singing
‘ She’s a lady bred and born,’ a la May Beatty,
for the sole delight and entertainment of a male
patient. Some peeping jade informed on her, and
the matron, bristling with indignation, arrived on
the scene to demand an explanation. The ama-
teur comedienne was permitted merely time for
excusing herself on the grounds of a Mark Tapley
spirit of encouragement and cheerfulness, exer-
cised on behalf of a depressed patient—then sack.
“ It is said that a lady who was somewhat con-
cerned in the starting of the Melb. Women
Writers’ Club (where men are occasonally wel-
comed) was at one time a partner in a matrimonial
agency.
‘‘The Police Carnival (August 9), in aid of the
unemployed, drew a huge crowd. The sham
battle between Britishers and Druids delighted
the youngsters, especially when Australian scouts
galloped in to announce a ‘Surprise.’ Among the
‘wounded’ carried in was a particularly podgy
Highlander, who gave three comrades all they
could do to tote him along. Inmates of the
reserved seats, judging from firelit peeps of the
fat hero’s build and sleek hair, set him down as
Lieut. Gpy Madden.
“ An ex-mummer, who is in rather a delicate
state of health at present, attends the theatres and
does her shopping in company with a Florence
Nightingale, who abandons uniform for the nonce.
Melbourne knows of a Western squatter’s dame
who did not bother to make this fine distinction
when she was on the same sick-list. She trotted
the nurse about in uniform, and was the joke of all
shopland.
“The new Carmenita of ‘The Runaway Girl’
(Rose Hamilton) has a raucous voice, wide teeth,
and an air of good-humored confidence that carries
her over all difficulties. She makes up in vigor
and slap-dash for the sauce piquante of her pre-
decessor.
“Just now, when flowers are scarce, it’s worth
noting that even Govt. House falls back on hardy
blossoms, such as anyone can have who gardens at
all. T’other night, tall arum-lilies gave height to the
mantel-shelf corners, squatter posies of lauresti-
nus stood near the middle, flanking small vases of
jonquils. Tiny clusters of violets were scattered
about the tables, and some slim scented gums,
growing in big pots, diffused a whiff from the bush
through the crowd.
Yours affectionately,

Poetry and prose of the A.W.U. Shearers’
Manifesto:—
The pastoralists’ flocks, like the Sybil’s pages, increase
in value as they lessen in numbers. And a 25 per cent,
rise in wool goes to his credit (not yours) on top of it all.
“This is a Government Without Knees,”
says Vic. Railways Minister Bent, “ and it is not
going to bend to the protests of the men in the
slightest.” A Government Without Knees—
naturally. To supply a Bent Government with
anything to pray upon would be sheer blasphemy.
But it has a back to bend and a good large bingey
to lie flat upon, and it may want them both before
long.

WHISKY
PERSONAL ITEMS.
Melbourne’s three C.E. bishops —Perry,
Moorhouse, and Goe resigned that see and
returned to their native England. Yet Melbourne
seems to be about to try a fourth from the same
stock. •
Thomas Reilly, who died at Chippendale,
Sydney, last week, aged 92, was 24 years orderly-
sergeant at N.S.W. Government House. He
witnessed the coming and going of over a dozen
Governors.
People are much disappointed at not being
able to find in the Watchman any intimation as
to what part of the vineyard the Rev. Tremayne
Dunstan (ex-associate-editor of that paper) is now
laboring in.
An official observes that the only persons
found using works on political economy in N.S.W.
Parliamentary library are Labor members. The
youngest Tory, it seems, enters the House with
his political education complete.
Obit., Presbyterian Pastor M‘Gregor, of
Carlton. Obstinate bachelor, well up in years,
with a good benefice. The Kurruk thought him
indecorous. However, he and Father Kelly, the
best of friends, looked after the local poor.
Solomon Wiseman, who died at Wingen,
N.S.W’., and left £33,000 to his family, was one of
the original Broken Hill fourteen. After his
lucky hit on the Barrier he lost heavily in Queens-
land mining specs, and on pastoral properties.
Dr. Samuel Thornton, for years Bishop of
Ballarat, is a relative of Lord Kitchener on the
side of his mother, whose maiden name was
Kitchener. Bishop Thornton’s great grand-
mother was also the great-grandmother of Lord
Kitchener,
When Premier See gave N.S.W. Assembly
the news of the colliery disaster an old Kembla
coal-miner was presiding—J. H. Cann, Chairman
of Committees. Cann went to work at Kembla
when he came to Australia, and remained there
till he went to the Barrier in ’BB.
Ex-Mayor Ware, of Adelaide, who caused
so much talk by kissing the Duke of York’s hand
in Melbourne last year, and who has been lame for
years, has had the main sinew of one leg operated
upon in London, with the result that a missing
inch seems to be restored to its length.
Any chance of Sir William Gordon Cum-
ming, Bart., of Altyre, who fell out of society
through Tranby Croft baccarat scandal, being
restored to Court favor now that his old pal, King
Ned, is on top ? Sir Gordon Cumming and his
wife (an American heiress) have lived very quietly
ever since at the Gordon Cumming family estate,
near Forres, Morayshire, Scotland. He breeds
cattle, and she takes a keen personal interest in
all local philanthropic work.

It will be news to the admirers of the late
Thomas Carlyle to hear that that dyspeptic
philosopher was a graduate of the University of
St. Andrews. Hitherto Edinburgh has had the
credit of being his “ Alma Mater.” John Norton
has, however, recently put the world right on this
point.
Senator Simon Fraser (no relation to
Senator “Phraser” Symon) was 67 on Thursday
of this week (Aug. 21). Defence Minister John
Forrest was 55, and ex-Chief Secretary Moore
(Tas.)7s, on Friday; while Purves, K.C., is 59
to-day (Saturday), which is also Dr. Cockburn’s
birthday. Graham Berry will complete his 80th
year next Thursday (Aug. 28).
Despite the good example set by Gov.
Chermside and Auditor-General King, various Q.
high officials exempted from Philp’s retrenchment
scheme continue to hang on to their full salaries.
One at least should consider the position, but he
isn’t built that way. And, besides, look at the
traditions of the profession !
The movement to bring John Burns to
Australia on a lecturing tour originated with the
Freetraders in the Federal Labor party ; but the
Protectionists acquiesced, believing that John
would be prudent enough to keep out of the
fight. The matter of inviting him has been sent
on to the various trade societies.

“ Another injustice to Ireland ” is a meaningless
cry. In the Senate O’Connor is king-pin ; in
N.S.W. Owe’Sullivan is boss, with Dan O’Connor
as Court jester ; while in Q. the Leahy family,
John, Patrick, and Michael, are not allowing their
lights to shine under any bushels.
It is lucky (writes a correspondent) for the
much-frightened crew connected with the slaying
of poor chair-mender Heaps that his father is dead.
The ancient was a real old gipsy, with whom
revenge was a pride ; he would not have thought
much of shooting some of his son’s assailants off-
hand without asking them to surrender.
Rather a neat thing from London Daily
Chronicle
Mr. Barton belongs to the school of Lord Salisbury
rather than to that of Mr. Chamberlain. The latter
statesman need not feel that he is left out in the cold,
for Mr. Seddon, on the other hand, belongs rather to the
Chamberlain school; unless, indeed, we ought rather to
say that Mr. Chamberlain is a follower who is outrun by
Mr. Seddon.
It is only on the stage that every police-
man’s name is Murphy. Of the 2172 members of
the force in N.S.W. on December 31, but 351 were
born in Ireland ; of the rest, 1314 were native-born
—which, true, might leave their name Murphy
after all. There were 320 from England, and 157
from Scotland (more than half of these latter, by
the way, in the city).
Mrs. Barrington, who died the other day
at Woollahra (Sydney), claimed to be a blood
relative of Captain Cook, and the claim was so
well supported that the old lady received a special
invitation from N.S.W. Government to be present
at the last Botany Bay Kurnell circus festival
celebrating the glory of Dacey and the landing of
the British navigator.
J. A. Andrews, who now edits Melbourne
Tocsin, is seriously ill, having broken a blood-
vessel. Andrews, when in Sydney, suffered from
a lung trouble, which he kept under with fresh
air, creosote, and a vagabond life. Steady work
in editorial harness seems to have had a bad effect
on one of the ablest and most sincere of Aus-
tralia’s Labor advocates.
The trouble about Senate-President Chaf-
fey Baker’s wig has been happily settled. The
portrait was wanted to hang in S.A. Leg. Council
chamber (Baker being a past President of that
body). But the artist included the wig of a
Senate-President, and the local legislators insisted
on having their Baker partly bald, as they knew
him in the flesh, So the ex-President has offered
to take over the painting, and to present the
Council with another, which shall be all Baker
and no wig.
The late veteran William Hanson, first
N.S.W. State printer under Responsible Govt.,
who died recently, was easily seen
any time through the last ten years
in his cottage at Mosman, almost
blind but clear-headed to the
last. He had a mania for telling
old yarns, which did not usually
tend to put haloes round the
heads of the giants we have
known. The Govt, printing-
office, when he took it over,
was a typical old-Sydney
institution. Plenty of busi-
ness in it but no work done.
All that was contracted for
out-side, and the contracts
were arranged without over-
much publicity. Hanson, who
had not served h is time i n N. S. W.,
went to straighten things in a
hurry. His Minister didn’t know
what to say, but thought he had
better consult a very godly gen-
tleman out of politics, but high
up in the printing business. “I
met him,” said the new man,
“in the hall brushing his hat.
He didn’t ask me to sit down, but
after listening a bit opened out.
What had I got to do with the way
the business of the colony was carried
on? What did I know? Whom was
I seeking to traduce? What was my
little game? Wouldn’t it become me
better to keep in my own office and
attend to my own affairs ? I left him
in a dazed sort of condition. For he
was a very big man, upright, a pillar of
the church, and that business was little
better than burglary. It was a year or two
before I found out that he was behind every man
who had a finger in it.”
Ketchener is a worthy emulator of Soult.
Bonaparte denuded Italy of its finest pictures for
the Louvre. Soult went further and appropriated
the Spanish pictures, masterpieces by Velasquez,
Murillo, and so on, for himself. Bonaparte’s
plunder was disgorged, Soult’s never. A number
of his pictures are in English galleries. Thus
Ketchener with the statues.
Vic. Chief-Justice Madden has just had
something severe to say of the “ monstrous state
of affairs ” arising from frequent disagreements of
juries. And how about the frequent disagreements
of judges and the costly litigation caused thereby ?
Imagine the blessed state of the community if
every judicial decision could be accepted as final
and not liable to being upset by some other
bench !

name, it seems, is to appear “ Sir W. J. Clarke,

performance in England was when he took up his
place in front of the troops when the Horse
Guards paraded recently before the Queen, and

nance and . wildly revolving hat will be long
remembered.

that “the inhabitants of Lismore (N.S.W.)

Deceased Vic. barrister Topp was one of
three brothers, all of whom have earned for
themselves good positions in Melbourne. _ One,
C. A. Topp, is now Public Service Commissioner,
and should have many years’ work ahead of him
yet. The other is assistant sub-editor of Melb.
Argus, and the best and most reliable cable-
faker in all Australia. Both of these are thin,
nervous men, while the equity lawyer mopped up
the fat of the family, and was at the time of his
death the only double-chinned man at the Vic.
bar.
Died at Melbourne, last week, John
Conway Burke, who claimed to have carried the
first mails between Melbourne and Sydney. The
N.S.W. Govt, contracted with Messrs. Hawdon
and Hepburn, of Melbourne, to run a fortnightly
pony-express between the two towns, starting on
Ist Jan., 1839. Hawdon appointed his stockman,
John Burke, to carry the mail-bag out and home
again, the journey being made from station to
station, with one long stretch—about 200 miles—
of unsettled country. Bourke was 24 when he
took up the healthy job, and he died at 87, full of
reminiscences.
The death of Dr. Milford, of Sydney, re-
calls the early days of N.S.W. when judgeships
and other high offices were in the gift of English
politicians. The doctor’s father was presented by
the English Attorney-General with the position
of N.S.W. Master-in-Equity, in 1843, and from
that till 1865 he administered justice in Queens-
land and N.S.W. It must be said of Justice Mil-
ford, what cannot be said of all the Judges sent
from England “ with a character,” that he was a
man of refinement and equable temper. He re-
ceived a public funeral —a very rare tribute to a
N.S.W. Judge.
King Ned issued a Coronation message to
his subjects, and the Jingo papers were very care-
ful to point out that it
constituted an autograph letter, being a personal spon-
taneous expression of his feelings entirely conceived and
written by King Edward.
This impressive fact was cabled to Australia.
The Bulletin hasn’t the grave doubt on the
point which this over-earnest assurance presumes
will exist in most minds. Why should King Ned
be deemed so utterly stupid as to be incapable of
writing on his own three sentences of very com-
monplace English ?
Apropos the rumor that King Ned and
Prime Minister Salisbury had a serious difference
over H.M.’s suggestion that Ernest Cassel should
be promoted to the peerage. It is a fairly old
story that Cassel “ funded ” the royal debts. It
was, the story runs, when Hirsch died with a
third of a million of the Hard-up’s acceptances
under his pillow that the Hard-up realised that it
wasn’t safe to go on like that. So Cassel, not then
even knighted,- offered to put things on a business
basis —in plain English, to arrange for time-pay-
ment. Where it was not advisable to suggest
terms Cassel himself advanced the money and took
over the debt. Altogether, if the story is as told,
it was a business that did credit both to the latter-
day Ned and his liquidator, who has since been
made, a Privy Councillor.

The wife of an old-time Victorian Go-
vernor was a kleptomaniac. She never went
shopping alone, and on her return to Govt. House
from one of these expeditions her companion
always sent back to the drapers the things she
had secreted under her cloak. The tradesmen
were aware of Her Ex.’s peculiarity, and also
knew that they would lose nothing by it.
Dr. Torrey, ’Murkan evangelist, says he
is 46 years old, and has been “saved” for 29
years, prior to which he was the champion poker-
player, waltzer, and sinner-in-general of Geneva
city, U. S. A. Also, that he ran through two for-
tunes. This gives him 17 years in which to have
“ seen life,” and, unless he began as an infant
phenomenon, he could hardly have made the pace
red-hot until he was between 15 and 16, so the
few years at his disposal ere his conversion must
have been travelled at hell-for-leather pace. In
appearance, by the way, Torrey is nearer 60 than
46. The effect, perhaps, of late piety.
George Raynor’s pulpit manners are being
first tasted by Pitt-st. (Sydney), Congregational
flock, whose pastor lately left rather unexpectedly
the other day. Mr. Raynor went to Adelaide
from a Sydney suburb eight years ago, and at the
fashionable Glenelg church has built up a reputa-
tion as S. A.’s most brilliant preacher. Formerly
he spoke far too rapidly ; but he has broken him-
self of that. A little middle-aged bundle of
nerves, and marvellously fluent, he doesn’t trust
himself to speak without first committing every
word to paper, and even that is re-written once at
least.
Arthur Lynch, now committed for trial on
a charge of treason, in England, is an M.A. and
C.E. of Melb. University. He was first a mathe-
matical master, then on the staff of the Vic.
railways. He left Victoria to study in Berlin and
Paris. After dabbling in London journalism he
went to S’Africa as war correspondent for a Paris
paper; fought (it is alleged) with the Boers
against England; and on the strength of that
report, was judged to be “the man for Galway”
at last bye-election and was returned to the House
of Commons in his absence. Choice fell on him
because there was a legal difficulty in nominating
President Kruger. The Galway electors wished
to show their sympathy with the Boers.
The Pilot, the organ of the Pitt-st. Con-
gregational Church (Sydney) records that last
Sunday evening
The attendance was not as large as „e have been
accustomed to, but those who were there were amply
repaid for coming. Miss Ituby Bowring sang very sweetly
a solo, a song of comforti “Oh, Dry Those Tears.”
Which seems to have some reference, to a congre-
gation sorrowing for its Tremayne Dunstan. But
why all this irritating mystery? Has Mr.
Dunstan gone for good, or is he only taking a
holiday, so that he may get renewed strength for
a wrestle with the Devil ? If he has gone, why
has he gone? and why did he go off with the
suddenness of a colliery explosion ? We’ve spent
twopence on numbers of the Pitt-st. Congrega-
tion Church paper without getting any news of its
beloved pastor. Why this frozen silence ?
Explorer Gregory, hale and still active,
had a birthday (the 83rd) on August Ist. Of the
original band three survive, one in England, and
two, Gregory and Melvin, in Brisbane. At a
meeting of the Geographical Society in Brisbane,
on his birthnight, he gave an instructive resume of
his expeditions and early life. Gregory’s know-
ledge of mathematics enabled him to take stellar
observations instead of delaying his band every
day, and his instruments, used 50 years ago, were
tested lately and found correct. G. R. Dibbs was
supercargo on the Monarch when Gregory and
co. sailed in her from the Brisbane River before
they started on their big trip across Australia.
Beyond a gold medal from the R.G.S. and a
beggarly C.M.G. no one in authority seems to
worry much about the man who crossed this
continent from West to East and made heroic
efforts to find lost Leichhardt.
“ Big ” Kelly, the giant who impersonates
the Terrible Turk in “ The Circus Girl” at Mel-
bourne Her Majesty’s, was, until lately, a member
of Vic. police force, and retired on a pension
through ill-health. Kelly, who stands 6ft. 7in.
high, and who has a chest measurement of nearly
sft., is a native of Bendigo district, and hid his
manly figure under a bushel as an obscure bobby,
until Bendigo Easter Fair of five years back
offered a prize for the handsomest man. He won
hands-down, being, unlike most giants, a well-
proportioned and symmetrical man. Another
Vic. immensity, the “Purnim Giant,” 7ft. high,
lies hopelessly ill of heart-disease. He came
before the public first about 12 years ago, when
his father was summoned for not sending him to
school. The boy, brought into court as an exhibit,
then stood 6ft. sin., and upset the dignity of the
Bench by sobbing bitterly during the proceed-
ings.
The recently-deceased Albert Read, attor-
ney, who died at 90, shared with-Frank Stephen,
Chief Justice Madden’s dad-in-law, the leadership
of Melbourne police court. They begun to haul
in cash with the sticking-up cases, between Mel-
bourne suburbs, in the early goldfields era. An
indignation meeting about these crimes'was held
in the city. Frank Stephen spoke, saying the
offences were greatly exaggerated, whereupon
somebody put in, “ Why, the money that’s robbed
from us goes into your pockets ! ” Read told the
present writer that the secret of his longevity was
getting up at daybreak every morning and work-
ing in the garden among his roses. Which was,
of course, a fallacy : he got up early because he
was a strong man and felt well. Though he lived
so long, and worked so tremendously hard up to
80, nobody can remember the time when he was
not afflicted with a kind of permanent palsy, a
perpetual tremble all over. He wrote with his
hiceupy left hand almost as badly as Justice
Molesworth.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. —ln the ease of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
In such an appalling state are the tracks
leading out from Broken Hill, and so pinched are
many of the stations for feed and supplies, that
there are no less than 40 camel-teams at work out
there These teams run from 20 to 45 animals
and all are worked by Afghan-bossed Hindoos!
with a few mean Afghans. One or two Aus-
tralian firms have gone into the business, though
not with white labor; but by far the most of the
teams are still owned by Abdul Wade and other
Afghans. It’s a harvest for the bosses; but the
other poor wretches have only to trudge the faster
for their £2 or £3 monthly.

“ T.N.” to The Bulletin :
Premier See says the losses in stock, etc., and the gene-
ral effects of this long drought, are not half as bad as
represented. Aren’t they ! Up this way (N.W. N.S.W.)
they are far worse—many sheepowners have lost half and
two-thirds of their stock during the past six months, and
some have a few hundreds yet to die out of several thou-
sandS; Many men are cutting their sheep’s throats and
skinning them. In some quarters, efforts are made to
conceal the losses, but shearing will tell the tale. Why
in the township, butchers have to get their meat from
a.vdney—an unheard-of state of affairs. Remember, this
is an Eastern and Central, as well as a Western Division
drought. Still, it is consolation to the producer to know
that there is yet money left in Sydney for illuminations
and junketings of all sorts.
• * • *
“Trotter”; On a large horse-breeding
station on the Macintyre (Q.) the equines are
dying in dozens daily. The country is perfectly
bare, without a sign of grass any where, and in a
month or so, unless rain comes, will be completely
denuded. But it’s the .same old tale. The squatter
trusts entirely to Providence. Here is a station
with a long river-frontage, and not a cultivation-
paddock. Nor is a stack of hay to be seen any-
where. “What’s wrong with irrigation!” An
adjoining station that has a splendid cultivation-
paddock, and several stacks of lucerne, has saved
hundreds of pounds and kept its horses alive
during these trying times. Fancy stations of 1000
square miles having to buy hay !

“ Shaker ” writes from Ebagoola (N.Q.) :
“Longa’s” experience (B. 28/6/’O2) notwithstanding—
there are few well-made “ bucks” to be seen even on the
goldfields. Those niggers who lately carried a helpless
man ,J 0 miles through the bush to Ebagoola must have
been sturdy. But I was not writing of the blacks who
hang about the diggings, rather of those living in their
natural state in the unoccupied country in the north and
west of Oape York Peninsula, of whom there are even yet
a few thousands left. What is true of them now was
once true all over Queensland, but contact with whites
knocks blacks to bits in no time.

“Bobrid” writes :
Rabbits having gone up to 7d. per nair at Old June#,
every local cocky is humping a load of traps about his
paddock. Have often wondered why rabbits were not
breeding this season ; it appears the trappers keep bunny
on the move so constantly as to prevent breeding—or
else, dropping the young ones, the does have to bustle
on. I saw a paddock not trapped, it was literally alive with
kittens and “ runners.” Bunny has a great aversion to
a trap, and should a trap with a rabbit in it‘be left at a
warren not a rabbit will go near it for months. One local
squatter refuses to allow a trapper in his paddock ; his
theory is that inbreeding will eventually bring about a
deterioration in the rabbit, resulting in extermination.
It may—eventually, but we’ll never see it, anyway. As
the run is not wire-netted, the experiment is a rather
dangerous one for his neighbors. The fox is adapting
himself to circumstances about Junee district. One
chased, t’other day, climbed a tree 16ft. high, and disap.
peared down a spout ; was cut out, and the hollow was
found to be worn perfectly smooth from constant use.
A true bill 1 Junee whisky doesn’t run to foxes. ,

“F.C.” : It is printed (B. 26/7/"02) -that
Earnscleugh (M.L.) station is paying better as a
rabbit-farm than ever it did as a sheep-run since
bunny came. This just bears out what I said in
Bulletin years ago (I don’t claim any special
kudos for it) that the only way to keep bunny
under control is to make him pay, and that the
money squandered in wire-netting, poisoning, and
digging-out is merely thrown away. Prices for
rabbits in England don’t seemed to have dropped
a farthing in the last 10 years, and that market
could consume 10 times as many as we send. Also
there are the Continental, American and S’African
markets. We don’t appreciate bunny because lie
is too plentiful, just as the G-ippslanders from
iraralgon, eastward and northward, scoff at hares
(which are as thick there as rabbits in N. Victoria)
and as the Californian settler turned up his nose
at salmon when his rivers were so thick with them
he could walk across on their backs. He made
them pay and now they are scarce—so with bunny.

M.L. Govt. Biologist Kirk throws some
ight on the sparrow-problem. He started with
11 pairs, and allowed one-third loss for destruction
by natural enemies, natural deaths, &c. In the
?u r !i year A he " £ alrs increased to 121 pairs; in
the second to 161,051 pairs ; and in the third year
to numbers fail us 1 Kirk also discovered that
ere the young birds are ready to fly, the mother
sparrow lays more eggs in the nest, allowing the
youngsters to do the incubating until within five
days of the second batch’s hatching. In the crops
of sparrows sent to Kirk from all parts of the
country, and at all seasons of the year, only 34 per
cent, of the contents were insects—the rest beine
gram and seeds of different kinds.

“ Alone ” : Re the discussion as to whether
trees grow upwards bodily, or from the top only.
I believe the text-books, as well as those who
champion either theory, are both right and wrong,
to a certain extent. I have scores of fruit trees,
the main forks of which seem not to have grown
upwards an inch, since the day they were grafted
on, 25 years ago ; but growing with them there
are hundreds of others that have carried their
main forks upwards from 1 to 3 feet in that time.
Then consider forest trees like the stringy-
bark or mountain ash, of Grippsland, with their
main forks one and two hundred feet up. How-
ever long it may have taken such a tree to grow,
the main fork has been carried up since it formed
near the ground. Then, again, the reproduction
of the wood cells by gemmation is all in favor of
the tree growing bodily every way.

“Smilax”: In answer to “Hypo” (8.,
12/7/ 02), I think he is right m supposing that tree-
marks are not carried upwards with the tree’s
growth. For instance, in Richmond paddock
(Melb.), near the M.C.C. ground, is an old tree
which has a large, barkless space, wherefrom—
according to an inscription on the tree, “ before
the white man came”—the blacks cut away a
large sheet of bark. The patch extends now
three-parts round the trunk, and reaches to the
ground from about 15ft. up.
• * • a
It being announced in the cable news that
some representative Boers were to be sent to
Canada and Australia “to learn farming,” “one
who has lived among them ” wrote to Sydney
It might be as well to tell the Australians, at least, that
they could themselves very well take lessons from the so-
called ignorant Boers. The Boers are most thrifty and
self-reliant, and do not look for any outside help. ‘ They
irrigate their crops largely in dry weather. . . . They
utilise their fruit instead of allowing it to rot under the
trees, with the cry that it does not pay them to send it to
market. Soap is made from all the bones, scraps of fat,
and ashes, so nothing is wasted. Gandies, bacon, ham,
sausages, and biltong, of which we heard so much during
the war, are also produced. They grow their own vege-
tables, make their feather-beds from the goose-down
grown on the farm. In fact, the Boer farm is entirely
self-supporting. No such thing as tinned meat is even
known amongst them. Where there is pasturage for
cows all the butter is made lor tue nome use as well as
cheese. In the absence of grass goats are kept for the
sake of the milk. Their lesson here would be only to
learn how to waste all those things.
* * * *
While in 1881 the Maoriland native popu-
lation was estimated at 44,000, it had sunk by
1896 to 39,800; but the returns of 1901 added
3,000 to this total. This arrest of decay is chiefly
due to the operations of the “Young Maori”
party, an association of the most educated and
progressive among the Maoris to avert the
threatened doom of their race by teaching “the
gospel of work,” and the avoidance of ignorance,
uncleanliness and laziness. The leader of the
movement is a young Maori named Apirana
ISTgata, M.A. and LL. 13. of the M.L. University.
It has been in operation during the past five years.
The members of the association visit the different
pahs, doing their best to discredit the Maori
tohungas or prophets, teaching the doctrines of
cleanliness and improved sanitation, and opening
labor-bureaux and technical schools in native
centres.

“ Alone ” : There is no necessity for the
various agricultural departments to unduly adver-
tise or excite themselves over their more or less
unsuccessful attempts to preserve the harmless,
necessary egg. The cheap, handy, and by this
time widely-known preparation, “ waterglass,”
will make them keep good longer than any house-
wife is able to keep her hands off them. Eggs put
away in it last October are as good to the palate,
and to look at, as the fruit the hens favored us
with this morning, Probably it’s only a question
of germ-free surroundings and a cool temperature.

“ J.M.” : “ Crede ” (B. 12/7/’O2) is wrong
in saying that a growth of one inch utilises all the
albuminous substance in the seed below wheat
plants. In Southland (Maoriland) linnets in
millions tear up young wheat and oat plants to
get at the grain below. Watch one, and this is
now he performs: He grabs one blade and pulls it
out, and if the seed does not come up that bird
drops it like a streak, and so on till a seed does
come; then it is shelled and the milky substance
eaten. Larks eat grain, like linnets, but being
few in number do little damage. Heavy rolling
immediately after sowing is the only preventive.

“M.A.R.”: Recently saw a strange in-
stance of “transmission of desire ” in an hotel-bar.
A half-caste Maori was threatening to biff a youth
about half his size when up jumped a navvy, about
the same weight and build as the pugnaoious one,
and took up the cudgels on behalf of the youth.
The half-caste seemed very full of go and faced
the music in a promising manner, but ere a blow
was struck he faltered, hesitated, and then
dropped on a form and declined to go on. Not
even the severest provocation could get him up to
the mark again and interest in the proceedings
was fast frittering away when, just as the navvy
had given up all hope of a scrap, the process of
transmission took place and the colored person
suddenly arose and decided to go on. Yet though
not more than a minute had elapsed since the
navvy was shrieking for gore, he now took a seat,
and under a perfect cyclone of taunts and abuse
remained immovable. Casey, the publican, then
objected to any further developments.

“Fardowner” sends The Bulletin this
spieler-and-mug story as quite true :
It happened in Adelaide, a few years back. I had
travelled by steamer from Brisbane, and, on board, had
met a couple of Western Queensland shearers, and decent
chaps they were. We chummed up, and went ashore to-
gether, taking a room with three beds in it, at a place in
Hindley-st. There was a big crowd stopping in the place,
mostly bushmen, down for a spell. I was pretty short at
the time, but had a job of wheat-cleaning to go to, at a
farm I had worked on before. The shearers persuaded
me to stop a few days in town with them, and then we
were to go up country together.
\mon"St the crowd was a long, red-haired and fluent
gentleman, who introduced himself to us, giving, unsoli-
cited, the information that he was a native of Queensland,
his name y, and that he was waiting in Adelaide to
take charge of a mob of horses for an up-country station,
and that he had lately returned from America, whither
he had gone with a circus from Australia. He had a most
engaging and affable style, but still we were “ not taking
anv,” and our united opinion, when by ourselves, was
that Mr. -y was no good. ’
It was warm weather at the time. My mates were out
for the night, and I slept alone. On retiring, I rolled my.
pants up in a tight ball, and put them under the bed. In
the pocket was a purse, and in it a half-crown and some
small silver. Had I possessed much I would not have
left it there. Just after daylight something partially
aroused me, and, opening my eyes sleepily, I saw Mr.
v clearing away from the foot of my bed. I watched
him leave the room, and lay some time considering the
matter, still more asleep than awake. Suddenly I was
wide-awake, and the thought struck me—had he ? One
glance under the bed was enough—he had ' On examin-
ing the purse I found it contained a “ traybit ” only. I
presume he thought I would need a “reviver”—it was
verv thoughtful on his part.
Now one might easily have raised a row with Mr. —y,
put him on his guard, and got nothing; but somehow I
said nothing then. When I told my mates on their
return they said that was just what they expected.
In two or three days we started up country. Before
leaving I explained to the landlord, who was much
obliged for the information and said he would lay a trap
with marked money for Mr. —y, as he had done with
success once before for another man of the same variety.
His parting words to me were “ Keep your eye on the
papers.” I did so, and in about a week I read that Mr.
—V had been snapped. This time he had stolen £24
from one poor “ bushy,” besides more from others—not a
bad night’s work. Committed for trial, I read the Judge’s
remarks when sentencing him. They were sweet to me.
“ You are a young, strong man, fit, I should say, for
almost any kind of work, but you prefer to steal the
earnings of hard-working men, and in passing sentence
upon vou it is my duty to earnestly impress upon your
mind the truth of that old maxim : ‘ Honesty is the best
policy.’ Think well over this in the seclusion of your
cell during the next four years.”
Now, it is quite probable that Mr. y ma.y read
this, as Tim Bulletin is well scattered over Australia. If
he does, he will now know, for the first time, how the
“ chump ” got even with him for his few shillings ; and
the advice I tender him, in case he has not accepted the
Judge’s (which is quite likely) is this : When “ upending
sleepers” for an honest living, always pass the teeto-
tallers (I know they’re scarce), and “ run the rule ” over
beer-chewers only. Otherwise you may get “snagged’
again. ,
“J.L.” on the wet-sheep question (B.
17/7/’o2j :
In deciding whether sheep are dry enough to shear,
much depends on how the shearers feel. After a few
weeks “ tigering,” when men are tired, it takes but little
rain to make sheep too wet to shear, but, after some
enforced idleness, things are reversed. In ’93, at Buk-
kulla, near Inverell, I saw men, after a few days’spell,
shear sheep so wet that the wool had to be put outside to
dry. Writer was once shearing, near Walgett, where
several wet-sheep disputes arose. A light shower fell one
afternoon, and we prejudged the outside sheep wet. In
the night, the boss and jackeroo ran the dry sheep out of
the shed, and refilled it with the outside sheep. Next
day, we “ cut out ” the damp sheep, but knocked off after
each man had shorn two dry ones. After that, the cook
always approached the discussion of wet sheep matters
with a broad grin.
* • * *
The Daly River mission property is now
wholly secularised. The Jesuits. spent £50,000 on
that Northern Territory speculation, and sacrificed
nearly a score of lives, and after all had to quit.
The aborigines were untameable. The stock, con-
sisting mainly of goats, was sold, and the land
reverted to Government. Oranges, bananas, and
lemons were the chief fruits cultivated.

SWAGGIE: “Hullo, Pat, I thought you’s a

TAT: “ Kape quiet. Oi am wan. Y‘ see, a

“ Piccola ” : Of all people, dairy-farmers
are the most scrupulous about the milk they per-
sonally use. Every cookie chooses a particular
cow, with nice clean udder, calved at least
three weeks, and keeps her milk for his own
house. The amount of dirt, droppings and other
foul matter that gets into the milk at most farms
is shuddersome. Cows are often milked with
revolting sores disfiguring the teats, and Sydney
people would do well always to boil the milk
before using. Also, a farmer’s wife told me she’d
never touched factory-butter for 20 years, because
she’d been in the yards of some of the suppliers ;
said that it was a common thing for milk from a
cow, calved but a few hours, to be sent up to the
separator. -Other details are unprintable. Since
that interview I’ve kept a cow myself, and make
my own butter in a washing-bowl with a hay-fork.
CALLING HIMSELF TO DINNER.—SCENE ON THE MURRAY.
“ Hagram ” : Some time ago a meeting
was held at Kiama (N.S.W.) to protest against
the containing electorate being called Cool-
angatta. Somebody didn’t want any
“blackfellows’ names,” and one wiseacre
gravely., informed the meeting that Coolan-
gatta was an aboriginal word, and meant
a collection of huts. I have since dis-
covered that Coolangatta was called—prob-
ably by Berry, a Scot —after a place in
Scotland ; and that the word means some-
thing forget just what and water.
[Bulletin’s “Gazetteer” shows not one
place in Scotland beginning with Cool,
though it records 23 in Ireland, including
Coolboy ; and there is Coolscamp in Bel-
gium.—El). B.]
A N.S.W. Works proposal, which seems to
have more in it than most of them, includes an irri-
gation settlement at Menindie, and alight railway-
line from there to Broken Hill. Out of the Darling
near Menindie runs a creek which takes the fiood-
waters of the river into two lakes, Menindie and
Speculation, running back 15 miles towards
Broken Hill. Around these lakes is some rich
soil; and the hood-water could be dammed-in at
little cost. The place is peculiarly suited for
irrigation; and the closeness of a good-sized,
isolated market, which can’t grow a cabbage for
itself, is a circumstance which doesn’t often come
in the way of a new irrigation colony.
The British Govt, having ended the war
entered upon for the alleged purpose of extending
freedom to Englishmen in S’Africa, might with
advantage direct its attention to maintaining’
Englishmen’s freedom nearer home. This is;
quoted in London Morning Leader from the<
regulations imposed on laborer's who rent allot-
ments on Lord Normanton’s Crowland estate:
10. No occupier shall work on his own land after six
o’clock in the morning, or before six o’clock in the even-
ing, without the written consent of his master when ii*
employment, nor when out of employment if he has re-
fused or neglected to obtain work or begun to work and
then left it.
11. Each occupier shall with his family attend some
place of worship once at least every Sunday, and shall
enforce the attendance at Sunday-school of all his
children of proper age.
19. Occupiers keeping their families regularly at home
when capable of servitude ineligible.
Presumably “ servitude ” means domestic or farm
service, but in any case it describes the freedom
allowed to Englishmen who may happen to be
tenants of such as Lord Norman ton. The above
' are regulations 10, 11 and 19. There are others
about equally interesting.
THE LAST STRAW.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The sick man felt the cool spring wind
Blow softly on liis head,
And murmured “ I am not inclin
At present, to be dead.”
The sick man heard a trumpet call
A far-off battle-cry—
And gaily whispered, “ After all,
There’s time enough to die.”
A friend came laughing in the room,
His voice was full of glee—
ys he, “ Old man, away with gloom—
They’ve made you a J.P. ! ”
The faint flush on the sick man’s cheek
Died suddenly away ;
His voice became a sobbing shriek,
His face was like pipe-clay.
“ And, when they found me sick and weak,
To suit their wicked ends
They went and made of me a Beak—
Because I had no friends !
“ They used to hold me in respect
Not very long ago ;
What dreadful wreck of intellect
Has brought me down so low ? ”
The sick man turned him to the wall,
With his last breath said he—
“ If letters I must have at all
Let them be R.1.P."
C.R.
A Talk with the Winner of the Dunlop
Road Race.
Littleohild, who won the Dunlop Road Race
from Goulburn to Sydney, and cut down the
record to Bhr. for 137 i miles —a cut of 2hr.
2min. off previous best—is Tasmanian born,
stands sft. llin., and weighs 12.6 in his skin. The
day after the race he talked thus: —
“ I’m not married; no fear ! I don’t intend to ;
there are no points in that; 137 miles on the bike
would be a long way from home if a fellow was
married. I was born in Hobart, and I went to
school up the East Coast. am 26 years old.
Night before the race I went to bed at 11; awoke
at 5 ; then I had another sleep till 6.30; I had a
steak, two eggs, and a cup of tea. Some of em
had 20min. start of me ; and I could hear em
going away as I was finishing breakfast, I here
was a frost. The bridges were heaped with it
like snow. I could see the men ahead of me,
riding with one hand, while they kept the other
behind their back, up their guernsey, to keep it
warm.
* * * *
“From the start I had one thought—‘ Would
the back men catch me ? ’—and my mind never
wandered from that one thought for the first 47
miles ; it drove me over 47 miles of mountains in
2A hours. Then it was Moss Vale, and they told
me I was ahead of all of ’em. That news was the
biggest shock I got on the road. So I got off and
ate a fowl—or most of it; I put the legs m the
bag and ate them on the road. I had no bread.
The fowl was sprinkled with. salt. I drank a cup
o’ tea with two eggs in it with the fowl at
Moss Vale. I had more tea and egg at Picton ;
and another fowl walking up the Razor-back four
miles this side Picton. I carried a can o beet
tea — lb pint—-fixed on the bike;. I had a pull at that
now and again. Another drink of beef tea at
Campbelltown and a glass of sherry at Bankstown.
And I chewed celery. It’s better than a drink;
it keeps the mouth cool. If you’re thirsty.on the
bike you can drink three times as much as is good
for you. But the bag o’ tucker they gave me at
Campbelltown, the hard-boiled eggs, the oranges,
the bananas and the raisins I carried round my
neck right into Sydney P.O.
* * * *
“I don’t feel ill after the race. But I know
I’ve had a ride. The only thing that troubles me
is my appetite. I’ve been hungry ever since I got
off the bike. I had two breakfasts this morning
straight off, without getting up from the table.
“ I ran over a fellow who fell off his bike—one
of the competitors —lb miles out of Goulburn.
Coming down the Razorbaek, in dodging another
fellow, I ran up a wattle tree. I held on
to the branches and let the bike go. There
were crowds at every town and village on the
load, farmers and their wives and people offering
us tea, milk, coffee, and tucker; and the cheers
of the people freshened me up—sent me along.
The only thing I suffer to-day is that my right
hand is numb with the vibration .of the handle-
bar—so numb I can’t hold a knife in it.
* * * *
“As I said, I had one thought for the first 40
miles; but at Moss Vale, where I had the
fowl, I got another thought—Harris. Harris
glued himself to my back wheel, determined
to stick till Harris dropped. For the last
hundred miles I had Harris on my back wheel
and Harris on my brain. I wanted Harris to pace
and pace about with me. But Harris said that
Harris was too tired. He, too, was tired of Harris.
I tried to shake Harris off all the time without
knocking myself right out. But Harris took good
care that I didn’t get away from Harris; Harris
watched me too closely. I used to suddenly shoot
■out on half-mile bursts to get a break of 50 yards
on Harris. And every time I finished a, burst I
would look round—Harris ! Afterwards I used to
put in a burst and not look round. I’d listen !
listen to the breathing of Harris. Harris hugging
my back wheel, up-hill; Harris, Harris down hill,
on the flat, Harris. Fowl and Harris, beef-tea
and Harris. Tea, milk, sugar, eggs, port wine—-
and Harris. From the top of Picton Range 50
miles into Sydney—head wind ! —head wind and
Harris. Coming down the range, with the sun
behind my back, the shadow that fell in front was
Harris’s. I felt that Harris bad married me to
Harris. I tried to think out how I could get
away from Harris; but I couldn’t, because
of Harris. At Bankstown Harris asked me to
have a glass of sherry with Harris. I felt
it was kind of Harris. I consented, got oft with
Harris, drank with Harris, drank Harris’s health.
Then I re-mounted with Harris. The last two
miles with Harris I took it easy, for I knew, same
as Harris, that I was in for a tussle with Harris.
Sure enough I was right about Harris. I had
suffered for a hundred miles from Harris, now the
crowd around the winning-post gave me Harris .
Harris ! Harris ! A hundred yards from home
Harris got in front with Harris’s wheel. Then I
put in all the kicks I could, and Harris and I hung
dead level for 25yds., and for another 25, I and
Harris. Then I had three more kicks left and
Harris hadn’t, so I got clear bv half-a-wheel from
Harris and beat Harris. f f• t all right at the
finish, but it makes a d; hr /ice when youre
winning, and if Harris had ii si shed in front of me
I’d have felt more knocked up than Harris.
S. A. MILLS.
“ J.R.” writes :
Bulletin’s “militaryvcorrespondent” (2/8’02) seems to
have found a mare’s nest. What on earth does Mr.
Chamberlain know or care about Australian rifle-clubs,
and why shou'd he give orders to Gen. Hutton ? He has
other fish to fry, and has many months’ work cut out in
S’Africa. If the members of the rifle-clubs don’t want to
belong to military bodies or to be drilled, but only to
become good shots, they don’t interest Gen. Hutton
either. He is only concerned with military bodies. A
good deal of humbug is at the bottom of the cry of the
rifle-clubs. The Commonwealth is expected to give them
free rifles and ammunition, and get nothing in return. If
the men become good shots, they are in no way bound to
defend the eountry. They do not form any part of its
defensive organisation. They also want a “staff officer
to look after their interests, or rather their ammunition
supply. Judgingby recentdevelopments, it would bea good
thing if the ammunition supply were cut short or cut off al-
together throughout Australia. There is a great deal too
much promiscuous shooting. It is quite proper to teach
the Commonwealth soldiers to shoot straight ; but it
should be done under proper supervision, and when a
man goes to the range for practice he should be given the
exact number of cartridges he will have to use, and it
should be someone’s duty to see that he pockets none.
Match-shooting is becoming a dangerous nuisance.
People who have a fancy for it should be made to join the
country’s defence force and be paid for their proficiency
by the country without the intervention of associations
or clubs. The money spent on such things would pro-
vide a very substantial prize-fund, and its withdrawal
from the associations and clubs, whose members shoot
without proper supervision, would save the public a deal
of annoyance and danger.

THE BOER AGRICULTURAL DEPUTIES TO AUSTRALIA—WHAT THEY WILL SEE.
“London, 2 5th Jul y . — The scheme of the British Government for giving instruction in scientific farming to specially-selected Boers, with a view to their imparting the knowledge
gained to their neighbors, has aroused much interest among the Boers, many of whom have applied for membership of the parties of agriculturists who are to be despatched to Canada
Australia and Maoriland for the purpose of studying up-to-date farming methods.” — CABLE.
