SRISBA.3NTE.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Is the Governor-Generalship to die of
inanition ?
Epitaph for Australian iron and steel
industries dying still-born —Nil nisi bonus.
The Radical Peter Airey was the first
victim of Q. Hansard’s retrenchment scheme.
He spoke at 2 a.m. (one of the best speeches heard
for years) and got four inches. A maundering
Philpite, who preceded him, got four columns.
The mere statement of an opinion such as
that of J.C. Fitzpatrick, M.L.A., (N.S.W.), that
a .Judge whose decision is overruled by the Full
Court should pay the costs of appeal, does a
service. The opinion is wrong, but its statement
is right.
In 1894, when N.S. W, Assembly still
numbered 141 members, an average of 20 was
with difficulty maintained during a debate on a
motion to abolish the Leg. Council. The other
night, in a. House of 125, the average attendance
during a similar debate was 24.
A Philpite orator recently dwelt proudly
(in the Assembly) on the fact that Queensland had
had 25 deficits and actually 18 surpluses. If all
the surpluses of the Continuous Govt, for the last
10 years were added together they would total
£105,000, while one deficit (the last one) amounts
to! £431.000.
“If Government gives seed - wheat to
farmers after a drought, so as to help them to take
advantage of the next season, why does not the
Skate provide us with food for ourselves and our
little ones to enable us to take the next job that
offers ? ” That is the logical demand of one of the
Adelaide unemployed.
The Bill providing lor the borrowing of
£1,000,000 for irrigation purposes is making good
progress through N.S.W. Assembly. The prin-
cipal change, so far, is that the proposal to provide
a sinking fund for the repayment of the loan has
been indignantly struck out. The idea of repay-
ing anything was too horrible.
Horace Tozer devotes himself, in his
leisure time in London, to writing magazine
articles for British journals. A bitter pro-Kanaka
effusion appeared recently over his name in the
Ejipire Review. Q. Govt, sees nothing wrong in
a public official thus comporting himself—and
openly in the Assembly defends Tozer’s action.
'Victorian Railway Minister Bent has
addressed a memo, to Manager Fitzpatrick asking
him to suggest a way of wiping out the railway
deficit. The Bulletin begs to suggest a way.
The most obvious cause of the deficit is too
much railway for too little population. If the
big, almost unpopulated estates were compul-
sorily resumed and cut up, .Victoria would soon
have 100,000 or 200,000 more people in its coun-
' try districts. Then there would be more traffic
and more revenue, and the deficit Jwould have a
chance to disappear.
A LOYAL SUBJECT—HIS COMPLIMENT
TO LOYALTY.
(Scene: Sydney Domain.)
1ST. DERELICT: “Bli’me, what yer doing of? ”
2ND. DITTO : “Aw—I wash my feet, doncher-
know, every time a King of England’s
crowned.”
It was truly cheering to hear a well-known
Northern supporter of the Philp Govt, rise
recently in his place in the Assembly and confess
that at least ten of the Govt.’s supporters were
against a reduction of payment of members just
as much as the members of the Opposition. And
the Brisbane press carefully suppressed his utter-
ances next morning.
The rottenness of Victorian politics is
suitably illustrated by ex-Solicitor-General Agar
Wynne’s leadership of the Anti-Factories Act
party in the Leg. Council. Wynne was a staunch
supporter of the Act when serving under Peacock,
and Prendergast, the most Radical man in present
day Vic. polities, called him “the only democrat
in the Peacock Govt.”
Premier Irvine has announced to the press
that Federation “costs” Victoria over £400,000 a
year, mainly because of the removal of these
duties : Tea (£100,000), sugar (£135,000), stock
(£60,000). This ought to be interesting to the
hardened N.S.W. anti-Billites, who used to reckon
that Victoria stood to make millions a week out of
poor N.S.W. in consequence of Federation.
Vic. Treasurer Shiels' Budget speech con-
tained one pill that wasn’t at all sugar-coated. In
proposing to knock £50,000 off the municipal sub-
sidy he said, “ The under-valuation in some shires,
especially the shires that have got most out of the
millions of money spent in irrigation and water
supply, amounts to a positive scandal.” Kyabram
(Rodney Irrigation Trust) winced, but said never
a word.
The incredibly foolish Philp, having got
his State into an unspeakable financial mess, tells
the public that he thinks the time has come for a
renewal of the system of free immigration in
Queensland. And roaming over great tracks of
Northern and Western Queensland you meet num-
bers of gaunt, hungry men who can’t get a day’s
work in a month. The news of Philp’s inten-
tions will certainly enliven them.
If the Commonwealth Government insists
that two houses, one in Sydney and the other in
Melbourne, shall be kept up for the Gov.-General
at Commonwealth expense, the Melbourne one for
quasi-permanent residence and the other for
occasional visits, the other States can reasonably
put in a claim to have houses in their capitals
similarly up-kept, or to be relieved from con-
tributing to the speculation in Sydney and Mel-
bourne.
Deputy - Postmaster - General Dalgamo,
under examination by the newspaper postal pro-
tection advocates, was confronted with a “divided
duty.” As a former officer of the State of N.S.W.
he had to condone the old unholy alliance between
post-office and press, and as a present Federal
official he had to avoid uttering a word which
could be construed as condemning the termination
of the liaison. It was a tight place, but he got
through without turning a hair.
S. Australia, the hard-up State which has
lately sacked its oldest officers to save their poor
pay, has just received from the “War Office” a
consignment of 10 drill-instructors, shipped from
the eastern States to do for £l5OO a year what has
hitherto been done for £65. Ten drill-instructors
—and there isn’t a recruit in sight! To the poor,
pinched State, making an honest attempt to live
within its means, it would seem altogether a farce
if it hadn’t so many of the points of a tragedy.
The offer that, if Australia agreed to help
along British industries by admitting British
goods on better terms than those of other countries,
Britain would still only admit Australian goods on
the same terms as goods from Franee and Russia,
but would repay the concession by giving larger
subsidies to Britain’s oion ships trading in Aus-
tralian waters, was the kind of liberal offer that
should be long remembered at this end. Aus-
tralia was to give Britain something, and Britain
was to show its appreciation by giving itself
something.
The attempt to get a second hearing for
the Iron Bonus Bill
roused up the Foreign-
trade party, and es-
pecially Thomson,
of N. S. W., to
protest against
“ nursing up” arti-
ficial industries to
“a sickly man-
hood.” And cer-
tainlyif the manhood
of the iron industry
proved as sickly as
that of the N.S.W.
dead - sheep pastoral
business and the eight-
bushel agricultural busi-
ness there would be
cause to complain. If
the iron business was
allowed to work its em-
ployees the same hours
for the same wages as
the agricultural biz does,
it wouldn’t need either
Protection or bonus. And
if it received half the aid
and coddling that the pas-
toral and agricultural biz
has done, it wouldn’t need to
pay the same starvation wages.
The misery of the affair is that
the Freetrade party insists on
spending all the aid on theindustries
in which Australia is heavily han-
dicapped by drought and barren-
ness, and refusing it to those in
which this country has a fair chance of success.
Despite the recent row in N.S.W. As-
sembly over faulty Consolidating Bills, the Govt,
still goes on passing them, stifling debate by
applying the “gag.” Many mistakes have been
made by the Commission, and some bills have had
to be “reconsolidated,” as it were. A ludicrous
error, for instance, occurred in the Vagrancy Act.
John Norton blushed—almost—as he drew atten-
tion to the wording of a certain clause, and
begged the ladies in the gallery to withdraw while
he read the offensive section.
A few weeks ago the House of Representa-
tives decided by 25 votes against 12 to grant
bonuses only to ironworks owned by the State.
It has now reversed that decision by 24 votes to
18, and thrown the bonus open to anyone who
likes to start the necessary works. But the Free-
trade party is very strong in the Senate, and it is
so bitterly opposed to starting any Australian
industry that will interfere with the profits of the
miserable gang of foreign importers that there is
probably still trouble ahead.
Another effort is to be made in N.S.W.
Parliament to pass the Amending Libel Bill.
Wise tried it, See tried it, and now O’Sullivan
will chaperon the measure. But the Assemhy
will scotch and the Council slay it. At no previous
period were Assembly and Council so thorough in
THE SHEARING TROUBLE.
—ABORTIVE SYDNEY CON-
FERENCE.
“ Mr. Cunningham (Pastoralists), in the course of a
conciliatory speech, said the facts and figures adduced
went to show that shearers were well paid, highly paid,
in fact. Pastoralists used to get on very well with the
shearers when they managed them themselves and they
had no occasion to apply to the Union. It seemed to him
they were not now allowed to manage their own busi-
ness."—Daily Paper.
The Churches also got on very well with the
heretic “when they managed him themselves” —
i.e.% till the formation oj the anti-bonfire league.
distrust of the Press. It is not very logical to
hang an illiberal libel law on the neck of the
Press because a powerful section of it is grasping
and mercenary, but both Houses get satisfaction
out of hearing the papers squeal.
Railways Minister Thomas Bent has un-
earthed the fact that the Victorian rolling-stock,
which professes to adequately represent £5,740,089
of borrowed money, is now only valued at
£3,042,000, over £2,000,000 in depreciation not
having been provided for out of revenue, as it
should have been. If Minister Thomas professes
to be surprised at that it is a very thin pretence.
Where, in Australia, is anything ever written off
for depreciation of works constructed out of loans ?
Bent shouldn’t confine his investigations to the
railways—unless he is trying to work up a case for
selling the lines to a syndicate.
Coghlan estimates that the capital sunk in
the N.S. W. pastoral industry is £138,724,000. The
gross return he estimates for 1901 at £13,707,000.
Out of this 26,619 hands have to be paid,
machinery of various kinds purchased, railway
freights paid, and losses of stock through bad
seasons made good. Altogether, the pastoral
industry doesn’t seem to return more than 5 per
cent, net on money invested. The country that
depends wholly or mainly on pasture and agri-
culture is always a poor country, and the “great
national industries,” as the Freetrade party loves
to call them, are a very moderate spec, on the
whole.
The Tas. House of Landlords is a perfect
whale at legislation affecting mere working people.
Decided, t’other night, in discussion on Inspection
of Machinery Bill, that no driver of an engine of
less than Bh.p. shall be required to hold a certi-
ficate of competency. A surgeon fathered the
brilliant suggestion—perhaps in the certain pro-
fessional knowledge that when a ricketty 7 h.p.
engine departs skyward, taking an uncertificated
driver with it, and leaving a large hole in the place
that knows it no more, the fragments will not
have to fall far enough to hurt themselves. One
horsepower makes a vast difference in calculations
of this sort.
There is a humorous side to the fuss now
being made by Melb. Chamber of Mines re the
excessive prices of explosives in Victoria, where
86s. a ease has to be paid for what the Johannes-
Cohens get at 555. One of the most awful
grievances the S’African Cohen suffered under
before he got Chamberlain to make war was the
dynamite monopoly, held by Nobels’ trust. Nobels
also hold the monopoly in Victoria, and the prices
they charge for explosives are just about the same
as those in S Africa before the war. The Irvine
Govt, should, therefore, be prepared at any
moment for hostilities on the lints of the historic
Jameson raid.
The present Vic. proposal to reduce
Hansakd by two men amounts to shameful
sweating. Some years ago, an attempt was made
to work Hansard with four men, and it failed
miserably, because the overworked staff was at
most times one short through somebody breaking
down. The Sabbatarian Minister M’Kenzie will
also be shocked to hear that the reduction will
entail the reporters working all the Lord’s day,
and filling the air with curses instead of prayers
and reading of the Book. The whole idea is'but
a miserable concession to the selfish demands of a
morning paper, chagrined at seeing its best men
walk, over to Hansard whenever the chance arose.
One of the new follies proposed by the Bill
Lyne Federal department is the. appointment of
a commission to consider the value of the State
properties to be taken over by the Federal Gov-
ernment. Why not simply pay the States what
they ask for the properties by taking over a
corresponding amount of their debt, and let things
rest at that? If theproperties are worth £10,000,000
and the Federal Government pays £30,000,000
for them it makes no difference, so long as it
is simply a transfer of debt. The Australian
people own the properties now when they are in
the hands of the States, and they will own them
when they belong to the Commonwealth. The
Australian people owe the money for them now
through their State Governments ; they will owe
it then through their Federal Government. When
there is so much to do it is foolish to haggle over
a thing that doesn’t matter. Whether the States
are paid nothing at all, or are paid six times the
real value by Jhe Commonwealth assuming that
amount of debt, is all the same thing.
The names of those strong and con-
scientious N.S.W. Freetraders, Edwards and
Holy Joe Cook, are noticeable among those who
voted against agreeing with the Senate’s proposal
to reduce the Protection granted to the beer in-
dustry. The necessity for giving lots of Protection
to the beer industry is one to which Free-
traders are very wide awake. A proposal to abso-
lutely abolish all Protection to the beer industry
would probably have brought up every Freetrader
in the House in protest against his own alleged
principles.
The motion of Arthur Griffith s jyI.L.A.,
for the abolition of N.S.W. Leg. Council should
be pushed to the uttermost. Some State must
make a beginning if this reform is to ba
carried out —merely through the establish-
ment of the habit and precedent, tha
abolition of one Upper House will make
it easier to abolish the others. • The N.S.W.
Council is a good one to begin with. It is
weak and easily knooked over. It is useless
in a democratic sense because it helps on no
reform, and in a Tory sense because it is
too feeble to seriously oppose any reform.
It represents about the last possibility of
harmless flabbiness —but it should go
despite its harmlessness, because it
costs money, because it isn’t orna-
mental, because of its past sins, and
because it is necessary to set a good
example to other States whose Coun-
cils are still actively injurious.
The rumor that the Vic. railway servants
will go on strike rather than submit to Treasurer
Shiels’ proposed wage reductions is gratifying.
The alternative lies between wage reductions or
an all-round tax on land, such as other countries as
good as Victoria, and other Fat Men as sacred as
the Victorian Fat Man, put up with as a matter
of course (the Vic. land-tax only touches proper-
ties that are more than 640 acres in extent and
£2500 in value, so that all Melbourne is exempt).
Shiels, being in political matters a coward of the first
magnitude, takes the wage reduction way as being
the line of least resistance. The only remedy is
to show him that he has made a mistake, and that
it is the line of most resistance.
N.S.W. Leg. Council having threatened
death to the Woman’s Suffrage Bill, there
are rumours of a possible appointment of
sufficient Labor M’s.L.C. to carry the measure.
The Bulletin trusts this isn’t so. The
only purpose for which Labor members should
be appointed to the Upper House is to carry
the Bill for the total abolition of that excres-
cence, and for that purpose they should be
appointed with all possible speed. Swamping for
any other purpose is utterly illogical. If the
Council is only to say ditto to the Assembly (and
the system of swamping it whenever it says any-
thing else than ditto proves that) then it isn’t,
wanted. The Assembly can say its own ditto.
The cabled news that Seddon has seen the
Austrian ambassador in London, and assured him
that his countrymen are treated just like other
visitors, has stirred the gall of the Austrians in
Maoriland. One of them sets forth that it is
over years since he applied for naturalisation
papers and complied with all necessary formali-
ties, and that he can’t even get an answer to.
his application; and he says that others are
similarly, or worse treated. Further, he says that.
Austrian immigrants who can comply with all
the conditions of the Immigration Restriction Act
are not allowed to land, or to prove that they can
pass the tests —the almighty Dick simply says,
they must go back, and Dick is law, or rather
Dick is above the law.
“ Atlang ” writes :
Re your par. (B. 19/7/’O2) concerning a referendum by-
the Queensland Bible-in-schools party on its pet hobby.
The modus operandi of a similar affair, which took place-
in S. Australia years ago, may be of interest An emis-
sary of the B. in S. party came round with voting-slips.
If he found both parents of a family in favor of his views,
both were asked to vote on slip. If only one parent was.
favorable, only that one was given a slip. If both parents,
were"antagonistic;' the B. in S. man entered a stroke (only
one, mind) in a note-book he carried for the noes—or else
he didn’t enter the stroke. Where the parents were
indifferent they were often badgered into signing on the-
plea that they had no marked objection. Result: A
brilliant one-innings victory for the B. in S. Result, a
brisk agitation through pulpit, press and platform. Re-
sult, an honestly-conducted referendum. Result: The
B. in S.—oh, where was he ?
Vic. Treasurer Shiels: “If the State
could carry on its business on the same free and
untrammelled lines as a private employer, if it
exacted the same application of energy to the
daily task, we might reduce the number of public
servants by 4000, and that would clear off the
whole of the deficit before us.” Just so ! Any
level-headed private employer—say the Melb.
Tram co. —would probably sack all unnecessary
hands, the ornamental Governor, Black Rod,
Sheriff, etc., and would pay as wages for each
billet what it thought the job was worth. (When
it found another equally large concern paying ita
Chief Justice £2OOO a year it wouldn’t give him
nearly twice as much just for the sake of looking
“ flash.”) But the vapid, indefinite Shiels doesn?fe-
explain why the State can’t run itself on common-
sense lines ; possibly the fact that it has a £9OO
a year Treasurer to start with has something to.
do with it.
A week or so before Tas. Parliament
opened, Premier Lewis, in accordance with time-
honored British precedent, journeyed down to.
Richmond (the whole village could be easily
stowed inside Sydney’s biggest church) to unfold ,
the Ministerial policy to his enlightened consti-
tuents. The policy was a fine large affair and
dealt with everything of Tasmanian, Federal, and
Imperial interest in a broad and statesmanlike,
manner. There were no interjections; the audi-
ence was far too over-awed to interrupt, and much .
too respectful to do anything but shuffle its feet
occasionally at a signal from the local magnate in ,
the chair. The statesman’s peroration was a sub-
lime flight of oratory. Then the local coach-,
driver rose apologetically and asked Premier Lewis,
K.C.M.G. (think of it!), when he thought it pro-
bable that that chains of metalling would be
done on the main road. And for the first time the.
audience grew interested.

VITADATIO
Suffered More or Less from 1888 to 1897.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER,
Dear Sir,—lt is with very great pleasure I testify to
the inestimable value of Webber’s Great Herbal Remedy,
VITADATIO.
In 1888 I had a severe attack of gall stones and conges-
tion of the liver, which confined me to my bed for some
time. The doctor who attended me said I would never
be thoroughly cured ; but I used to feel quite well be-
tween the attacks, which recurred very often —sometimes
two attacks in one week. In the year 1892 I was very
ill with my long-standing trouble, and had another
medical man, and he told me that gall stones could only
be cured by operation, which I would not consent to.
In February, 1897, I received a letter from my sister in
Dunedin, New Zealand, telling me of the wonderful cures
VITADATIO had affected there, and urging me to take a
course of this medicine. As soon as I knew VITADATIO
was obtainable in the colony, acting upon the advice of
my sister, I obtained six large-sized bottles of the remedy,
and am happy to inform you—and, in fact, the world for
the matter of that—l have been free from pain ever since
and now am in perfect health.
FLORENCE BURK.

Yarraville, Melbourne.

it Silvertail ” on a penal matter :
The Bulletin (12/7/’O2) slated Judges Backhouse and
rJckerfor making foolish addresses to convicted prisoners
nf the “respectable” class, and then ending up by giving
?hem the Ximum sentence. But why should the unfor-
tunate “Silvertail ” get the limit? In nine cases out of
ten the “respectable” man falls through financial dis-
honesty He has greater temptation because his know-
ledge suggests to him greater opportunities and less
risk If he is pushed for money, he must need ten
Hmes more will-power than the common crook to
resist temptation. Then one month’s confinement to the
“ SUvertai'l” man, who is generally hypersensitive, equals
six months to the ordinary criminal who, even if he is a
“ first-timer ” here has probably been “ in ” somewhere
else. And the “ Silvertail’s ” family perhaps suffers a little
more than the common “ crooks.”
rOn the other side ; if the educated man has greater
temptation of opportunity he is (or should be) also better
armed bv his education to resist temptation . if the
“ Silvertail ” has a greater chance of avoiding detection
bv his cleverness, that is a strong reason why, when
detected, he should get an extra heavy punishment,
followin''' a well-known and logical principle of law ; as to
the fine feelings of culprits, the law shouldn t take fine
feelings into account at all, for how can it gauge them,
unless by the very unsatisfactory way of holding that
Balbus the bricklayer is necessarily a common rough
person and Nero the Emperor a gentleman of most
refined feelings?—Ed. 8.l _

THE CARE of the HAIR
“ ]y[y hair is grey, but not with years ; so
says Byron, and how applicable this is to many of
us who are distressed by personal and business
worries. Still no trouble ever yet obtained for
which some relief has not been found.
The hair is one of Nature’s adornments and
should not be neglected ; proper attention snou c
be given to its preservation, just as you find it
necessary to attend to any "physical ailment.

Kipling’s aunt, Lady Burne-Jones, is a
pro-Boer, and when the news of Peace came she
hoisted over her residence at Rottingdean a black
flag inscribed in white letters: “We have killed
and also conquered.”
; A Tory politician, put hard to the question
t’other night, could only instance one thing which
had certainly deteriorated in N.S.W. politics
since the advent of the Labor party—the whisky.
The spirits supplied at Parliament House are
certainly poor, but the politicians, whether from
abstinence or from lack of cultivated taste, don’t
complain. Reporters, however, refuse to suffer
in silence.
The postal chiefs in Melbourne are just
now grappling with a question raised by some
unknown miscreant in Java. The officials pride
themselves on the certainty of their delivering a
letter, provided the address is at all legible, but
the sender of the missive has so far baffled them.
It is addressed, ‘‘ To the richest man in Mel-
bourne, Australia.” As it is probably a begging
letter none of Melbourne’s plutocrats rush to
claim it.
The big-drum party has scored another
triumph. At Gunnedah (N.S.W.) an elderly man
arose in the intoxication of the red jersey and the
hysteria of the blaring cornet and the greasy
torch, and declared that he had committed two
minders in Queensland 28 years ago (bones
are hard to find after 28 years), and killed
another man 10 years ago (even after 10
years the skeleton is generally beyond identifica-
tion). Ihe 28 year-old bodies were burned, and
their ashes have probably blown to Guam by
y l . oW ’J lnc ' khe 10-year-old corpse was tipped into
the Warrego river, so its present address should
now be South Pacific Ocean generally. Salvation
Army confessions are an interesting study. The
converts rise one by one to tell what bad charac-
ters they were before they were converted, and the
man who was the worst sinnergets the biggest round
ol applause. The individual with no big sins gets
no applause. It is painful to hear a comparatively
respectable person, who never did anything bad
in his unregenerate days, trying, to arouse a shud-
der by telling how he used to neglect his wife and
speak unkindly to. the house-cat when he came
home at night, while the red congregation looks
at him m stony approval. Sometimes a perfectly
harmless individual loses his head and begins to
invent sins for the effect of a little fleeting popu-
‘anty. Ihe confessions of the converts are the
Army s principal dissipation, its Newgate Calen-
dar, its Police Gazette, its Chamber of Horrors,
its visit to the morgue, its moral drunk generally’
And when the man with three dead bodies on his
sou is only eclipsed in popularity by the man
with four dead bodies, the natural result is that
the man who is responsible for five dead bodies
may turn up any time.

*'* * *
Derby colt Strata Florida, at the top of Melb.
Cup prices and already well backed, did not do at all well
when seen but a couple of times lately. In one race,
Strata Florida was third to the favorite, Scobie’s Bon
Voyage. Strata figured again, Saturday last, in Malakoff
Stakes, and, though he ran well, his stable-chum Seclu-
sion won ; all of which means that, unless S.F. comes on
a lot and does something acceptable soon, he’ll drop con-
siderably in the Derby and Cup quotations. Seclusion’s
success was noteworthy, for behind her were Footbolt,
Kinglike, Ampier, and other good ones. She is a six-
year-old mare, by Carnage from The Nun, and belongs to
Mr. J. Wilson, jun. Her first race was last season, and
then wins were scored at Maribyrnong, Mentone and Bal-
larat. Seclusion has 6.13 in Caulfield and 6.8 in Melbourne
Cups.
* * * *
Extraordinary ill-luck of Sydney nag T.M.S.
owned until last week by bookie Oxenham, has set turf
scribes suggesting an alteration in the prad’s name—the
initials of a close friend of Oxy’s—a lawyer who was
a politician and at one time chairman of Sydney Tatts
Peculiarity of T.M.S.’s racing is the remarkable con-
sistency with which he runs second. Good, bad or in-
different company is the same to him. Oxy dropped a
fortune on T.M.S. and the bejewelled one has no more
fruitful source of revenue, for every second points to the
likelihood of a sure success next time, particularly when
the opposition doesn’t seem so dangerous, but the result
is invariably the same. Last week T.M.S. ran second to
Winoline at Moorefield and a few days later money went
on him in stacks and this time he struggled home—
another second—close behind Patronage.
A largely hypocritical inquiry, by the House of
Lords (of all people !), re the bearings of the Betting Evil
has resulted in a memorandum to the effect that betting
“ ought to be restricted to the actual scene of racing and
athletics.” In short, the committee of noble earls has
unintentionally advocated the totalisator on racecourses
for the relief of the large army of infatuated punters.
Tom, Dick, and Harry, under present conditions, lose
about 40 per cent, of their annual aggregate investments
to Ikey Mo, whereas they would lose not more than 7A
Eer cent., or 10 per cent, at the most, to the respectable
etting machine. Also, the temptation to bet with illicit
books “ in town ” would be reduced, if not abolished, by
the discovery that Ikey was an absurd robber compared
with the tote. Therefore Ikey would, in a great measure
perish for want of business, and thus the temptation to
punt, punt, punt would be further reduced. But the
House of Lords has nob begun to think of the Betting
Evil as an argument in favor of the tote.
* * * ,
If a mare is to win Caulfield Cup this year
handicapper Dakin must surely fancy the chance of
Verne. In his estimate, she is entitled to 7.7 over two
miles, whilst his rival, Kelly, gives her the postage-stamp
of 6.12, with half-a-mile less to travel. A rising six-year-
old daughter of Bill of Portland from She, the staying-
power should be there. Though mares are generally
kittle cattle in Oct. and Nov., it looks as if the
V.R.O. man is giving the public a tip for the first leg of
the double. Events will prove if the cute Kelly or
veteran Dakin is the better prophet.

Handicap, Saturday, Patronage is more than a possibility
for coming Metrop. or one or other of the two big Cups
He has 8.2 in the Metrop., 8.3 in the Caulfield, and 8 2 in
the Melbourne Cup. Prior to the latest, Patronage had
two wins to his credit—Rosehill August Handicap and
Newcastle Mayor’s Cup ; he also ran a good second when
San Fran annexed the Metrop., and should have won but
for bad luck. Patronage is by Grand Flaneur, and full
brother to those sterling gallopers Ruenalf, Patron and
Patroness,

* * * *
S.A. Parliament is asked to repeal, in case of
large and bona-fide clubs, the existing law which will not
allow (outside of Adelaide) two clubs to exist (or to use
the totahsator, which is the same thing in practice)
within 20 miles of each other in a straight line. The rule
works very hardly in one or two cases that come just
within it, where the distance by road is well over the 20.
A clause tacked on to this Bill seeks to allow book-
makers once more to bet in public, though only on a
racecourse and when the tote is actually at work.

The keen struggle for places on S. A. Jockey
Club committee was astonishingly close at the finish.
Veteran Ben Rounsevell was dropped, but got 76 votes,
and the other four were all between 80 and 82. W. Pile
was head of the poll, S. J. Jacobs one vote behind, Harry
Bickford and Hugh Dixson, M.P., tied at 80, and chairman
Baker gave a casting vote tor the former, as the old mem-
ber. Thus Jacobs, president of Tattersall’s, is the only
new member.

First news of fifth test-match (Australia v. Eng-
land) this tour at Kennington Oval is awaited as The
Bulletin goes to press. Though the rubber already
belongs to Australia, interest is keen.. English side chosen
differs from that of immediately preceding test. Hay-
ward, .Jessop and Hirst supplant Abel, Tate and Ranji.
Inclusion of first-named two looks like strengthening
the position, but substitution of Jessop for the
Indian is unaccountable, unless it is that
latter has fallen off in county matches lately,
for, inside a month since, he had the batting average over
Trumper, which made him top-notcher for the season up
to then. Kennington hasn’t been a lucky venue for Aus-
tralians, as of 9 tests decided there they’ve only won one
(by 7 runs in 82), two were drawn, and six went England-
wards Apropos, it’s worth dwelling on that the two
men (f ry and Hirst), whose non-inelusion among Mac-
laren s lot caused his team to be flubbed, in some quarters
unrepresentative, haven’t been over-much in demand for
tests—particularly Fry.
* * * *
, was ever any idea of bringing out
another Maclaren team this year it has been wisely aban-
doned. More likely that Lord Hawke’s team will take
Australia on its way home from Maoriland, and play
matches in Sydney, Melbourne and Adelaide.

• A u , stralians had an easy-thing last week against
K>.nt forces of second-class counties Glamorganshire and
utshire. First day saw two completed innings, when

• * * *
Match Australia v. Hampshire, last week, was
unexpectedly soft for former through Hampshire’s ill-
luck. Play was limited to two days. Hampshire opened
on a treacherous wicket that became faster while the
Australians batted ; then just as the home side started
out to score in its second innings rain necessitated
stopping for some time. Result was that Australia accu-
mulated 325, towards which Darling brought 116 and
Noble 113, while Hampshire’s figures were first 130 and
next 116. Australs. thus won by an innings and 79 runs.
Hampshire shaped a heap better against the ’99 team.
Scoring 393 in first essay, the county declared its
second closed at 6 for 212. Australia responded with 360
and three wickets for 92.
* * * *
There haven’t been many more remarkable
games of football than one that was played lately—an
Association match —at S. Broken Hill. A terrific dust-
storm was blowing, a bad one even for the Barrier, where
there has been only ljin. rain in ten months ! Several
times the ball was lost; and when the one in play went
out another was thrown to the umpire, while a pack of
small boys groped round in the grime for the first one.
Once a goal was kicked with the wrong ball; and another
time an agile wing player scudded round under the lee
of a particularly dense wall of moving sheep-station and,
after calling the umpire over to witness it, kicked a goal,
while the rest of the field were crying “ lost ball.” There
weren’t many lunatics out to witness the game ; those
that there were followed the ruck up and down, which
was their only chance of seeing anything.
* * * *
S.A.-Victoria toe-ball struggle, on Melbourne
Cricket Ground, Saturday, drew 8000. S.A. reps., true to
their reputation, played a fine, fast, open game and
passed with great skill. Occasionally Victoria appeared
completely outclassed, particularly in the early part of
the second half, and at one stage S.A. had 10 goals 6
behinds to the home side’s 5 goals 4 behinds. In the last
quarter Victoria, by a big effort, scored so well that S.A.
only won with 10 goals 8 behinds to 9 goals 6 behinds.
« « .
In reply to “R.J.” (Q.) The Rugby ball is
oval-shaped, the round one is the British Association ball.

“Sharkey, so signally defeated by Ruhlin, the
Dutchman, at the N. 5.0.,” writes Tub Bulletin’s London
correspondent, “ only fought in sheer revenge. He hates
the stone-heavier and 6in. taller fighter, and-showed the
‘ tears of warlike men ’ in the dressing-room. The Sailor
Boy put up a rushing, plucky fight, but the long left
jabbing told its tale, and the better boxer won. The
vanquished is said to be worth at least 100,000dols. and a
flourishing N.Y. saloon, behind the counter of which he
should now stay.”
« * . .
Bruising bustle between Paddy Martin and ex-
Broken Hill miner Mat. Mitchie, at Sydney Golden Gate
t other day, was a promiscuous maul of 14 rounds.
Martin was a whirling cyclone. He rushed head-
l°nK &na forced Mitchie to the ropes or the floor, or went
there himself, while the audience alternately shook with
laughter and screeched with excitement. Each, stoutly-
made, strong, and well-conditioned, stood the crash
without flinching, Martin doing all the roughing, while
Mitchie had his work cut out stopping the other’s mad
rushes with either shoulder or hip, and driving in a punch
as opportunity offered. While swinging in the fifth
round, he fractured his left hand on Martin’s bonv elbow
and, forgetting the occurrence, used the same member
again in the same way, and suffered another break. Still
Mitchie struggled, always on the defensive now, till he
found the task hopeless and caved in. It was
Paddy Martin who gave the late Otto Cribb the
toughest fight of his career, when Cribb was serving up a
sensation weekly, buc then Martin brought a bigger stock
of force and vim to bear, and was more effective than
against Mitchie.
* • * *
Australia’s lightweight pug premier, twice
married' Jack M'Gowan, is falling off some, and may go
under if he stays in the game a bit longer. Last three
. 8 saw a .U ou ti with success anything but assured
till it actually did come. Frazer made things uncomfort-
ably hot in his two battles ; and Palmer, last week, was
unquestionably best till a weighty left lifted him clean
from the perpendicular to the horizontal. . Palmer
made the mistake of taking too many chances
by forcing the game from the outset, but
will probably act differently, and consequently
do much better, when they get together again. Mean-
while, M'Gowan has to tackle another tough task in the
Sydney lad, “Rbllo,” as tricky as they make them, and
the owner of a head that sizes up things, and ducks out
of danger automatically. To win, the champion will have
to fight hard. M'Gowan fought Oolless of Albury (NSW)
12 or 13 years ago, and, seeing him now, the dad of two
families, 'tis simply marvellous how he keeps going.
* * • *
Sydney featherweight “Hock” Keys dealt out
stoush to Melbourne’s alleged bantam champ., Harry
North, at Sydney Gaiety A. O. last week. North hung on
hoping to wear t’other down or get one on the right spot
but Keys faded away too smartly and fended too well.
Keys left shore through North’s defence straight for his
nose, which early assumed a claret color and a flattened-
out- appearance that stayed. Once a right uppercut
flashed out and Keys flopped, but bounced back in-
stanter and was pouring out biff immediately. In the
fourth round a couple of rights on the body and head
spread-eagled North for longer than 10sec., and for once
the more skilful man won.
• * » *
Saturday last, in London, Dick Cavill won the
British Half-mile supremacy in 11.50 2-ssec., with his
fellow-countryman, George Read, lm. 5 3-ssec. behind,
third equivalent, at that stage, to somewhere about 70
or 80 yards. Oavill’s time is claimed as a world’s record
and so it is under conditions existent “down under”
which call for still water unaffected by current or tide
and at least furlong laps ; but in February last, at Wan-
ganm (M.L.), with 25 yard laps, Oavill put up llmin. Bsec.
which, seeing that he “shoved off” at every turn isn’t
quite as good as his English achievement. His second (by
3 seconds) to Billington in the 500 yards championship of
England a few weeks back, when he traversed the journey
in 6.28 2-ssec., was the speediest swim Dick ever got
through, and 6 3-ssec. faster than the prior world’s best
Next came his defeat by a touch for the sprint cop-
notchership in 60sec., which squelched all previous per-
formances. Pity, seeing the excellent form Oavill is in
just now, that there are only two more blue-ribbon events
fc ? b ® 1 e ® 1 ? ed —the 220 and 240 yards—both of which
should fall to him unless his compatriot Lane proves too
quick over the shorter journey.

Victorian rifleman Grummett’s win of the
Service Rifle Association Cup with 79 out of a possible 80
was an excellent performance. The score must be made
in eight shots at 300 and 600yds., and event is re-
stricted to members of National Rifle Association. Last
year saw 922 compete for the 95 prizes, worth £250 and
more competed this year. Grummett went to England
as a free lance. °

Recently deceased Sydney fat stock salesman,
Mr. J. G. Maiden, was, just 10 years back, when Australa-
sian athletics were at their best, among this continent’s
fore-front athletes. He first gathered in the club
supremacy of Darlinghurst Harriers, since merged into
East Sydney district club. Later, he closed on 100 yards
supremacies of Victoria and N.S.W. J.G.M. was short,
thick-set, and a natural sprinter. Bright’s disease is said
to have caused his death.

Quotations Supplied to the Trade
SOLE AGENTS FOR AUSTRALIA.
A Sydney printing firm has obtained a
judgment of nearly £6OO against N.S.W. Govt,
for compensation for the compulsory closing of its
shop during the plague outbreak in April last.
When the Board of Health closes and cleans up a
place which has become, a menace to the com-
munity, the tenant or owner can claim compensa-
tion from the State for doing what he should have
done himself ! It doesn’t exactly encourage
sanitary energy in private persons.
A recent N.S.W. Parliamentary return
gives the cost of Sunday work in the various
departments for the,year as follows : _
Chief Secretary, £12,707 9s. 3d. ; Treasury, £15,006 12s.
Bd. ; Public Works, £646 ; Lands, nil; Public Instruction,
£5OO ; Attorney-General aifd Justice, £8250 ; Mines and
Agriculture, £440. Total, £37,550 Is. lid.
And the Government which authorises and carries
on this wholesale paid smashing of- the holy Sab-
bath forces the police to hunt the widow who
sells a banana or glass of milk on Sunday as a
criminal breaker of a sacred Ordinance.
More “identification” farce! A couple
of employees of different Melb. drapers, on whom
valueless, chequed had been recently parsed by a
“stylish-looking lady,” were lately taken by a
smart ’tec. to view a perfectly innocent woman,
who protested that she had for some time not been
near the shops in question. Both employees were
so positive of her identity with the “wanted”
lady that she was arrested and remanded from
time to time for over a month. Then the d.’s.
stumbled across the real culprit, who didn’t re-
semble the other a little bit. The ill-used, sus-
pected woman happened to be the wife of a well-
known citizen; had she been otherwise one can
imagine that the police authorities might,
expended less sole-leather in pursuing their invea- ■
tigations once they had made an arrest.
The House of Representatives has decided that
no State politician can become a candidate for the
Federal Parliament without first resigning his seat
in the State Parliament, the reason assigned being
that his free railway pass as a State member would
give him an undue advantage over other candi-
dates. But the Federal politician also has free
railway transport, and he has no scruple about
travelling free on the railways while fighting for
his seat against the ex-State member who has to
pay his own fare. No man should hold a seat in
both Parliaments; but the present idea isn’t
aimed at that. It looks more like an effort on the
part of the men in possession to penalise and dis-
qualify those who are most likely to compete
for their seats.

A Green Plag Ahead.
Ip Victorian Railway Minister Thomas Bent has
any sense at all he will see that there is a green
flag ahead on the line just now, and slow-down on
his reckless rush to smash. There is great need
for him now to be, not unspeakable, but unspeak-
ing. Every utterance of his is an ignorant taunt,
calculated to force on a general railway strike in
Victoria. Speaking on Monday, with regard to
the strenuous objection of the railway men to
bear the burden of Government extravagance and
Kyabram dishonesty by consenting to a reduction
in wages, Mr. Thos. Bent said :
If they choose to strike, they may do so, but let them
clearly understand that if they do I shall not be caught
napping. I know what they say and think about the
difficulty of filling their places, but I have not been idle
in the matter, and can get more drivers than they
imagine. Let the men be under no misunderstanding as
to the results that will attend a strike. If they go out,
others will have to go, too. The effects of a strike would
be more far-reaching than seems to be apparent. All the
men working on the Mildura and other lines would be
thrown out, and other work would be paralysed. Why,
the effect of the strike would be worse than a war.
This is a Government without knees, and it is not going
to bend to the protests of the men in the slightest.
“ Strike ” is an obscene word in the mouth of a
Crown Minister as “ war” is in the mouth of a
diplomatist. The Victorian railway men haven’t
started to talk strike directly; it is Mr. Thos.
Bent who talks strike and dares them—practically
eggs them on —to that step. If the strike which
he seems to almost wish does come, it will not last
W66k, a/Tid. it will break M!r. J?hos. Bent s shins if
it doesn’t bend his knees. The Victorian public
know Mr. Bent asafinancierandas a politician, and
the knowledge isn’t- likely to lead to any marked
confidence in a railway train run by a Thos. Bent
blackleg. The Victorian public, too, will generally
sympathise with the men. Now that, more
revenue is needed it has become a question of
cutting down their wages—or making Melbourne
and other large Victorian towns pay land-tax,
from which they are now exempt. Coarsely and
brutally Thomas Bent sets forth that the Govern-
ment which he doesn’t ornament would rather
retrench the poor than make the rich pay up to
the same extent as in almost every other civilised
country. When Thomas falls in he will get little
sympathy and deserve less.
Said now in Federal circles that Bigjohn
Forrest has utterly spoiled the chance, which his
wife had made for him, of the Federal High Com-
missionership, by his blundering speeches in
London.
The Drayton Grange Royal Commission
will have some curiously conflicting statements to
investigate. Col. Ricardo, Commandant in A.,
explained that the pneumonia-stricken patients
were not removed from the transport at Albany
because it was “ pouring with rain.” Col. Lyster,
in charge of the transport, says it wasn’t raining at
the time. Acting-Minister for Defence Lyne was
reported to have stated m the House of Reps.,
when the first news came, that the press reports
were “ grossly exaggerated ”; he now claims that
these were General Hutton’s words (which he
quoted), not his own.

A mob of 121 bullocks from Womblebank
station, Roma (Q.), which were fattened on
prickly pear in its natural state, and were for
nearly two months without water, were recently
auctioned at Homebush sale-yards, Sydney, and
brought up to £l6, averaging £l3 12s. The exact
number started from the station, viz., 120, were
landed in Sydney without a lame or injured beast.
Is it the duty of a respondent to pare a
co-respondent’s corns regularly? One witness in
the Hall divorce case (Adelaide) testified that she
had only once seen the rev. gentleman who is
accused of undue familiarity, having his naked
feet trimmed by the fair hands of the defendant.
Now corns are excrescences that require constant
watchfulness. If they are only pared once they
might almost as well be left alone. _ When a man
takes off his boots and socks and lies back in an
easy chair, while a lady takes his feet firmly but
tenderly under her arm and fixes them up, it may
be pleasant enough, but if it is a solitary episode
it must be regarded rather as a sign of tenderness
than as steady medical attention. It is pretty
and agreeable, but it isn’t chiropody. The co-
respondent who has found a respondent who really
knows how to look after his feet, and will attend
to them patiently twice a week, has found a
treasure. Most respondents are frivolous and
unbusiness-like, and lavish their attentions on the
top end of the co-respondent, where they aren’t
really wanted.

charged with smoking on the railway platform'
urged m defence that, although he was standing
on the platform, he was really smoking inside a
properly-labelled smoking carriage. His head and
shoulders were inside the windows of the com-
partment while he was alternately engaged in
smoking and saying “goodbye” to a friend
1 urther, lie said, a man was sitting in the carriage
with his head out of the window blowing clouds
of smoke on to the platform, and that, in his
opinion, constituted smoking on the platform
much more than his actions did, as the combustible
end of him was m a Government place set apart
for smoking. Ingenious, but he was fined all the

Health is Wealth
FREEMAN & WALLACE
Surgical IpStitOtc.
The Two Men of Especial Eminence in
Pathological Problems who have the
Largest Practice in Australasia.
To the Readers of the Common-
wealth.
It is always necessary, when introducing to the
public notice any new subject, that the introduc-
tion should be clear and complete. In calling
your attention to the literature of this page, our
IS
I
fd
vm
i
HOWARD FREEMAN,
Member Dr. Harmon’s College, Washing-
ton, Cor., Cal., U.S.A. ; Professor of
Electricity; Founder and Director of
the Institute.
course is more than clear. Messes. Freeman
and Wallace have long ago been introduced to
the populace of Australia, but there are many
matters connected with the great Institute con-
trolled by them of which the public have not the
most intimate acquaintance. We are desirous of,
describing to you the outward appearance and
the inner workings of their great national establish-
ment, the Electro-Medical and Surgical Insti-
tute, a splendid photograph of which is here dis-
played. In addition, a few words will not he too
reiterative, explanatory of the international
eminence enjoyed by its governing staff of
Specialists.
There are two well and widely known men con-
nected with this great INSTITUTE.
The handsome illustrations will afford the
reader an opportunity of judging them, and a
glance at the qualifications will .complete the con-
viction that their eminence has been gained by a
life long study in the dark pathways of human
suffering and devastating disease. It is in these
dark pathways of existence that true Experience
is gained, it is there Theory shows its uselessness,
and Practice its utility. Practice embodies Ex-
perience ; Experience, Success; and Success,
Eminence. Thus we have Messrs. Freeman and
Wallace standing pre-eminent in the Southern
world as Medical Specialists in Nerve, Brain,
Fiver, Heart, Lung and Chronic Blood
Diseases. Their fame is essentially an evolution
of profound individual research and refined in-
tellectual thought of a most advanced and prac-
tically scientific nature.
The experience and popularity enjoyed by
Messrs. Freeman and Wallace is not evolved
by the passage of a few days. Although a man
may awake to find himself famous, that fame is
always the result of great application and
patient endeavour. Many long and arduous
days, nights, and years of earnest toil and hopeful
research lead up to the dawn of the final famous
morning. This has been the case with these great
Specialists; they have been for a great number of
years before the public of Australasia, to whom
they are as well known as the Premiers and high
political luminaries of each Federated State.
There- is no doubt as to their skill; no question
of their eminence ; their ability in healing certain
classes of diseases each day ensures their deathless
popularity.
Conjunctive with the great international posi-
tions attained by them in the fields of Specialism,
is the scene of their scientific labors, the Electro-
Medical and Surgical Institute. This great
and imposing building is an exposition of the
Specialist’s skill, as well as an impressive struc-
t oral admission of their abilities. It is handsome,
pretentious, elegant; splendidly adorned exter-
nally and internally ; it is replete with all modern
facilities, its usefulness and necessity is charac-
terised by great popularity and professional
renown. This is a renown which has arisen from
the efforts of two scientific lives which have
never known one failure in the treatment of a
pathological disease, or in the elucidation of a
pathological problem.
To accurately describe so immense an Institu-
tion would infringe upon our limited space, but
to satisfy the curiosity of the reader, a few of its
chief features will be treated. The accompanying
illustration, etched by one of Sydney s leading
photo-artists, will show that the building is three-
storied, occupying a large area of ground, and is
pleasantly situated beside the most picturesque
floral domain of Sydney —Hyde Park. Here
thousands of people congregate for recreativ
purposes during the hot summer months, and in
the calm of the twilight evenings, and as they rest
beneath the calm glory of the Southern stars,
their attention is attracted to the great Institute
adjacent, which tells them of a way of escape
from physical torment, and which points out to
them the paths to follow to obtain bodily peace
and mental rest.
The outward appearance of the Institute is
always superlatively grand, the various entab-
latures of blue and gold and silver which adorn
the numerous facades, standing out m handsome
and well-assumed relief. Artistic taste and
execution dominates the entire external adorn-
ment upon which the names of the Specialists
stand out in letters of gold and blue. Ihe
base of the building contains the Pharmacy,
known to the public as the Owl Drug Store,
where the medicines required by the Institute are
dispensed. This department, which contains all
the drugs which a first-class Pharmacy requires,
is supervised by a member of the Pharmaceutical
Society of N.S. Wales, under whom is a staff of
assistants. All the drugs are imported by the
Institute. The Pharmacy is handsomely decorated
in blue and gold, the ceiling being a work of art
in the above charming colors. Plate-glass mirrors
gleam from the surrounding walls, while the front
of the Pharmacy, with its massive white marble
pillars and vestibule, calls for general admiration.
On entering the vestibule, through stained-glass
doorways, with its tessilated mosaic pavement,
we are prepared in a manner for better tilings
upstairs. On ascending these stairs, covered with
rich, soft Brussell’s carpeting, we find ourselves on
the first floor landing, which comprises the waiting-
room, the consulting-room, and laboratory. r l. he
telephone department is also on this floor, as well
as an office for a typewriter immediately in
attendance on the Specialists. The waiting-room is
quietly and tastily furnished, there being nothing
of a gaudy nature, but everything of the best and
choicest quality. ' A few good paintings adorn the
walls, whilst the mantelpiece is surmounted by an
elaborate mirror, which imparts a pleasing air to
the scene.
Leaving the waiting-room, we find ourselves
requiring minute observation, and one of the
finest instruments for this purpose of exploration,
manufactured by Arnold, of London, occupies a
conspicuous position on a moveable platform so
that it can bs available for use during night or
day. Space will not admit of further details of
this department, but the order and cleanliness
patent to the most casual observer cannot fail to
strike the eye ; and we feel as we reluctantly quit
this room that everything that can be done to
further scientific research has been accom-
plished.
On the next floor, approached by an elegant
flight of stairs, is situated the office of the
manager, with his staff, _ and the department
where the correspondence is carried out. This is,
of course, the most busy-looking room m the
building—if, indeed, any room can in a sense be
called more busy than another—where all are en-
gaged in faithfully carrying out their various
duties; still, one is struck with the active energy
which pervades the scene. Quite a number of type-
writers are at work, and these wonderful machines,
clicking beneath the nimble fingers of the clerks,
produce a sound indicative of cheerful alacrity.
Some idea of the amount of correspondence car-
ried on in this department may be gained from the
fact that the seven type-writing machines are
actively in use. No such word as procrastination
is known. Quite recently one mail brought letters
from patients at Hong Kong, Suva (in the Fiji
Group), Glasgow (in Scotland), Gibraltar, South
Africa, and Newhaven (England). This will at
once demonstrate that the ends of the earth, so to
speak, are in communication with these celebrated
Sydney Specialists. The public has no idea of
the augmentation of the correspondence during
the last few years, but, when the above facts
are considered, the realisation of this assertion
will be comparatively easy.
All branches of the building are in communica-
tion by telephone with the staff of Specialists.
One important department is the Operative
Theatre, where the most delicate operations take
place daily. This'chamber is replete with all the
most modern requirements tending to successful
and efficient operative skill.
The packing department is a wonder in itself ;
each week sees hundreds of bottles of valuable
medicines transferred through the post from the
Institute. So careful is the packing and trans-
fer executed that no breakages are ever reported,
and no one but the persons to whom they are ad-
dressed have the least knowledge of what the
package contains. This is a fact worth remem-
Bering by those who do not care for publicity oi
their ailments.

TRAMS STOP AT THE DOOR.
in the sanctum of the presiding Specialists,
Messrs. FREEMAN AND WALLACE. An
elegant apartment, indeed, with its numerous
desks, and glass-cases filled with all the most
modern requirements for surgical as well as
electrical work. A magnificent multiple-celled
battery catches the eye—by Gaiffe, of Paris—a
Scientific expert in the manufacture of such
powerful machines, and another by Fleming, the
famous American Electrician. We cannot enter
into details of this chamber, but the hand of the
art decorator, as well as that of the artistic
upholsterer, is evident on all sides. We must not
omit to mention a most important factor here, we
allude to the Medical and Sur-
gical Diplomas which hang on
the Walls, bearing testimony to
the skill of the Physician-in-
Chief, Dr. Wallace, and which
afford convincing proof that we
are in the presence of one in
every respect qualified for the
position which he has gained
in Medical and Scientific
circles. Certificates of Registra-
tion by the Government Medical
Board accompany these and attest
the legality of qualification, and
the rights of those possessing
them.
The analytical laboratory is de-
serving of special attention, as
here are conducted operations of
vital importance, both chemical
and microscopical, the various in-
struments and test-tubes neces-
sary for chemical analysis have
each their particular location here,
while retorts, sand baths and
chemical troughs, denote the
labors carried on.
Analysis, whether qualitive or quantitive are
leading features in this establishment, so that
nothing escapes the watchful eye of the operator ;
the “ spectrum ” even, being frequently called into
use, when a diagnosis respecting minute mineral
substances, such as arsenic, antimony, and mer-
cury are required.
We have been informed that mercury m no
shape or form is used in any preparation leaving
this establishment, and the importance of this
assertion will- be readily understood by sufferers.
The microscope also finds its place here, as
chemical analysis is not deemed sufficient in cases

500-page Medical Work.
An Xmas Literary Present-
Since the inception of the Institute, it has been
famous for its medical publications. Several
editions of “Rescued at Last,” an exhaustive
and profusely illustrated work were sold out.
This treatise was followed by “ Clinical Experi-
ences,” of which several thousand volumes in
several editions were circulated throughout
Australia. Both these works were well re-
ceived by the public and press, but useful
and instructive as they undoubtedly were, they
stand but poor comparison beside
the HANDSOME AND VALUABLE
edition which is now in the press,
and which will shortly be placed
before the public. This work, of
which the accompanying illustra-
tion affords a faithful pictorial
view, will retain the title “Clinical
Experiences,” of which previous
work it is a greatly enlarged and
revised edition. Its revision is
according to the requirements of
the law, which forbids, in the
interest of the public, any men-
tion of certain classes of diseases
which are termed private.
This great u'ork is handsome
in appearance, bound in blue cloth
with gold embossed reliefs, and
will make a pretty and useful addi-
tion to all household libraries. It
w’ill be The Chief Book in any
house where it may enter. A
child may read it, the most
fastidious may peruse its pages,
while those who are ignorant of
the pathological conditions of
their systems may become enlightened by
reading its instructive letter-press. It con-
tains 500 pages and 100 illustrations, showing
the internal organs in various positions ; together
with a description of the ravages of disease
affecting them. Several full-page plates show-
ing the muscular, nervous, and bone systems,
with twenty pleasing and artistic illustrations
of the exterior and interior of the Institute are
enclosed within the pages. These will impress
the reader with the vastness of the Institute,,
and will show the various departments in which
the Specialists pursue their important studies.
In addition to a review of almost every disease
man is heir to; the book contains useful chapters
upon certain subjects, the information imparted
being of a most valuable nature. These chapters
teach the reader how to manage a sick-room,
what to do in cases of drowning, suffocation, burns
and scalds, accidents, snake-bites, sunstroke,
apoplexy, plague, etc., etc. There is no gauging
the benefit such a work must confer upon any
person fortunate enough to possess it.
A Gift to the People.
The Specialists of the Institute, recognising
that persons who live in the remote portions of
Australia, where medical aid is unavailable, re-
quire a work of this character, have resolved to
publish it and present it to their patrons PRACTI-
CALLY Free of Charge. It is a handsome literary
Christmas present to the patrons of the Institute
from its Proprietors, and copies of this most valu-
able work will be posted free to Any Address in
the Commonwealth on receipt of sixpence in
stamps. There is no question that it is a hand-
some and most instructive presentation book, the
best of its kind ever published in Australia, and
as orders are rapidly rolling in, no time should be
lost in applying for a copy of its first edition.
All applications should be sent direct to the
Institute, addressed to Messrs. FREEMAN
AND WALLACE, Australia’s Leading
Specialists.

SPSS'S
RICHARD WALLACE,
M.Dr, L.R.C.P., L.F.P.S., L.M.R.C.E,; late of
the' Medical Staff, British Army; and
late Consulting' Staff, Homoepathic
Hospital, Melbourne.
A little while ago we spoke of Electricity as an
adjunct to the medical treatment of diseases, and
as Messes. FREEMAN AND WALLACE are
devout believers in the efficacy of Electricity as
a remedial agent, they have fitted up an entirely
new instalment of Electricity in connection with
medical baths. It has been found that in a variety
of chronic cases, when the condition of the patient,
so to speak, is one that the regular medical reme-
dies will not effect a cure, that something extra
is required in order to cause the necessary absorp-
tion of the medical agents. For this reason Elec-
tricity has been found to act admirably in
conjunction with medicines. Messrs. FREE-
MAN AND WALLACE were the first Specialists
to introduce moist and dry Electricity into their
business, the results of which have been very
gratifying to the patients and to the Specialists.
In cases of Indigestion or Dyspepsia, arising
in many instances from an altered condition of
the nerve centres supplying the Stomach, Pan-
creas, and Spleen, and extending to the Liver,
and very often implicating the Kidneys and
causing any amount of DEPRESSION OE
SPIRITS, with melancholic tendencies; Elec-
tricity again finds a large field for its benefi-
cial influences. Hence, when during an altered
condition of the nerves of digestion, the power to
dissolve the food, so that the nutritious portions
are rendered fit for absorption into the blood, is
interfered with, often to an alarming extent,
we can artificially supply the required nerve power
by introducing Electricity into the system.
This subject is so full of interest that much
more might be said concerning its influence, and
the book already referred to, “Clinical Experi-
ences,” contains, amongst many interesting sub-
jects, a chapter alone dedicated to Electricity.
Anyone suffering in a way which may lead him
to believe that Electricity may assist his vital
powers, should send for one of the famous Electric
belts which are kept in store by the Specialists,
and which have been guaranteed in accordanee
with a most rigid electrical test. They will revive
the drooping spirits, re-strengthen the nervous
system, revitalize degenerating powers, and im-
part vigor and happiness where often the sufferer’s
case has appeared hopeless. The prices are
reasonable, and the electrical influences great and
permanently effective.
Professional Jealousy.
It is a fact that jealousy arises from a vivid
cause. Many members of the medical profession
of Sydney evince their dislike of the modus
operandi of Specialists and their doctrine,
Specialism. It is the proudest moment in the
Specialist’s life when he knows that he has
caused a professional jealousy to exist through his
superior knowledge and treatment of pernicious
and problematical diseases. The INSTITUTE
has always excelled in this superiority. A proof of
excellence is contained in the valuable testimonial of
Sir Dominic Corrigan, Bart., late Physician to
the Queen, who knew personally one of the lead-
ing Specialists of the Institute. Sir Dominic
says -“I entertain a high opinion of Dr.
Wallace, both personally and in respect of his
professional attainments.” While such profes-
sional asservations are given, professional jealousy
is easily accounted for.

A. MIDDLETON, General Manager.
Mt. Lyell: “ There is nothing whatever in the
appearance of the mine generally to warrant the con-
tinuous fall in the shares.” So say the daily newspaper
men. They may or may not know that the present price
is justifiable only on one of two contingencies—amalga-
tion with North Lyell or a sharp advance in the value of
copper. As both of these events seem imminent, the
people who are selling the shares may not be acting too
wisely, unless they are working on the principle that by
selling small lots at low figures they may be able to scoop
m larger lots at about the same rates.
* • * *
Last fortnight the Moon group of mines,
Bendigo (New Moon, South New Moon, and Clarence)
paid £5900 in divs. This average, it is thought, will be
maintained throughout the year, and, in the case of the
first two, for some years to come.
• » * *
Glenfine South (Vic.) has again come into
•prominence through the picking-up of the pay ore chute
in the 408 ft. level south. The lode is reported to be from
7to Bft. wide, and payable right across. Mine not likely
to justify the big expectations of two years ago, but ought
to became a steady producer. The length of pitch of the
pay chute having been determined beyond question,
regular returns will be forthcoming if the directors adopt
the proper system of keeping development work well in
advance of stoping. The temptation to devote all energy
to breaking pay-ore and distributing every available
penny in divs. is great, particularly where market
interests are strong, but Glenfine South directors profess
to be proof against these. They will be judged by then-
actions and not by their professions within the next six
months.

A unique record: “Forest Creek Goldfields,

* * * *
Hustler’s line of reef, Bendigo, showing up
splendidly. Hustler’s No. 1 co. during past fortnight
crushed 376 loads for 13700 z. Total crushings from new
reef, on which only level driving has been done so far,
1303 tons for 31940 z., average over 2oz. Sdwt. per ton.
Within three months co. has paid £II,OOO in divs. The
reef on which it is operating is supposed to be a con-
tinuation of that from which, in the early ’7o’s, the
Extended Hustler’s Cos. and its “ Pups ” got such sensa-
tional returns. The Extended Cos. seems to have an
excellent prospect at 2900 ft., the greatest depth reached
on this line. A winze is being sunk through a lode
formation, some of the spurs in which show good gold.
Cos. about to start driving at 700 ft. from Latham and
Watson’s shaft to try to pick up the Hustler’s No. 1 reef;
It was at Latharii and Watson’s mine that the late Duke
of Edinburgh was banquetted in an underground chamber
known as the “ jeweller’s shop.”
* * * *
Long Tunnel Extended, Walhalla (Vic.): Last
return 17510z.' from 938 tons, or loz. 17dwts. per ton.
Stone raised from below 2000 ft., and reported that two
lower levels are being opened on equally good material.
Long Tunnel Cos. likely to renew proposals for amalgama-
tion. It is understood that the Extended has never
viewed the project with a favorable eye, and if in the past
it adopted a stand-off attitude the splendid prospect
which it now has will assuredly induce it to hold
further aloof. All practical considerations favor the two
properties being worked as one, but the millennium will
come when the cos. arrive at a reasonable understanding-.
♦ * * *
Princess Royal, Norseman (W.A.), the best
W.A. mine owned in Australia, has declared its 15th div.
This brings up total distribution to £60,000 against
£13,500 subscribed capital. Main shaft is down 400 ft. and
levels opened up at 220, 300 and 380 ft. Stone at 220 ft. is
Bft. wide and is supplying mill with 17dwt. ore. Lower
levels practically untouched. Nothing fresh transpired
re sale of mine to John Bull.
* * * •
Peak Hill (W.A.), is now taking mill supplies
from an open cut 300 ft. long by 100 ft. wide. Eyes been
completely gouged out of mine by former managers, and
present incumbent has hard work to top the sdwt. mark.
40 head mill treats 4800 tons monthly, or 4 tons per stamp
daily. Only hope for shareholders recovering any of their
money is to increase mill and so reduce expenses, but
water supply is insufficient to warrant extension.
* * * *
At recent annual meeting of W. F. Reward
Kanowna (W. A.), chairman told shareholders that “we
have a very fine mill and a long chute of ore.” This was
swallowed without demur, although no reef had been cut
in the new main shaft, and no gold chute was proved to
exist. u As to the alleged fine mill it consists of 20 heads
of 6501 b. stamps over six years old, the utmost capacity
of which, on a comparatively soft, oxidised ore, was less
than 400 tons a month. Divs. of £15,000 to £16,000 a
year are promised. As this means over £3 a ton treated
it does not need a clairvoyant to foresee fresh disappoint-
ments in store. Cos. has already existed, by the aid of
sundry corpse-revivers, for a period of eight years. Of
divs. there have been none, and there is never likely to be
any.
• * * *
North Boulder (W.A.), reconstructed only a
year ago, is again in dock . f der repairs. In conjunction
with the Kalgoorlie Bank of England it is to start on
another stormy voyage. New co. will have 400,000 shares
of 10s. each, of which Bankites will get 55,000 fully paid
th’e' Northites 260,000 paid up to 7s. 6d. Balance of
85,000 will be held in reserve. Chairman buoyed up
desponding shareholders with numerous fairy tales
among others “ that the rich western chute of the Orova
may pass through our property.” As the Oroya is on the
west, arid the “ western chute ”is underlying still further
west, North Boulderites must be marvellously sanguine
or verdantly green if they base any hopes on the chair-
man’s ‘‘ may.”
* * * *
The Associated (W.A.) has cut a lode at 800 ft.
in Tetley’s shaft, and another at 1050 ft. in Judda’s The
former is a highly mineralised body about 6ft. thick, and
may prove of value ; blit the latter is no good, so far as
tested. . These finds are the first ore bodies discovered
below the 600 ft. level, and for that reason the most will
be made of them, but the mine is hopelessly overweighted
with its half-million treatment plant, during the exis-
tence of which it can never be made to pay.
* * ¥ *
The’ Atlas, Kalpini (W. A.), will ere long join
the list of producers. The main incline shaft is down
320 ft. and levels are well opened up at 200 ft. and 3joft
A 12-drill compressor is being erected, and the 10-head
Martin battery will be on the mine shortly. Two years’
supply of 15 to 20dwt. ore is already in sight, and this
will be greatly augmented when the rock-drills get to
u VO ,; ,Tifis mine is being splendidly and economically
handled, and, as the reef is a very free milling quartz,
costs will be comparatively low.

Westralia Mt. Morgans is eagerly pushing on
developments so as to be in a position to supply the extra
30-head mill now under erection. Main shaft is now down
500 ft. on the lode. On Guest property the shaft is to be
pushed down and levels opened up to correspond with
those on main property. July yield, 40300 z. from 3600
toifs, practically the same as immediately preceding
returns. &
"V-. * * * *
Merton’s Reward (W.A.) has been thoroughly
overhauled and put into shape since taken over from the
original owner. The 30-head mill has been practically
re-built, while 10 heads have already resumed crushing
which are as many as the scanty water supply will run.’
Kaufmann and Rothschild have exercised their option on
Merton’s 100,000 shares, and taken them over at £1
apiece. The scrip will be put on the market as soon as
the occasion is favorable, and, till they are unloaded
good returns may be looked for, whatever may happen
subsequently.
* • * *
Queen Margaret, Bulong (W. A.), is not looking
too well, and the patchiness of the mine is again being
emphasised. There is a hitch in the negotiations for the
long-promised Govt, bore, as the co. wants to hold the
big end of the stick, in the event of anything good being
found. It will be an uncommon event if they don’t hold
the whole of the stick.
• * * *
Great Boulder (W.A.) is now down 1600 ft., and
opening out at that level will be started shortly. The
main ore bodies seem to go down regularly, but
unfortunately, outside of them, the property appears to
be a blank, both to east and north. The six Merton
furnaces are now on the mine and in course of erection.
Probably they will supersede instead of aiding present
furnaces, as the mine appears to be worked pretty well to
its full capacity under existing conditions.
* * * *
Lake View Consols has beaten several records,
but none so conspicuously as thatfor ephemeral brilliancy.
It went up like a gorgeous rocket and the stick has
dropped like a lump of lead. Not long since it held the
world in wonder with its marvellous yields and, seem-
ingly, magnificent prospects. Now, its controllers are
going cap-in-hand to the shareholders asking for money
with which to continue operations. Reconstruction with
a fresh issue of 100,000 shares at ss. premium has been
agreed upon. Nothing of any value has as yet been
located in the lower workings, and great difficulty is
being experienced in maintaining the returns. Unless
a fresh discovery takes place soon the monthly output
will shortly drop to 2000 oz. Doubtless the £125,000 of
additional capital now sought will be subscribed. To
complete its record-breaking career the Consols may yet
have to acquire another property.
* * * *
Hannan’s Oroya (W.A.), besides breaking out
fabulously rich ore from No. 6 level, is still crosscutting
eastwards for the continuation of the rich chute at No. 8
level. Capt. Parkes is to be sympathised with. He has
nursed the mine for years, hampered by financial diffi-
culties, and, on the eve of success, sees all his hopes
blighted and work rendered futile by an amalgamation
"bat has nothing practical to recommend it and which he
did his best to frustrate. As Moreing invariably installs
his own men, Parkes’ tenure of office must be very in-
secure. About 1000 tons of rich ore are bagged ready for
smelting. J
* * » ■»
Paringa Consols, Kalgoorlie (W.A.), has been
lying very low lately, and the bootnsters are probably
awaiting the usual Sept, flutter ere making any more
flattering announcements. The main shaft is being
pushed ahead and is now down 1100 ft. and has 200 ft. more
to go before another search for the lode is made.
* * * *
Bewick, Moreing & Cos., who now boss most of
the leading mines of W.A., threaten to freeze out local
traders by importing mining supplies direct from the
manufacturers. Their idea is to establish bulk stores at
i?remantle and supply from there explosives, machinery,
steel, cyanide, and other leading lines as required. Store-
keepers are raging furiously at the proposal. From a
mine-owner s point of view it has everything to recom-
mend it. There is no reason why companies should not
co-operate and get the benefit of the enormous profits
made by middlemen and retailers. It is only by such a
combination that, for example, mine-owners can get on
even terms with the Explosives Trust, which now charges
Australian cos. over 30 per cent, more for blasting com-
pounds than has to be paid by S’African companies.

The average miner is handier with the
pick than mth the pen. This is how the officers
of Chiltern (Vic.) branch of the A.M A
in their last half-yearly report, deal with
