; ; • • Tbis ha , s npt bee n done and as the passing politi-
cal van rattles o er the stony roads of time we notice in
every instance that this is the first occupant to be hurled
from the window. We have from time to time to raise
this battered and slaughtered innocent from the gutter
and succour unto it new life. This political infant has
been nursed at the hustings by every candidate for politi-
cal honors, and no candidate expressed shame for the feed
bottle being nestled close to his breast, and, more cried
for its mercy and pleaded for its future. But as soon as
the public trusted full charge of the infant care to him he
dashed it to the earth and blazed his teeth defiantly at
his constituents and indulged in the sweeter devouts of
his nature.
The Spear-Parker case in Westralia has
developed curious features. Mr. Spear, editor of
the Spectator, was tried twice on a charge of
libelling' Mr. Justice Parker; then & nolle prosequi
was entered, and Spear, who seems deadly anxious
for notoriety, objected to not being further prose-
cuted. 1 he Crown then produced a very pathetic
letter from Mr. Spear’s mother, begging a friend
of hers, to use influence to have the prosecution
discontinued; and then this letter from the
Judge to the Crown :
Rex v. Spear.
Hon. R. S. Haynes yesterday read me a letter from
defendant s mother, who is greatly distressed at her son’s
position. In these circumstances, I shall be content if
you abandon the prosecution. -Faithfully yours, S. H.
Jr ARKER.
Acting on this the nolle prosequi was entered.
■“j iix ai r s not being prosecuted is awful,
and Mr. Justice Parker’s calm way of interfering
with the course of the criminal law does not lack
humor. A Royal Commission has, at Mr. Parker’s
request, been appointed to inquire into the charges
made against him, and that should satisfy all
demands for investigation. The only thing it
won’t satisfy is Mr. Spear’s thirst for being prose-
cuted.
At Poverty Point.
“ A.D.” : Re your remarks (9/8/’O2) “ that Rostand and
Gross got their respective versions of ‘ Cyrano de Ber-
gerac ’ from the same source, which in itself is a satire on
the Academy’s exaltation of Rostand’s genius.” Surely
it will be admitted that the Academy crowned the work
for its exquisite literary quality, not for its story. There
are countless versions of the legend of Dr. Faust, but
those of Marlowe and Goethe are best known. Other
versions possibly have more incident, but the first two
take the palm for literary and philosophic merit. So
with “ Antony and Cleopatra.” Sedley’s has more gore
and melodramatic effect. Dryden’s is more coherent, and
preserves the unities better. Sardou’s is more adapted
to the modern stage than Shakespeare’s version, but the
latter’s will be remembered when the others are for-
gotten. Anyhow, a Sheecago judge’s opinion on literary
matters isn’t necessarily infallible. Rostand’s letter to
a friend on the matter is characteristic : “ I confess I
stole ‘ L’Aiglon ’ from Tom Sambo’s minstrel of Spring-
field, 111. The idea of ‘La Samaritinne ’ was derived
from an aprocryphal Gospel by the Rev. Hon. G. H. Augus-
tus YVormacott, of Hartford, Conn. I may add that, at
the present moment, I am in negotiation with a Virginian
planter for the purchase of a MS., and I have caused to
be stolen from a Louisiana ship-broker a grand play on
Joan of Arc, the Maiden of New Orleans.”
Veteran R. S. Smythe was so “knocked” by Henry
Lee’s performance of Cyrano de Bergerac that he had to
express his admiration in a letter to the actor. A travelled
Frenchman who has seen the hero of Rostand’s drama
played by several artists of repute reckons that Lee is
better than anybody— except Coquelin. This sounds
strange to Australians who have known Lee only as a
somewhat heavy and mannered impersonator of historical
personages on the variety stage, but it is quite in accord-
ance with the testimony of Kyrle Bellew. Speaking of
Lee in ’96, vvhen he first brought his “Great Men” to
Australia, friend “Curly” remarked that his “imper-
sonation ” entertainment gave the public no idea of his
fine powers as an actor.
“ Melb.” : Cyrano makes too much of a draft on the
knowledge of a modern English-speaking audience. A
host of intelligent people say they don’t know what it is
all about, and it is not worth knowing. I would give a
lot of Cyrano knowledge for a little more about the Com-
monwealth. I would give a lot of French for a little more
German. Also, a lot of Latin for a little more Greek.
Like many more, I began with the wrong tongues.

Madame Lydia Yeamans-Titus, the sweet-voiced mimic
now at Melbourne Opera House, should be tolerably used
to her singing-baby turn (her trade mark) by now, as she
has been doing it almost nightly for a score or more of
years. And she has become so familiar with it that there
is not the faintest deviation in action, or difference in the
inflection of her voice, on any night. Madame was formerly
Miss Lydia Yeamans, and has probably found the devo-
tion of a beautiful though small voice to mimicry a much
more paying game than the concert platform. But the
organ s early training must have been excellent.
A.E. M. : With all the available talent Rickards has
at present in Australia, what an ideal vaudeville pro-
gramme of say, nine or ten turns could be arranged 1
bandow and Cinquevalli would, of course, be the biggest
stars with the Florenz Troupe, Peggy Pryde, Jackson
Fanuly Derenda and Breen, the Nawns, Lydia Titus, and
either Callaghan and Mack or Fagan and Byron compris-
ing the rest of the programme.
Maggie Moore seems to have picked up some fairly good
comedies in America this last trip. “The Widow from
Japan,” for instance, is a wildly improbable name-part
calculated to suit Maggie very will. She descends upon
the household of her son-in-law as a giddy actress from
Japan, and when he has palmed her off as somebody else
and gone mad after the manner of all young husbands
who descend to deceit in farcical comedies, the calami-
tous visitor turns out to be his disguised mother-in-law.
.this must be the first case on stage record of a plump lady
pretending to be the actress who used to tickle her
daughter’s husband before he lapsed into respectable
married life. And a new brand of mother-in-law deserves
encouragement, anyhow.
May Yohe, the much-advertised future duchess (of
Newcastle) whose future duke divorced her a while ago
seems to have made an awful mess of her infidelity.’
Colonel Strong, the co-respondent in the case, promised
to marry May, but a recent cable stated that the gallant
Colonel had got away with her jewellery, pawned it for
£lOOO, and sent the pawn-tickets to his mother with a
note to the effect that he would commit suicide shortly
May Yohe is another addition to the long list of actresses
who, after taking down” every well-to-do fellow who
got infatuated with them, have been eventually taken
down themselves by a bounding adventurer. The actress
and the barmaid are the representative female “ fly-flats ”
of this world. They get to know such a lot about men
that at last they believe in nobody except the most prac-
tised of deceivers. The cunning trickstress is the cunning
trickster s softest snap. 6
One London paper, the Lady’s Realm, published full
accounts of the original Coronation functions which didn’t
take place. The performance at Covent Garden Theatre
was quite severely criticised. Jean de Reszke had lost
some of his wonderful power, Melba’s singing was no
better than it ought to have been, one of the other princi-
pals was “ distinctly disappointing,” and the prices it
was stated, fell with a sickening thud at the last moment.
And yet there never was any last moment, or any per-
formance. Said that the Coronation number of Lady’s
Realm sold at fancy prices as a historical work of fiction
—exposed fiction. The market price for newspaper fiction
is always normal, except when circumstances divulge its
untruthfulness. 8
“ Nani ” : “ Vul.” (Bul. 2/8/2), re Cinquevalli’s catching
a cannon-ball on back of neck, terms it “a unique feat.”
It, daringly and cleverly executed though it be, is cer-
tainly not such. I have seen it done by others, notably
by a certain Holtum, a Dane. “Cannon-ball” Holtum
he was called, from his really wonderful manipulation of
these projectiles. He used to throw a 321 b. shot to a
height and let it fall on the nape of his neck, but he did
not retain and balance it there as Cinquevalli does. He
also caught a ball fired from a cannon. The ball had not
the initial velocity of a shell from a Krupp gun, but it
travelled fast enough to hinder anybody else from attempt-
ing the feat. Apropos, I remember a street-performer
who threw a large raw potato—previously passed round
for inspection about 30 feet into the air, and allowed it
to descend on his forehead, where it smashed itself
There are many ways of making a crust besides jour-
nalism. J

Alice Crawford got good notices in S’Africa for
her playing of the Queen-mother to Wilson Barrett’s
Hamlet. Alice is 24. The first time she was given the
elderly part (in Maoriland), she said to the august YV.B.,
“ I suppose I shall be Ophelia by the time I’m 48.” The
future is full of compensation for a stage-girl. If she
sticks to her business, and doesn’t die in the meantime,
she has the hero half mad for love of her fresh young
beauty when she’s 48. And the hero is usually a gallant
experienced boy of 60, which makes her triumph all the
more enviable.
“ A private concert given here ” (writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent) “ this ill-fated Coronation week
by Mrs. Cooper, the latest millionairess, cost in fees for
artists and artistes alone, £750. ‘La Ballade de D6sesper6,’
by Bemberg, was said and sung in duet by Sarah Bern-
hardt and Melba, accompanied by the composer. Herr
Arens, Plaugon, the great French baritone, M. Coquelin,
the celebrated French actor, and one or two others filled
the bill, which was truly great.”

Circusman Tom Fitzgerald, back from N.Q., extols
that part of Australia as an unexcelled winter
resort for shivering Southernites. His panegyric is
worthy of Artemus Ward at his best—only-that the Fitz
1S W < e . earnest. He says the cost of a trip to Japan
and back would maintain one in luxury’ up North for six
months, and keep the money in the country.
The story, published by a Yankee paper, of how Melba
discovered a wonderful Australian singer named Miss
Muriel Mayston at Monaco, looks as though it must have
been intended for contradiction. Tiib Bulletin is
solemnly informed that Miss Muriel Mayston, a Melbourne
singer, has never been out of Melbourne. Therefore, it is
certa'n she wasn’t the Miss M.M. whom Melba discovered,
if there ever was such a person.
While the cable lines were clear t’other
Sunday, a trial of speed was made on the Cape
cable to London, with Perth, Cocos, Rodriguez,
Mauritius, Durban, Capetown, St. Helena,
Ascension Island, St. Vincent, and Porthcurnow
in circuit. When all was ready Adelaide sent a
short message and leceived a reply (both via the
Cape) in three minutes !

“J.F.”: Be Sydney firm advertising for man,
to see how many of its own hands would apply. A man
out of work advertised for a smart man—“man just
leaving job preferred.” Got 17 replies, and then went
round applying for the billets which his correspondents
stated they were just vacating. At the fifth try he struck
an employer who “didn’t think Mr. Smith intended
leaving ” : but when shown Smith’s application for a new
job, the boss took the smarty on out of pique. The
“ fly ” man ended by showing the employer a point. In
fact, the latter might have known that a man who would
work such a trick would go “ cronk ” first opportunity.

There was a grand gamble open to anyone who
“ knew something ” as late as June 9th. On that day the
rate for insuring at London Lloyd’s against postponement
of the Coronation was 2s. 6d. per cent.— Boo to 1 against !
And just two weeks later the postponement was an-
nounced.
• * » *
Re loss of telegraph-forms (8., 5/7/’O2), this is a
bigger item than may be thought. At one Q. office (not
Brisbane) the annual return of messages sent and forms
supplied, for one year, showed a leakage of one hundred
thousand forms —which, at 3s. per thousand, means £l5.
What is the Commonwealth’s loss ? In a broker’s office
writer saw a pile being used for memoranda.
* * * *
Telegraph-forms are largely stolen by small
publicans who don’t subscribe to race-news-agencies.
They get news by ’phone and then transfer to a tele-
graph-form to be posted up in a conspicuous posi-
tion. The leakage must be large, as the ordinary man
uses two forms to word his message properly, and I have
myself seen a woman use seven forms, and then the mes-
sage read “ Yes Six.”
* » * •
The attention of H. Gyles Turner, late of the
Commercial Bank (Melb.), is specially directed to the fact
that numbers of (valueless) £lO notes of the defunct
Provincial and Suburban Bank are now being “put
off ” in Melbourne by some smart forgers. There
is nothing unusual about this—but it is the sort of
thing that doesn’t happen to a State Bank. By way of
another case in point: About two years ago an aged
bushman—a bush fossil from Queensland’s farthest back
—came into civilisation with his little fortune. He ex-
plained that he had found himself in possession of a good
sum of money in his middle life, and had hoarded it
away as a provision for his old age. He hadn’t been able
to add to it, but he had kept the original sum intact, and
being now past work he meant to retire on it. Then he
put the little fortune on the bank counter for safe keep-
ing. It was in notes of the Bank of Queensland, which
perished in 1866, and the paper was just as valuable
as Hannibal’s I O U. That also is the sort of thing that
seldom happens to a State Bank.

“Eller”: Re faked liquors (B. 12/7/’O2). On
our way to camp at George’s Basin (N.S.W.) once, we
bought a bottle of (ostensible) Usher’s whisky, but found
the cork, when drawn, bore the name of a well-known
N.S. W. brewing firm burnt-in. Yet the bottle, externally,
looked as if it had never been tampered with. Why don’t
bottlers attend to such a detail as the cork ?

* * * *
Auckland (M.L.) papers are agitating for the
shiling-per-week telephone, on the ground that the Id.
post and 6d. telegram have proved profitable. The pro-
posal will need serious consideration, but meanwhile the
P.M.G. of every Australasian State might make a decent
turnover by establishing public bureaux from which men
could ring-up the wife when out with the best girl, to
inform the old Dutch that there was a terrible rush of
business on at the office, which makes it impossible to
come home to dinner, my dear. Any man would gladly
pay 3d. for the facilitation of this harmless little lie.
* * * *
“S.P.”: Vic. mill-owners seem just now to be
struggling hard to ruin their own trade. Ever since the
collapse of the Flour-Millers’ Association prices have been
everywhere cut to the utmost, apparently without any
thought of the total smash that must inevitably come to
many. At present, a balance-sheet showing no loss is a
rare exception. Cash has to be paid for wheat, but the
product is sold on terms —often covering several months
— and sold below par at that. And yet people continue
to put their money into such a rotten business. Com-
panies are floated, boom for a bit, bleed the shareholders
dry, then go bung ; and another co. rises from the ruins ;
and so on afresh. The individual mill-owner can’t make
a living, and either goes insolvent or, more prudently,
shuts down. Rarely the latter, however, as everyone is
waiting “ for things to change,” but making no attempt
to help that change. A great evil is the reciprocal dis-
trust of the millers, which prevents them banding
together to bring about a wholesomer state of the trade.
• * * *
There is a big slump in butter-factory property
throughout Victoria. The once phenomenally prosperous
Fresh Food and Storage Co. is feeling the fall of late.
Home-separators have made the small dairying “ cookies”
independent of the citv octopus.

“ N. 8.” : A swaggie intending to go from Syd-
ney to Bourke (N.S.W.) told me that he would get on a
train without a ticket, and when he reached Bourke.
would do li days’ gaol. Explained that it would take six
or seven weeks to tramp it, with hard graft and little
tucker all the way, so that he would save time and get
food by sneaking a Wain-trip'.- 1

The newest thing in insolvents : A Melbourne
official assignee has filed. Main trouble —having unre-
munerative estates to deal with. Few of the estates that
go into Melb. Insolvency Court nowadays are remunera-
tive. Nearly half belong to small railway and other
public employees, who have drifted into the hands of the
public service money-lenders. 1

There promises henceforth to be a good deal of
Australian fruit shipped direct to Continental Europe
per the big French and German liners. There is plenty
of room for foreign competition in this trade, fruit-
shippers being at present not too tenderly treated by the
British carrying co’s.

A civil servant (now ex-civil servant) of 30 odd
years’ service Suffered at the hands of a tardy Nemesis in
a neighboring State. ‘ Some 20 years ago Govt, called
tenders for a supply of timber. The c.s. gave the tip to
a “ great friend,” and the great friend was successful
tenderer, and made enough out of the contract to start a
pub. Their offsprings consisted of a son each (the cussed-
ness of things—if one had only been a girl!) and the pair
both (more cussedness) grew up with a desire to go on
the land, and (stiß more cussedness) the same piece of
land. The c.s. appealed to the great friend to “ remem-
ber what I did for you once.” The g.f. admitted the
obligation, but couldn’t, in fairness to his son, forego an
application for .this piece of land. A Gazette, notice, some
little while after, showed the c.s.’s son in possession of
the land. Then the g.f. took out the hoarded documents
as to his successful tender for the timber contract, and
sent them to thy c.s.’s chief, Investigation, and the c.s.
asked to leave the department.

Something Aboriginal.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In Kendall’s poem on the “Last of his Race,” he
mentions “the nulla, the sling, and the spear.
Kendall was evidently not aware that the sling,
like the bow and arrow, was absolutely unknown
on the Australian continent. The sling was the
favorite weapon of the New. Caledonia aborigines,
and they could use it to some purpose, as was
promptly discovered by the French soldiers in
their first conflicts with the primitive inhabitants.
The intense conservatism and spirit of isolation
of the Australian tribes is nowhere more sharply
illustrated than in the northern point of Cape
York Peninsula. There I found that the Straits
islanders, who are pure Papuans, were using the
bow and arrow of New Guinea, and the woomera
and woomera spear of the Cape York Australians,
obtaining the latter by barter. Though this
barter must have continued for unknown hundreds
or thousands of years, the Australian copied
nothing from the Papuan. You expect a reason,
and I surmise that the Australian aborigine repre-
sents the oldest living race of mankind, in the
stage when all weapons are thrown or used as a
club, long before the advent of the bow and arrow.
The deltoid throwing-muscle of the Australian
aboriginal is more developed than in any other
race at present in existence. An old grey-headed
myall, or a youth of 16, or a thin eight-stone man
of any age, whose muscles are in working order,
can throw a missile with a force that the most
robust white cricketer could only feebly imitate.
The old, grey-haired blackfellow who speared
Sub-Inspector H. P: Kaye on the Woolgar, west
of Cooktown, on Sept. 14, 1881, was about 60
years of age, and certainly not more than Bst.,
yet he jerked the woomera spear from under his
arm and drove it through lungs, heart and chest,
and Kaye fell dead from his horse into the grass.
A white man was speared beside me, in 1881, on
Liverpool Creek; the black palm woomera spear
was driven through him, and penetrated the grass-
tree beside which he was standing, so that it had to
be cut out with a tomahawk.
The Australian aboriginal with the boomerang,
nulla, and woomera spear is to be credited with
making the most formidable wooden weapons men-
tioned in the history of mankind. With him, the
wooden weapon had reached the maximum
development, and there was no further stage of
evolution.
The woomera spear, when properly made and
effectively thrown, is a much deadlier weapon
than the bow and arrow. The return boomerang,
weighing from 4oz. to 90z., will either mal e
frightful gashes and ricochet, or go clean through
a man. I have seen a boomerang, striking
end-on, go through a gin. pine board as_ if
it were a sheet of paper. The Australian aborigi-
nal, being in the “ throwing stage,” has therefore,
so far, not adopted the weapons of any other race.
In the early history of Victoria, two or three
aborigines were at large, with rifles and ammuni-
tion, and did considerable damage, but their
example was never imitated. It is a curious fact
that hundreds of aborigines have since been
owners of firearms and skilled in using them, and
yet there are not more than four deaths credited
to firearms in the hands of pure-blooded abori-
gines in the last 50 years of Australian history !
The astounding conservatism which prevented
one tribe from either adopting the weapons or
learning the dialect of another, was curiously
illustrated by the distribution of the woomera
spear and boomerang, and the separation of two
dialects by a river, or range of mountains, or an
arm of the sea.
The woomera spear in use by the Sydney blacks,
and thence round the Australian continent, west-
ward and northward and eastward to the Cape
York peninsula, stopped abruptly on the east
coast at the Bellenger River, and from there
north for 1200 miles was unknown until it re-
appeared at Townsville beyond the Burdekin.
The return boomerang was absolutely unknown
in Cape York Peninsula, or anywhere north
of Mackay, until taken there by blacks from
boomerang-throwing tribes.
Three entirely different dialects were spoken
on Stradbrooke, Morton, and Bribie Islands,
though all three are in sight of each other. Three
different dialects were spoken on Fraser’s Island,
the negatives being “warr,” “wacca,” and
“cabbee.” Still, as there are three entirely
distinct languages—English, Gaelic, and Welsh —
and numerous dialects, spoken on the very small
island of Great Britain, and an astonishing
variety on the continent of Europe, two languages
being occasionally only separated by a river or
range of mountains, this is less extraordinary than
it looks.
The Australian aborigine must die with his
language and his weapons. All three will become
extinct together. It is well that it should be so,
for one is not worth preserving without the other.
They are doomed —that ancient and venerable
Trimurti.
The Australian aborigine has come down to
the present direct from the first-born of Human
Protoplasm, when it was breathed on by the spirit
of Life, rocked in the cradle of the Primeval Deep,
and lullabied by the music of wild Silurian storms
far back in the misty morning of the World.
Weirdly and unspeakably pathetic is the picture
of that rapidly-disappearing ancient race, of whom
the few melancholy survivors stand to-day amid
the oldest existing flora and fauna—the gloomy
Araucarias, the feathered Macrozamias, the black-
stemmed Xanthorrhea, the last of the Marsu-
pialia, and the Dipnoid Ceratodus, “Children of
Elder Time,” the Deep of the present calling unto
the Deep of the Mesozoic through the measureless
Ether and the awful silence of unknown ages
recorded only in the Doomsday annals of God ! !
QUEENSLAND.
ARCHIBALD MESTON.
An Episode.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Noon, on a bush-section. A thin, small-boned
wiry-looking woman at the wood-heap of a small
bush-whare, splitting a large rata-log with the
aid of maul and wedges. As she works she
listens for a cry from her sleeping first-born and
wonders wearily if her husband will return that
night or stay in the township to spend the £lO
due to him for railway-timber. A shadow falls
across the log, and she is confronted by one of the
bad class of ‘ ‘ swaggers ” —one of the men who'
have been the cause of many honest but unfortu
nate travellers being shunted.
“What do you want here?” asks the woman,
sharply.
“ Is the boss in, missus ? ”
“Yes, he’s in all right; and he’ll soon be out if
you don’t clear.”
A fitful wail is heard from the whaiA : “ Why
don’t you look after that chi’d, Tom?” cries the
woman to an imaginary man n the house.
The child’s cry comes louder.
‘ ‘ Guess Tom must be dead,” the man says with
a ghastly grin, and seats himself on the log.
The woman strikes desperately at theiwedge until
it is driven right home. The child’s cry ceases.
“I don’t want much, missus, but what I want I
mean to have. I want a good square feed, in the
first place ; some tucker to carry me on the road,
and a kiss. I might be satisfied with one; but
we’ll see about that later on.”
The woman’s face hardens suddenly, and she
thinks a second of the hammer in her hand ; and
then her eye is caught by the crack in the log. It
has crept almost to where the man is seated.
“ Meet anyone on the track as you came along?”
she asks, starting another wedge alongside the
first.
‘ ‘ Not a soul, missus; so don’t let that worry
you.”
The split travels along the log till it is an inch
wide where the man is seated. The woman’s face
softens into a pleasant smile as a fold of greasy
cloth drops into the crack. Bang! one well-
directed blow and both wedges fall to the ground.
Two minutes suffice to loose the dogs—one of
them half-bull, and the best pig-dog in the district.
Towards evening the woman might have been
seen gathering pieces of cloth from the log and
the first wire fence for patching purposes.

They have no special consideration for
Gent One in England. A leading solicitor in
Worcester, ex-mayor, County councillor, &c., has

misappropriation, though most of the big men of

The feelings that actuate some people
would puzzle Solomon. Some days ago a man at
Temora (N.S. W.) was sent to gaol for two months
(the P.M. regretted he couldn’t give him more), for
brutality to a harmless little tyke that had not
even sniffed at him as an excusable provocation.
After kicking and beating it to death, he knelt on
it, and pummelled it with his fists and knees till
every bone was c usned.
“ Paterfamilias ” writes to the Argus on
the administration of the Vic. Truancy Act—•
“ what may happen to a man in Victoria”: —
For not sending two children to school the required
number of attendances I was, at a court of petty sessions,
fined 2s. 6d. each, in default 48 hours. The children had
been sick. Having been out of employment for several
weeks, I decided to take it out. I was sent to Melbourne
Gaol to do a sentence of 48 hours for a ss. fine. I was
herded with criminals, my description, height, weight,
marks, religion, where born, what ship I arrived in,
taken down by a prisoner, after which I had to strip in
the presence of a warder, and while a prisoner was turn-
ing my clothes and pockets inside out I was told to take
a bath. Eventually I landed in a cell with two others
about 5 p.m., with my bed under my arm, a few inches of
candle, and one match, being cautioned if it missed fire
not to howl out, as I would not get another one. At 8
a.m. next morning I received my first meal for 24 hours,
a pint of hominy. In the afternoon I had solitary and
some oakum to pick. At 4 p.m. more hominy, and then
locked up without the bed, and one match, till 9 p.m.,
when I was let out.

ALONG BACK IN THE BEGINNING OF THE EIGHTIES
Ilerr Rassmussen introduced his now world-famed Alfaline Herbal Remedies to the Australian public at 547 Geotv
Street, Sydney. Australia was then flooded with American importations and proprietary remedies, manufactured
by doubtful persons with assumed names and fictitious addresses, who took the' people’s money without
responsibility, and very often without giving them fair value for same.
HERR RASSMJ3SEN BROUGHT ABOUT A REVOLUTION
by straightforward and fair dealings with the public, offering to stake his reputation and future if his remedies
were not as efficacious as he claimed them to be, accepting all responsibility, and willing to abide by the people’s
verdict.
THE HAPPY RESULT IS KNOWN TO EVERYONE.
Over twenty years have passed away, hundreds of remedies have come and gone—many of them were forced on
the public in numerous designing ways—thousands of pounds spent on advertising same with the assistance of the
world's greatest experts in laying traps for the unwary; but the public would have none of them, and they have
disappeared as suddenly as a flash of lightning.
“THE ALFALINE REMEDIES EXCELL ALL OTHERS,”
and are well known to be more popular to-day than they have ever been. They have not only obtained, but
have retained the goodwill and confidence of the Australian public, and have become famous throughout the
civilized world. The first Sydney Office not only yet exists, but offices have been opened up for their sale at
Melbourne, Brisbane, Adelaide, Wellington, N.Z., and in West Australia at Perth, Fremantle, Kalgoorlie and
Boulder City, with Agencies throughout the world,
LET THE PUBLIC JUDGE FROM THE FOLLOWING:—
The Alfaline Remedies have held their own for over twenty years in Australia, and Herr Rassmussen has
been awarded the Parisian Acadamies’ Gold Medal and Diploma of Honour ; Gold Medal, Sydney, and several Silver
Medals; honourably mentioned in almost every newspaper in Australia; received many thousands of unsolicited
testimonials—every one of the originals open for inspection at his Offices. The Alfaline Remedies have never been

i AS A SUBSTITUTE for the European medicinal spring treatment for CONSTIPA-
TION many eminent medical men and the most prominent medical journals have recom-

recommended. It exercises a beneficial effect on the liver, gall-bladder, kidneys, and
stomach. The flavor is refreshing, and it is readily taken by women and children. It has
also been successfully prescribed in diabetes and for the cori’ection of the lithic and uric
acid diathesis, with or without jaundice, constipation, acid dyspepsia, and hemorrhoids.
“ Its advantages,” says a medical correspondent, “ appear to arise from its possessing the
power to eliminate from the body the excess of bile and acids, thereby purifying the blood
and strengthening the general tone by improving the assimilative processes of the
debilitated system. Consequently it has a salutary effect in cases of hypochondriasis
associated with low spirits and general depression.”
For the above reasons KUTNOW’S POWDER has been employed, under the highest
medical authorities, for patients leading sedentary lives and those suffering from abnormal
accumulations of fat. For corpulent women, in particular, physicians will find it a pleasant
remedy when aided by the appropriate diet. The well-to-do classes, with leisure to com-
mand, periodically seek the cleansing and health-restoring medicinal Spas of the European
Continent. Of the million of sufferers, however, only comparatively very few are favored

sterling merit the approval of such authorities as Sir Spencer Wells, M.D., Professor

eminent physicians everywhere.

SYDNEY
At Her Feet.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Acland rode into town at dawn and found Doctor
Clive in bed. He sent word to him that a woman
lay very ill at Percival’s selection.
“I feel sick and nervous,” said Clive, entering
the room.
The droop of his lower lip and the redness in
his eyes told a story which Acland was not slow
to read.
“ Take a straightener,” said he, “ and, for God’s
sake, make haste.”
“Is she very ill?” inquired Clive, pouring
brandy into a tumbler.”
“Very—with fever.”
Clive raised the liquor to bis lips.
“ What name did you say ? ”
“Percival.”
Clive’s head went back, leaving his lips dry.
“Is she married ?”
“Yes.”
“ And her husband’s name ? ■”
“Percival, of course.”
“Ah!” —of course. Where do they come
from ? ”
‘ ‘ Melbourne—settled next to me six weeks ago.
Her husband’s a brute.”
“No,” said dive, in a loud voice, “only a
drunkard.”
Acland looked at him in surprise.
“ He never tastes liquor, Doctor, says he hates
it, but he treats her like a brute.”
Clive emptied and re-filled his glass. “ Power
to his arm ! ” he muttered.
Acland stirred impatiently.
“ Get your horse —it is a long ride.”
“ One word.”
"Well?”
“ Her eyes—-are they brown or blue ? ”
“Brown.”
“ Very soft, eh ? ”
“ Yes—how well you know her 1 ”
“Perhaps ; I have seen her once or twice ”
drinking half his brandy.
After a pause—
“ And her hair is brown also, Acland ? ”
“Yes.”
“ With tints —golden tints, at times.”
“ Not that I know,” said Acland.
Then Clive asked a more curious question still—
“ Has she a scar here, Acland ? ” and he pointed
to his chest.
“How should I know ?” said Acland in amaze.
“Only her husband knows that.”
“Only her husband should know,” Clive re-
marked in a quiet tone.
Then he finished the brandy, and his unsteady
hand went towards the bottle again but up stood
Acland with a gathered brow.
“Enough ! ” he cried ; “ would you let a sweet
woman die for the sake of a cursed drink ? ”
“Who made me a drunkard?’’—and at the
question the frown of impatience passed from
Acland’s brow, lifted thence by wide wonder.
The doctor moved to the window and, drawing
the blind, looked out.
“ Raining still, Acland ? ”
“ Yes. The river is up, and if we don’t hurry
the bridge at Calvert’s Crossing may be swept
away.”
Clive did not answer but continued,to gaze out.
From far away came the sound of chopping.
“ Always ringing, ringing, ringing,” he said dis-
contentedly, “making the green trees into dead
wood.”
“It is necessary,” remarked Acland.
“ But, to make green lives into dead hearts—is
that necessary, too, Acland ? ”
Acland said nothing.
“A week ago,” resumed Clive, “ the limb of a
tree he had ‘rung’ fell on Peter Henson and
killed him.”
Then he continued, turning round and ap-
proaching the other: “And I will save the
woman, Acland.”
He went to a drawer and drew from it a pink
envelope.
“With this powder, Acland,” he added, “I
will save her.”
His flushed face had paled, and there was a
smile at the corners of his mouth —a white, fore-
boding smile. Curiously enough it recalled to
Acland thB storm of a few days previous, when
an ashen wreath, livid with venom, streaked
against a background of black-blue cloud.
Half-an-hour later they rode from town toge-
ther. The doctor carried the pink powder in his
top vest pocket and near his heart, as though he
dearly loved it.
Acland hurried him persistently. Every
moment made a rise in the river, and even now
they might be too late. If that were so, it would
be good-bye to the woman. Acland pitied her.
That was what sent him from home at midnight
on a 20-mile ride for Doctor Clive. He told no
one of his purpose—least of all Percival, because
he was sure Percival would sneer.
Doctor Clive rode behind, silent, chewing a
different kernel. It rained heavily and he felt
cold. The brandy had passed from his veins, and
reaction crawling in filled him with desolation,
making him heave and sigh and yawn. Some-
times he said silly things to himself, and some-
times savage things to another, but that other
was not Acland. And sometimes he awoke and
shook off a strange feeling, the feeling that a dead
man might have who knew that he was dead.
Little by little he had wasted his will. None
of his present associates knew the reason, but
some guessed it was a woman. The pity of it. Five
years before, many mouths spoke to many ears,
saying grand things of Doctor Clive, who would
make a name for himself, a gilded name. With
D gifts God had given him, he would cheat
Death of many a victim, and take the tears from
tender eyes. But Clive had suddenly given the
lie to these good-natured forecasts.
Men went about saying, “Have you heard?”
and women said, “Ah, the hussy! she was not
worthy of him.”
For the rest, his friends pitied him, and were
sorry, and it is a good deal to say that even his
enemies were not glad.
All of this seemed very long ago, and now he
was riding through the rain to save a woman’s
life. “To save” —Clive smiled, again, and
Acland, turning in the saddle, wondered what
made the doctor merry.
The road was soft, and they found it heavy
going. The sticky clay glued their horses’ hoofs,
so that, after every lifting of them, a sound of
suction followed. At noon, they had made but
10 miles, but were near the river. They heard it
long before its murky tide rolled in front of them.
It had a voice, which it used like a giant. For
18 months the river had waited for this voice,
sleeping like a long, yellow, silent serpent beneath
its lord, the Sun. Its hollow roar rumbled now
through the trees, and the drip, drip of the rain
from the pointed leaves falling into the little
leaves lying under, was as if sound should fall on
sound.
Presently, the two men halted.
“ Gone ! ” exclaimed Acland, pointing to where
the bridge had been.
“ Gone ! ” echoed Clive.
“No use, now,” said Acland.
“ What does it matter ? ”
“Everything,” Acland replied, bitter at the
callous question. “The next bridge is 10 miles
away. ”
“Too far,” said Clive, quietly ; “what are we
to do ? ”
“Go round ; it’s the only thing.”
“No; there’s another way,” said Clive. Acland
opened his eyes and stared at him. He had the
calmness of one who cares no longer for anything.
“ But you will drown,” said Acland.
The doctor rode down the slope to the water’s
edge.
“Itis a life lost, anyway,” he replied. Acland
did not answer. He was too wrapt in the doctor’s
peril to say anything.
Clive spoke again.
“If I live—she dies!” he said. “If I die,
she lives—perhaps ! ”
The river was boiling and surging and swirling.
There was no method in its motion ; it was a mad
thing indeed.
“ Did he mean that, or is the river in his
brain ?” said Acland.
Clive did not wave his hand, nor say good-bye,
nor do anything to make him seem a hero. Acland
might have thought him one, but for the smiles, the
pink packet of powder, and the words he had just
uttered. The doctor’s horse, with the water at
the stirrups, made an effort to evade the perilous
adventure, trembling violently and turning to
regain the bank. Clive pulled him round with a
jerk and spurred him. The animal reared, leapt
forward, and next moment was out of depth. It
was going to be a great fight, and the doctor,
slipping from the saddle, swam beside the horse,
his hand on its mane.
Acland, sitting still, watched the struggle. It
was the trifles that attracted him most. The tree-
root that came down stream was one of these, and
he calculated to a nicety that it wuuld just miss
them. The red nostril of the horse as he swerved
up stream was another, and the pink thing in che
horse’s mane a third. What was this powder
intended for ? —and Acland remembered the smile.
Strange to say, while his eyes were bent upon the
struggling pair, his mind had gone tracking, and
found a faint impression among dead leaves.
They were in midstream now, and anon beyond
midstream, but here was a huge log coming down
on them—a riotous, reckless thing, leaping and
falling, like a hobbled horse in pursuit of a teasing
terrier. A chill fear struck through Clive, and he
breathed some long-forgotten words of a kind that
men refuge in when death menaces. The log
slipped by with no harm to man or horse, and
then Clive remembered that he was utterly in-
different to any happening.
A minute after, they drew out on the farther
side, and shook themselves free of wet. Then
Clive turned towards Acland and held up the
pink packet as a victor waves a flag.
Acland nodded that he saw. He watched the
doctor mount and ride away. Then he lit his pipe
and smoked. The smile and the words and the
pink powder troubled him more and more. At
length he laughed bitterly. “He went to bring
life and brought death,” he said—and ’twas of
himself that he spoke.
When a half-hour passed he knocked the ashes
from his pipe, and rode down to the river, to take
his chance, as Clive had done. The faint impress
had become a well-defined footprint.
It was late in the day when Doctor Clive came
to Percival’s selection. The plover in the near
grasses shrilled at his approach. A chestnut horse
dropped its head over a fence, and near by, on a
rail, sat a magpie, lost in dreams of fresh furrows
and fat worms. Far away a man was cutting
timber, flaking the air with sappy wood, and
filling the silence with echoes.
“ Percival! ” said Clive, “ she will be alone.”
He dismounted at the open door, and an old
woman, coming from an inner room, bade him
enter and be seated.
“ Can I see Mrs. Percival ? ”
“ She is asleep and much better. Would it be
well to wake her?”
Clive moved across the room and paused at a
door that stood ajar. “In here ?” he whispered.
The woman nodded, and Clive gently pushed the
door and followed it. Then he swung it to again,
and darkness was about him. There was a bed to
one side, and on it someone who breathed slowly
and gently. Clive listened for a moment, and his
practised ear perceived that it was the sleep of
one who had passed a crisis. But the fact did
not cause him to smile, nor to feel in any way
relieved. He only clutched the pink powder
more tightly, as though it were a conscious thing.
Moving noiselessly to a window, he arranged
the blind so that the light of the sunless day came
in. Then with slow steps, as of one very ill, he
approached the bed.
The woman did not stir as he opened the
curtains. He looked at her pale face and wonder id
what had stolen its rose tint. Her brown hair
lay massed on her shoulders. It was soft as ever
—the same that swathed his face in silk when he
was strong and young.
“I loved you,” whispered he.
The coverlid was at her throat. He turned it
down and saw the scar.
“ No child of mine,” said Clive, but it was not
of the scar he spoke.
Then, as he stood above her, the old days
trooped back and looked him in the face. One
reminded him of how he and the woman first met;
a second of their first kiss, and the perfection that
he regarded as his for all time. A third was his
marriage day, and he remembered the revelation
of the scar, and the pain it had brought him to
see such whiteness so disfigured. Then came the
bitterest remembrance of all—the sin on her soul
that was worse than the scar on her flesh.
And now hell presented him with a gallery of
pictures. He saw a life, his own, drifting from
shame to shame, and remembered incidents that
draped his heart in humiliation. He that was
fashioned to walk erect had been made to crawl
on all-fours, for in such attitude he imagined the
world saw him. All the little meannesses of
which he had been guilty and the sorry situations
that he had been in returned to him, and made
his eyes fill and his cheeks burn. But worst of
all was the terrible thirst that led to the theft
from the blind beggar at the street corner.
“Through you!” he growled, and shook the
sleeper.
She had started and opened her eyes. It had
grown dark, and neither could distinguish the
other’s features. The woman, however, sighed a
deep content and stretched her arms to enfold
him.
“I have been calling for you all day,” she said.
Clive turned to a table at the bedside and mixed
the powder.
“I could not come before,” he murmured.
“I thought I should die without seeing your
face.”
He stopped in the middle of the mixing and
breathed deeply.
“ Did you care that much for me ? ” he said.
“Bend down that I may kiss you.”
Clive’s heart quickened and he told himself that
the memory of her voice had been even less sweet
than the voice itself.
“ Then you love ? ”
Her reply came up to him softly.
“ Always,” she said.
A very low, regretful wind was feeling round
the house, saying things that conveyed a world of
sadness.
“ You love me,” he continued—“ more than you
do him?”
“Him !—who?” she breathed quickly, peering
up at Clive's face. Then, unable to distinguish
him, she called in a startled voice—“ Nancy!
Nancy ! ”
“ Hush !” said Clive. “You did not know me,
then?”
She was silent and raised herself on an elbow so
that her face came close to his. Clive felt her
breath hot on his eyebrows.
“Do you know me?”
“ Ah !” she whispered, sinking backwards.
“ So it is not me you loved ? ”
She made no reply.
“ You cheated me,” said Clive.
With a look of agony she brushed the palm of
her hand against her forehead.
“ You made me a drunkard,” he said.
“It should have been different,” she sighed,
“ but the heart will have its way.”
“ I treated you as a queen.”
“He treats me otherwise,” she said.
“ I would have died for you.
“I would live for him,” she replied, sobbing
quietly.
It was tender and fitting for the gentle rain to
sob, too, falling from the eaves without.
“You love him very much ?” said Clive.
“Very much,” she answered.
“ And you would like to live ? ”
Clive’s voice was soft now. ■
“ Ah, if it could be ! ’’—with a sudden spirit in
her tones that was like a light leaping up in the
darkness.; . .■ : : ' z :
“ I have brought you life.”
“ You—you to do so. Where ? ”
“ Here. ”, The door opened and the room filled
with light. Acland and Percival came in quickly.
‘ ‘ Take it—take it, ” said Clive eagerly, ‘ 1 it will
make you well.?’
“ Give it to me ; ” she trembled so that some of
the liquid lapped over and made a red streaking
on her fingers. She rose and raised it to her lips,
but Acland coming forward, caught her wrist with
a quick hand.
“Ha ! ’’ lie said, looking into Clive’s eyes, “ too
clever for you, Doctor Clive.”
Clive stood dumbfounded; his fingers in his
hair. Then he suddenly straightened himself,
reached for the glass, and grasped it.
“See,.it is harmless,” he said, “you have a
great wit, Acland—pity you should waste it on
little things,
He raised the glass to his lips.
“Your health, however”—and he drained the
liquid.
They remained silent for along time, the woman
looking vengefully at Acland for having robbed
her of the life-giving draught and the doctor
gazing down at her with eyes that slowly dimmed,
In a little while he began to tremble like a leaf, or
a lark’s wing in a clouded sky, and his lips to
twitch and gather as thdugh with unutterable woe.
“Shall I live?” said the woman. “Tell me,
for you must know.”
“Yes,” he answered—“but I shall die.”
Acland was watching him narrowly.
“ When ?” said he.
“ Now ! ”
Clive drooped a little, more and more, and at
last slanted sideways and leaned upon her bed.
“ At your feet, my wife ! ”
“ It was poison then ! ” whispered Percival.
“ Ah ! ” and the woman shuddered terribly.
“ But,” said Acland, “ how he loved you ! ”

PEARS
The Anguish of a God.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A god sat up in his big white throne,
Carved in ivory, chased in gold,
Sat in his dignity all alone,
Midst bloodless beauty and grandeur cold,
While a choir of thin pale angels white,
tn passionless voices sang his praise,
And a, heavenly radiance swept its light
As chill and blue as an arc-lamp’s rays
A mighty feeling that’s seldom known’
Is to be a god on a big white throne.
But this god, he shivered, and bade them cease,
As he sadly gazed on his wide domain—
Its pallid purity, paltry peace,
With never a sign of a sinful stain.
He warmed his hands at a star’s red side
That flamed with folly and bled with sin •
Then, raising his arms in his passion cried :
“ Is this the Heaven that man would win—
This ty ? Give me life,
With its wars and women, and strength and
strife.”- '
And the god looked down from his throne above,
To scan the worlds to their utmost ends.
He looked on the girls that he might not love
The men that never he’d name as friends ;
He thrilled with the wars that he might not fight,
Fretted for hate that he could not raise ;
For the sorrow and sin of the mortals’ night,
For the sun and snow of the mortals’ days.
Then he rose and cursed at the lot so lone
Of a mighty god on a big white throne.
The mortal lay on the mortal’s breast -
In the clasp of love or the grasp of hate ;
With the flush of joy and the careless jest,
When they faced their god for their final fate.
Little they cared for the laws he made ;
For the look of love and the gage of strife
They plunged to the torment unafraid,
As they gave, or they took, or they marred a life.
And the men of the world who live and die
Seemed gods to that god who ruled on high.
These things that grovelled in mire and clay
Could rise to a grandeur not his own,
Weaker was he in his strength than they,
Defying the power of his big white throne.
The god as he pondered angry grew
That joys were theirs that he could not feel ;
And he pelted the worlds with his lightnings
blue,
And smudged a few ’neath his potent heel.
As they rose through the dust in their grief and
pain,
He saw them gather and sin again.
To a greater god (if a one there were)
He knelt and did as a prayer up-raise—
That he might the joys of those creatures share
Or be a god of the olden days,
From the stifling bonds of his godhead free,
For the field of fight or the maiden’s bower—
Away from the Heaven so chill, where he
Sat impotent in his awful power.
At the foot of his throne in the upper air
The god in his anguish wept his prayer.
W.D.
A Good Quarter-of-an-hour.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A cheeky, brown minah sat on the fence. The
man paused to look at the minah and saw the girl.
A distant piano-strumming came from a house
somewhere behind the trees.
“Isn’t he an impertinent chap?” he asked,
looking at the minah.
She blushed.
“ Isn’t he ? ”
Still silence.
“You know you think he is”—
“ I know I think someone is.”
“Who?”
She smiled.
The man smiled, too. He could enjoy a joke
against himself. Then they laughed together.
“ We’ve never been introduced,” said the girl.
“ Don’t know each other’s names.”
“ What’s in ”
“ Oh please ! ” she interrupted.
“Forgive me, I’m a ‘phrasey ’ beast.”
‘ ‘ I’m, unbearable ! ”
The man stared.
‘ ‘ ‘ Butter for fat ’ —exchange of attributes. You
said you were ‘ phrasey.’
“ But— ‘ unbearable ’! ”
“Dad said that. That’s Dad playing.”
“ Disagreeable man ! ”
The man climbed over the fence. A book lay
open on a chair, and a wet cambric ball. “ You’ve
been crying ! —on whose account ? ”
She was charmingly pretty—an embodiment of
Spring. Brown hair with a wave in it, tantalising
dimples, blue eyes. The grace of a daffodil—
Spring !
“I’m forty,’’confessed the man. “I am poor
my name is Brown ! "
The girl laughed.
“ Don’t do that—pity me ! ”
“ Why ?—because pity is akin to ? ”
“Just that.”
The girl blushed, from sweet-pea to peony.
“I would not call you unbearable. You’re just
what I want for my ‘ Spring/
“ Oh, you’re an artist! ”
“ And in love.”
“ Dad’s a music-master— and in love ! ”
“Stepmother?”
The girl nodded.
“ Don’t / compare favorably with a stepmother ?
If ‘ Spring ’ succeeds ? — But —my name’s Brown ! ”
“ I like your looks,” said the girl, impertinently.
“ Well, if I call to-inorrow (with references)
will you engage me?”
“ We might consider an engagement.”
“ Thank you. . . It’s so lovely to be uncon-
ventional—till to-morrow!
THE YOUNG ’UN.
The Vigilance at Coolaba.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Foe several months the crushings at the big mine
outside the township had been exceptionally good
so good that a number of prominent citizens
had sent for their wives, and had determined to
settle down in this desolate corner of the out-back.
Thereupon it was agreed that the moral atmos-
phere of the place would be refined if the “tea-
shop ” of Kumasura was permanently closed ; and
word to that effect was sent to the proprietor.
The stock of his establishment comprised a
dozen empty tea-cases, a few packets of cigarettes,
and six lady assistants. How we younger men,
passing on our way to work, were wont to look
at the tiny round faces peeping over the window
curtains.! The painted cheeks, the gilded lips
parted in wide, innocent smiles and showing
pretty teeth, the gay kimonos . But respect-
able women were coming, and the Japanese must
go. Kamasura was given a week’s notice, and on
the Saturday morning specified for departure very
few turned up to work at half-past seven. In-
stead, we waited in groups along the street.
The Japs, had to walk several furlongs down a
hill which the coach would not climb ; thence they
would embark for Esperance, where they could
get the train and go to the devil. The train left
for him once a day. All trains in Westralia go
there or thereabouts.
The coach was run out from the pub. at the foot
of the hill, the horses were put in, and the blast
of a horn announced that within ten minutes
intending passengers must be aboard.
Five minutes later the procession issued from
the tea-shop. The girls were loaded with mats
and various household gods. Kamasura alone
went unburdened, as, with head lowered and
hantjls in his coat pockets, he led the way down
the hill. The little women seemed scared and
bewildered. Surely they had done no wrong ?
They did not steal ; they did not kill people.
O Dura San, smallest and prettiest, came last,
trotting along on sandalled feet, and keeping
close to the girl before her. Accidentally she trod
on a stone and fell, her bundle rolling yards away.
Then she burst into tears.
Dick, Weston was the first to reach her. He
tossed the bundle to the nearest man* and, picking
the lilliputian up in his arms, bore her to the
coach, holding his head up, looking defiantly
around him.
A wave of sympathy ran through the crowd.
We didn’t carry the o'.her girls, but each had at
least two men to make the wav as easy for her as
possible. Then we saw them off. No word was
spoken, and no sound but the heavy rattling of
the coach accompanied them on the way. Our
eyes followed the lumbering vehicle until, wrapped
in its enveloping cloud of dust, it disappeared
over the shimmering horizon. A bell tolled
angrily up at the mine, calling us to work.
.The cause of righteousness and truth had been
vindicated. The moral atmosphere was cleared.
And we felt we would have been a good deal hap-
pier if we had left the moral atmosphere as it was.

The Melb. monument to late baronet
Clarke has been placed just near to where Flem-
ing’s unemployed hold their daily gatherings.
“ I asked for bread and they gave me a stone.”
“ Raupo ” from Maoriland :
Dear Bulletin.— Re a recent par. The fall of 9,000,000
bushels, since ’9B, in M.L.’s annual wheat crop may have
been caused, to an extent, by the contingenting craze.
The Millers’ Trust may also have had a say in it, but,
without doubt, the primary cause is that the South
Island cookies, who did practically all the cropping, have
found that they can make more money in less time at
dairying. I am too far away anywhere, at present, to
look up statistics, but am confident that you will find the
increase in the annual butter exports since ’9B knocks
spots off that 9,000,000 bushels of wheat. At the same
time, even my unsophisticated brain can grasp the fact
that no country is going to get out of debt by giving
away men and money. M.L. bids fair to become one long
dairying district, with a few jumbucks on the highest
hills. It is already dotted with creameries, a few miles
apart, from end. to end ; and sheep-runs are being con-
stantly cut up into dairy farms.
The Transcontinental land-grant railway
party is so strong in S.A. Parliament that the
thing looks a 3 good as done—but for the trifling
fact that the syndicate hasn’t been found yet.
The average English land co. in Australia makes
large profits, yet it makes just about a quarter as
much out of its land as it might do. As a rule, it
has a pathetic faith in blocking settlement and
sitting down hard on its bare acres as a means of
making money, and so it gets a wildly exaggerated
idea of the amount of land required to make a
good big swindle. Some co. may yet ask for all
the Territory and a bit of S.A. thrown in, also a
present of the existing *iilway, as its price for
finishing the line. All the same, if Premier
Barton had been half as anxious to take over the
Territory, which was his business, as to help
Burns, Philp, and Co.’s New Hebrides fakes,
which weren’t, a great danger would have been
averted.
Alma Mater, the Melbourne University
paper, flogs Prof. Tucker for disparagement of
Australian literature. Alma Mater says :
Among the many fettering recollections our Professors
and our administrators fetch with them from lands of
more cultivated thought, surely the most galling is that
which tethers them and us to the opinion that Australian
literature must be British literature, writ small and writ
over again. . . . The things that made for. British
literature must be allowed free sway here in Australia,
or we will have tenfold worse disasters than any that
wrecked Chatterton, Byron, Keats, Shelley, Kendall,
Gordon, or Clarke. The plain fact is that in Australia
we have conditions answering rather to ancient Greece,
or ancient England, than to more modern times. Yet a
sapient newspaper article, of local manufacture, recently
declared that until Australian writers produced stuff
equal or alike to that of Thackeray, Carlyle, and such
complex products of an older nation, we had better not
look for a local literature ! We have flocks and herds,
droughts, floods, fire, old convict days, horses and horse-
men, Burke and Wills, savages, wild bush lands and
barren steppes, impassible mountain chains, coral reefs,
great sea captains and bold young navigators like Cook,
Flinders, Dampier, Bass, founders of cities, finders of
gold. Where in all the literature of Britain shall we find
these things to imitate? When the mother-land was
nearest to those conditions her singers were nearest to
our own hearts, but they sang in a tongue uncouth, that
we do not care to imitate. It suits our ridiculous critics,
and worse than ridiculous poetasters, to observe that
“ We have no past, our past is all before,” but I doubt
whether even the poetaster who said so would care to
live up to his words. The one real and all-inspiring fact
in Australian story is so absolutely the direct contrary of
that silly antithesis, that there is no other country in the
whole world with so intimate and large a past as ours. It
is yonder at our window waiting, Valentine-like, to be
admitted into our hearts, a great, wide-eyed, panting,
beautiful crowd of sinewy men and heroic women and
gigantic impulses eager to be written into everlasting
remembrance. . . . Yet local critics stand aloof in
giant ignorance or gaze askance with inuendo and
superior quip whenever some new planet of the Austral
firmament swims into their ken. ... We have founded
our Universities to teach us Creation, not Imitation;
Worship, not Slavery; Observation and Experiment,
not the reading of text-books.
